THE RED PAGE.
Kipling’s new book of ballads does not heighten
his reputation, bub confirms it. We know now
just what to expect from this bright writer—he
has reached the limits of his powers in prose and
verse, and, could he do more, would be no longer
Kipling. London Times, with not unusual
infelicity, declared that unmistakeable genius
rang in every line of the Barrack-Room Ballads.
There is not a trace of genius in Kipling:
his supremacy is clearly that of talent.
He works on ordinary lines ; and by dint of his
instinct for a good thing, and his knack of saying
it, and his industry in getting their utmost pos-
sible value out of knack and instinct, he has
managed to go just a little farther on those
ordinary lines than the previously best workers of
his kind. Genius generally develops on ex-
traordinary lines : its outlook differs from
that of talent not so much in extent as in di-
rection. Of the same ballads the Pall Mall
Gazette asked: “ If this be not poetry, what
is?” shirking dictum in interrogative. But
Kipling’s verse is not poetry in any exalted
sense beoause it is almost entirely lacking in
imagination. He never gives to airy nothing a
local habitation and a name. What he does is to
find _ forcible expression and comfortable verbal
lodging for solid something. He is not creator,
hut interpreter.
* . ■ • •„ *
The splendid lines, “ M'Andrews’ Hymn,”
for example, in the new volume —on the whole
the <best thing Kipling has done in verse—are
decidedly not poetry. And why not ? Because
there is no intoxication about them, no frenzy:
the pythoness is always watching the audience
out of the corner of her eye : is always master
of her oracle. Nobody can be a poet till the
oracle masters aim. And Kipling’s verses,
too, want ideality : they have no direct refer-
ence to abstract questions, universal emotions:
their soope is narrow, their standpoint apart. It
is doubtless this very isolation which gives them
truth and intensity : they are wonderfully real
and living; but their life, their reality remain
always things outside the reader. So throughout
Kipling. You feel that he is always writing
about other people—never about yon. You can ad-
mire him, be interested in them, yet he and they
are aliens all the time. It is not so with
a poet. The magic of poetry is potent in
proportion as the reader can identify himself with
the thoughts and emotions expressed. It is the
broad human appeal that glorifies it. Kipling,
like other realists, can only get narrow individual
appeal. And, though this is often marvellously
strong, it is never of the strongest. To any fel-
low, the other fellow can never be quite as impor-
tant as this fellow.
« • * *
The new ballads seem mostly reprinted, and are
padded with a good deal of dull stuff. The long
“Song of the English,” whioh begins the volume, is
heavy—a failure. But there are quite enough gems
to make the book’s fortune, and it is permissible
to quote one or two which bring to mind the state-
ment of Kipling’s old Indian editor that he wrote
his rhymes to airs humming in his head. Isn’t
there a fine swing in this ?—
THE LAST CHANTEY.
' And there was no more aea.’
Thus said The Lord in the Vault above the Cherubim,
Calling to the Angels and the Souls in their degree:
. ' Lo! Earth has passed away
On the smoke of Judgment Day.
That Our word may be established shall we gather up
the sea 1 ‘
Loud sang the souls of the jolly, jolly mariners:
* Plague upon the hurrioane that made us furl and flee!
But the war is done between us,
In the deep the Lord hath seen us—
Our bones we’ll leave the barracout’, and God may sink
the sea 1 *
Then said the soul of Judas that betrayed him:
' Lord, hast Thou forgotten Thy oovenant with met
Haw once a year I go
To cool me on the floe T
And Ye take my day of mercy if Ye take away the seal *
Then said the soul of the Angel of the Off-shore Wind
(He that bits the thunder when the bull-mouthed
breakers flee):
‘ I have watch and ward to keep
O’er Thy wonders on the deep,
And Ye take mine honor from me if Ye take away the
sea ! ’
Loud sang the souls of the jolly, jolly mariners :
‘ Nay, but we were angry, and a hasty folk are wet
If we worked the ship together
Till she foundered in foul weather,
Are we babes that we should clamor for a vengeance on
the sea ? ’
Theu said the souls of the slaves that men threw over-
board :
' Kennelled in the picaroon a weary band were we;
But Thy arm was strong to save ;
And it touched us on the wave,
And we drowsed the long tides idle till Thy Trumpets
tore the sea.’
Then cried the soul of the stout Apostle Paul to God:
‘Once we frapped a ship, and she laboured wouudily.
There were fourteen score of these,
And they blessed Thee on their knees,
When they learned Thy Grace and Glory under Malta by
the sea I ’
Loud sane the souls of the jolly, jolly mariners,
Plucking at their harps, and they plucked unhandily:
‘Our thumbs are rough and tarred,
And the tune is something hard— -
May wc lift a Deepsea Chantey such as seamen use at
sea?’
Then said the souls of the gentlemen-adventurers—
Fettered wrist to bar all for red iniquity :
‘ Ho, we revel in our chains
O’er the sorrow that was Spain’*;
Heave or sink it, leave or drink it, we were masters of
the sea! ’
Up spake the soul of a gray Gothavn ’speckshioner
(He that led the flinching in the fleets of fair Dundee):
‘ Oh, the ice-blink white and near,
And the bowhead breaching clear !
Will Ye whelm them all for wantonness that wallow in
the sea?’
Loud sang the souls of the jolly, jolly mariners,
Crying : ‘ Under Heaven, here is neither lead nor lea I
Must we sing for evermore
On the windless, glassy floor?
Take baok your golden fiddles and we'll beat to open
sea 1 ’
Then stooped the Lord, and He oalled the good sea np to
Him,
And ’stablished his borders unto all eternity,
That'„uch as have no pleasure
For to praise the Lord by measure,
They may enter into galleons and serve Him on the sea.
Sun, wind, and cloud shall not fail from the face of it,
Stinging, ringing spindrift, nor the fulmar flying
free ;
And the ships shall go abroad
To the Qlory of the Lord,
Who heard the silly sailor folk and gave thorn hack
their sea 1

In the first version of this lines 3 and 4 of verse
10 ran—
‘Oh, the ringer and right whale,
And the fish were struck for sale.’
Kipling has got a trifle more technical slang in,
after his effective habit, to increase the reality of
the picture; and perhaps also he thought the
“sale” idea objectionable. It does not seem
necessary to know exactly what a ’speckshioner
is, if you classify him dimly as one of the para-
sites of the whale.

And here is another haunting rhythm: his
music nearly doubles the value of muoh of Kip-
ling’s verse—
‘ FOLLOW ME ’OME.’
There was no one like ’im, ’Orse or Foot,
Nor any o’ the Guns I knew ;
An’ because it was so, why, o’ course ’e went an’ died,
Which is just what the best men do.
So it's knock out your pipes an’ follow me !
An' it's finish up your swipes an' follow me !
Oh, ‘ark to the big drum callin’,
Follow me—follow me ’ome l
ffl mare she neighs the ’ole day long,
She paws the 'ole night through,
An’ she won't take ’or feed ’cause o’ waitin’ for ’is step,
Which is just what a beast would do.
'ls girl she goes with a bombardier
Before 'er month is through ;
An’ the. banns are up in ohurch, for she’s got the beggar
hooked,
Whioh is Just what a girl would do.
We fought ’bout a dog—last week it were—
No more than a round or two ;
But I strook ’im cruel 'ard, an’ I wish I ’adn’t new,
Which is just what a man can’t do.
‘E was all that I ’ad in the way of a friend,
An’ I’ve ’ad to find one new ;
But I’d give my pay an’ stripe to get the beggar book,
Which it’s just too late to do.
So it’s knock out your pipes an’ follow me !
An' it's finish off your swipes an’ follow me l
Oh, 'ark to the fifes a-crawlin’ l
Follow me—follow me 'ome 1
Take ’im away ! 'E's gone where the best men go.
Take 'im away I An' the gun-wheels turnin' slow.
Take ’im away ! There’s more from the place ‘e come.
Take ’im away, with the limber an' the drum.
For it's ‘ Three rounds blank ’ an’follow me,
An, it's ‘ Thirteen rank ’ an’ follow me;
Oh, passin’ the love o’ women,
Follow me—follow me ’ome I
There are several other pieces as good or all but.
The book is sprinkled with vigorous sketches
by an aboriginal artist—hailing apparently from
N.S. Wales, but his name and lineage are not
given. One would like to learn them, for his
work is most interesting. Andrew Lang intro-
duces the stories with a short note which success-
fully disguises a good deal of ignorance of their
authors, though scraps of Marcus Clarke’s Gor-
don-prefaoe reappear effectively. And, for
tasting, the Noongahburrahs and Mrs. Parker
and the anonymous artist are herewith palatably
represented—
' In the morning, Bibbee would come over to her oamp
and ask her what was the matter that she had called
out so in the night. She told him that she thought she
heard someone walking about and was afraid, for she was
alone with her four little girls.
He told her she ought not to be afraid with all her
ohildren round her. But night after night she sat up
crying: “ Wyah, wyah, Deereeree, Deereeree."
At last Bibbee said : “If you are so frightened, marry
me and live in my oamp. I will take care of you.” But
Deereeree said she did not want to marry. So night after
night was to be heard her plaintive cry of “ Wyah, wyah,
Deereeree, Deereeree." And again and again Bibbee
pressed her to share his oamp and marry him. But she
always refused. The more she refused the more he
wished to marry her. And he used to wonder how he
could induce her to ohange her mind.
At last he thought of a plan of surprising her into giv-
ing her consent. He set to work and made a beautiful
and many-colored arch, which, when it was made, he
called Euloowirree, and he placed it right across the sky,
reaching from one side of the earth to the other. When
the rainbow was firmly placed in the sky and showing out
in all its brilliancy of many colors, as a roadway from the
earth to the stars, Bibbee went into his camp to wait.
When Deereeree looked up at the sky and saw the won-
derful rainbow', she thought something dreadful must be
going to happen. She was terribly frightened, and called
aloud: “Wyah, wyah.” In her fear she gathered her
ohildren together, and fled with them to Bibbee’s camp
for protection.
Bibbee proudly told her that he had made the rainbow,
Just to show how strong he was and how safe she would
be if she married him. But if she would not, ahe would
see what terrible things he would make to come on the
earth, not just a harmless and beautiful roadway across
the heavens, but things that would burst from the earth'
and destroy it.
So, by working on her mixed feelings of fear of his
prowess and admiration of his skill, Bibbee gained his
desire and Deereeree married him. And when long after-
wards they died, Deereeree was changed into the little
willy-wagtail who may be heard through the stillness of
the summer nights, crying her plaintive wail of “ Deeree-
ree, wyah, wyah, Deereeree."
And Bibbee was changed into the « oodpecker, or elimb-
ing tree bird, who is always running up trees as if he
wanted to be building other ways to the sky than the
famous roadway of lii» Kuloowirree, the building of which
had wo him hi 3 wife.
♦ 7~
Hubert Crackanthorpe, the young English
writer whose body was the other day found in
the Seine—murder or suicide?—was the clever
son of a clever mother. He wrote prose stories
and sketches, half-realist, half-symbolist, which
were often very striking. Decidedly he promised
well, but his nerves vibrated too keenly for health :
the modern melancholy suffuses all his work.
Ho was very sensitive to sex : the relations of
men and women are the theme of nearly all his
magazine contributions. “Wreckage” and
“Sentimental Studies ” —two volumes of collected
fragments—sold in small editions : there was an
earlier volume, “A Set of Village Tales' 1 ; and
his “Vignettes,” a few small leaves of impres-
sions, reached Australia some fortnighb ago.
These are of them—
PARIS IN OCTOBER.
Paris in October—all white and a-glitter tinder a cold,
sparkling sky, and the trees of the houlevards trembling
their frail, russet leaves ; garish, petulant Paris ; com-
placently content with her sauntering crowds, her mono-
tonous arrangements in pink and white and blue ; ever
busied with her own publicity, her tiresome, obvious
vice, and her paroohial modernity coquetting with cos-
mopolitanism . . .
IN THE BAY OP SALERNO.
To gaze across the block sweep of sea, out into the
mystery of the night; to hear the restless waves slowly
sighing through the darkness, as they beat ths rocks a
thousand feet beneath ; to love a little so, with quiet
pressure of hands; and listlessly to ponder on strange
meanings of life and love and death.
And so, amid a still serenity of dreamy sadness, to
forget the tnad turmoil of passion, to grow indifferent to
all desire, and to wait, while the heart fills full of grave
gratitude towards an unknown God.
And then, once more, to understand how life is but a
little thing, and love but ft passionate illusion, and to
envy the sea her sighing in the day when the c r d shall
have come.
* * * •
Despondent enough—likely it was suicide. One
acknowledges a real grief for Craokanthorpe—not
that he is worse off, or we better—but he was an
intellectual comrade, and these are rare. Grief is
one of the joys of egoistn, apparently.
Some items —
" Mr. Coulson Kernahan is one of those Irishmen of
genius whose imagination and powers of expression are
out of all proportion to their logical and intellectual
faculties.”— Freethinker, . . Armand Jerome, whilom
editor of the dead-in-life Cosmos, pleaded in mitigation
of sentence for embezzlement that “he wanted to do
something in art and literature that would put him above
the ordinary level of his fellow-men.” That was what
he wanted to do. What he did do was to pay Faveno
and others a few shillings for articles which he published
over his own signature. He even had the impu-
dence to ask Louise Mack to write a story to
be issued as written by Armand Jerome. . . .
Olive Schreiner, who does her own cooking, has had a
new book in the kitchen-table drawer for several years
past, so they say, and ponders corrections while waiting
for the joint to “do on top”. Satisfied at last that she
cannot better.it, she will bring it to England for publica-
tion in ’97— so they say. . . . “ The public oriticism of
fiction, as distinguished from the reviewing of books for
purposes of advertisement, should be primarily the court
of appeal from the popular judgment. Therein the con-
scientious writer should find the consolation for his hard-
won failure, and the successful impostor the end of hie
complacency.”— Saturday Review. ... He Louie
Sixteenth’s brutality, “L.M.F.” quotes for Lond. Spec-
tator a paragraph in the diary of Gouverneur Morris,
American Ambassador during the Reign of Terror in Paris
—“M. de Trudaine mentioned as having heard from
young Montmorin that the King is by nature cruel and
base. An instance of his cruelty, among others, is that
he used to spit and roast live cats. In riding with
Madame Flahaut, I tell her that I could not believe such
things. She tells me that when young he was guilty of
such things; that he is very brutal and nasty, whioh she
attributes chiefly to a bad education. His brutality
once led him so far, while Dauphin, as to beat his wife,
for which he was exiled four days by his grandfather,
Louis XV.”
Mrs. K. Langloh Parker’s “ Australian Legen-
dary Tales ” have ethnologioally little signifi-
cance, and seem to have been invented at a com-
paratively recent date. The Noongahburrahs are
evidently as happy in thoughtlessness as all their
kindred, and do not, so far as Mrs. Parker shows,
pose themselves with the deeper problems of exist-
ence. Life and death and the hereafter they take
for granted: their tales are imaginative attempts to
account for the obvious natural phenomena thrust
daily under their noses—the characteristics of
beasts, the cries of birds, the origin of fire. The
intrinsic interest is sight; though three or four of
the stories—“The Bunbundoolooeys,” “Weedah
the Mocking Bird,” “ Meamei the Seven Sisters,”
‘ Deegeenboyah the Soldier Bird’’—have suf-
ficiently dramatio action to hold a reader’s atten-
tion. The undoubted value of the whole collection
is chiefly that of a literary curiosity—the pratt-
lings of our Australia’s children, which even in
their worthlessness must have charm for a parent.
DEEREEREE THE WAGTAIL, AND THE RAINBOW.
Deereeree was a widow, and lived in a oamp alone with
her four little girls. One day Bibbee came and made a
camp not far from hers. Deereeree was frightened of
him, too frightened to go to sleep. All night she used to
watch his camp, and if she heard a sound she would cry
aloud: “Deereeree, wyah, wyah, Deereeree.” Some-
times she would be calling out nearly all night.

the XMAS CAROL?
What was OLD SCROOGE like before he saw
MARLEY’S GHOST ? A miserable wretch,
begrudging a starving cur a lick out of the gutter,
the children their Xmas feast, and all mankind its
Xmas enjoyment.
What would you be like if you didn’t see
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
XMAS SHOW.
Not so bad as OLD SCROOGE, surely, for he
was a bad case indeed. But you would not, nay,
could not, be happy. Your heart would yearn
for something, and you wouldn’t know what. You
would be low-spirited, snappish, and bilious. There
would be no brightness in your eye, no elasticity
in your step, your weary feet would be heavy as
lead, your flesh would fall away, and instead of
the sleek, WELL-GROOMED CITIZEN, a lean,
woebegone, CADAVEROUS PANTALOON
would OCCUPY YOUR PANTS and wear your
skin. Friend, don’t risk it, make a point of seeing

The Laws of the Sevang.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The East had tugged my heart-strings, and I
stepped aboard the mail,
wk iere the etiquette is like the ship—of steel,
Where there’s laws and regulations quite enough
to run a gaol,
And the nine Commandments pasted on the
wheel.
The master of the naval skip—his cap is bound in
brass—
The airs he gives himself give us a pang,
And his whole existence says to you: “ Young
man, keep off the grass !
And abide by all the laws of the Serang ! ”
That is to say :
“ If you travel in the first saloon,
Sahib ! you mustn’t spit in the spittoon;
Smoking ain’t allowed where there is a crowd.
Whether in the Bight or off Rangoon,
Whisper not the cursed words, ‘Damn ’ or ‘Dang,’
On our line ’twixt Plymouth and Penang
We’re the P. &O. ! Salaam, Sahib. Lo !
Listen to the laws of the Serang ! ”
So we let old Breaksea Island and the Sound go
down astern,
And we formed a little party and a school,
And, next day being Sunday, we all sat down to
learn
How to ring a grey with fivers in the pool.
And a blasted greasy nigger “Salaam Sahibed ”
us all to cease
When the game was just becoming warm and
strong,
Though we “ hooshtahed ” him he entered caveats
against our lease,
And jumped it with the law of the Ser-ong.
Which also remarks :
“ Sahib, you musn’t shake the nimble dice—
Musn’t take the risk of nary pice—
Throw the bones away—’tis the Sabbath day
(The Cabin-Service ladies all are nice) —
Dinner dress includes a mile of shirt—
Sahib ! secure a drawl and take a flirt
Leeways on the longboat—but this ain’t no wicked
songboat—
Assume the British air or we’ll feel hurt.”
They’ve a menu of French grammar, and some
tough old ewe, to eat,-
And the British girl reduced to skin and bone
Wears an opera-dress and clothes not much above
her blessed feet,
And glories in her nudity alone ;
And next her is the English dude whose waist is
twenty-one,
And whose brains could make a passage through
a ring,
And the fleshless nudity and he damn all beneath
the sun,
’Cept the Empire which is run by the Ser -ing.
Who furthermore states :
“ ‘ Pied a mouton —sauce a la piquant,’
Blast your lack of French and the hongtong,
Call it ‘ mutton trotter ” and our British brain will
totter —
We’re all British—are we Lascars and Ser -O'ag 1
Though the space of three-feet-six by four-feet-
four
And some stinks will serve to lodge of us a score,
We’re all British, and we’re royal,
In our rupee way we’re loyal
To Queen Vicky from the Largs to Mirzapore.
There’s not a bite of fresh meat to be had for love
or money—
Only ’Murkan beef grown tasteless in the ice—
And they give you a French menu, and they
think it very funny
If you don’t say “ Tray hiong. By God, it’s
nice ! ”
They give you a French menu, and a bony British
lady
Dressed like a living picture to the waist,
And a dude who’s exiled from St. James’s for
doing something shady,
And who damns what he’s not seen with British
taste.
The bar’s the only winner—there’s a loss on the
French dinner—
But they’re English, thank you, though it gives
a pang !
And all manly thoughts they muffle under cere-
monial snuffle,
And they’re guided by the laws of the Serang.
Who says conclusively :
Sahib ! I’m Moosa Khan !
Sahib ! I’m British man !
Fork me out a dollar for I filled your water-can,
Whisper not the curse words “damn” or “ dang,”
Tip and tip ’twixt Plymouth and Penang ;
Wear your mile of shirt,
Have your longboat flirt :
These are all the laws of the Serang !
In the Bight.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Dear Bulletin, —I am interested in the
subject of cannibalism. My memory retains an
inkling of having read somewhere that cannibalism
survived in Scotland till the days of good Queen
Bess, about whose time a peeress (Loudon, I
fancy) was tried for child-eating. Can anyone
named M‘Pherson give me a finger-post to reach
the source of that inkling?—Yours, &c., X.Z.

you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
iveek for a year. , .

to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other A rticles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion — i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C. ; Gordon
and Gotch, Ludgate Circus ; Street & Co., Cornhill.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 9, 1897.
A Year’s Record.
New South Wales, the province where the
fiscal issue rages without ceasing and the
tariff disagreement is never wholly at rest,
has reached the end of the first year of the
new Foreign-trade epoch, and a great
attempt is being made by the party which is
responsible for the consequences to favorably
size up results. So far the most conspicuous
fact is that there are hardly any results
worth mentioning, and very few that are not
best buried in oblivion. The absolute
Foreign-trade policy didn’t infest New South
Wales for the whole of 1896, but the great
bulk of the protective duties were removed
on the first day of the year, and some more
at the commencement of July ; and the very
few remaining duties which it is proposed to
sacrifice are departing by instalments ; so
1896 may fairly be called a year of Foreign-
trade. And on the whole it has been a year
of lost hopes and of aspirations that didn’t
come off; and “the Depression” which was
to have lifted has rather settled down with
renewed intensity. The same historical de-
pression is heard of more or less from all
parts of the world ; it has apparently become
one of the permanent concomitants of civili-
sation, and its causes seem to be too many
for present enumeration. But in New South
Wales, through much worrying of the weary
fiscal issue, it has grown to be almost a fixed
belief with one large section of the popula-
tion that Foreign-trade is in itself an abso-
lute cufe for the whole trouble, while
another large section believes that every-
thing can be put right by Protection. The
province had some 50 years of Foreign-
trade, and during that time the depression
came periodically just as usual. Neverthe-
less during the semi-protective Dibbs regime
the party prophesied the great things which
its nostrum would bring to pass, if it
only had a trial, just as if it hadn’t
been already tried more or less for half a
century with no results worth mentioning.
And now another year’s trial has also pro-
duced no results worth mentioning, never-
theless the same old prophecy goes on as
vigorously as ever.
* * * *
The one experiment which New South
Wales made in the way of Protection also
produced no very large or beautiful conse-
quences. Nevertheless, as Foreign-trade
had more than fifty years’ run and failed de-
finitely, whereas Protection had only a hal -
hearted trial for three years and hadn t time
to fail definitely, the conclusion is that
Foreign-trade is much the worse failure of
the two. The evidence is that half-a-century
or more of Foreign-trade resulted in nothing
but depression and disappointment ; while
there is no evidence that half-a-century of
Protection would have done anything of the
kind, because the experiment hasn’t yet been
tried. This time Foreign-trade came in with
a louder, wilder, madder whoop of exultation
than ever. Premier Reid asserted that his
fiscal creed would abolish the unemployed in
24 hours, or some other very brief period.
It was announced on all hands that the
country was “ saved ” —it had been “ saved
so often before that the statement seemed
rather less reliable than it might be, but
anyhow it was saved once more. And at the
present moment the country looks exactly
the same as it did in the days when it was
lost. Considering all the hopes that were
raised and the promises that were made, this
is a saddening result.
» • » *
There are as yet very few data to go upon
as to the condition of things in 1896. It is
known from the reports of Sydney Benevo-
lent Asylum and other charities that the
general destitution has been worse than in any
previous year within their experience. This
is despite the fact that an enormous amount
has been done to smother the distress and
put it out of sight by shunting the destitute
on to relief works and by sending them out
scrub-cutting, forest-thinning, and bush-
felling in the Far West, where the difficulty
of getting back has helped materially to keep
them out of evidence. Most, if not all, of
these works have been paid for with borrowed
money, so that the bill for quite a lot of pre-
sent day pauperism has been handed down
to posterity. The deposits made in the P.O.
Savings Bank were less in ’96 than in any
year since ’93, and the withdrawals were the
largest on record save in ’93 only ; also the
net increase in deposits was the smallest
since 1890. The increase in deposits in the
Commissioners’ Savings Bank has been
smaller than in any one of the previous
eight years, the panic year of ’93 alone ex-
cepted, and the increase in the two institu-
tions is only half what it was in ’94 and ’95.
As the Savings Banks are essentially the
banks of the people, these figures go to prove
that the condition of the masses was worse
in ’96—the first year of the new Freetrade
era—than in either of the two previous years.
The N.S.W. deposits in banks of issue
declined £850,000 against last year’s de-
cline of £163,000; and this doesn’t seem to
show that any special outbreak of prosperity
has struck the propertied classes. This
decline is especially noticeable as the total
deposits for the seven provinces show a large
increase.
* * * *
The State revenue in New South Wales
has been going through a bad experience.
Last financial year closed on 30th J une with
a deficit of £635,000, which fact, revealed in
the Government statistics, made a depressing
commentary on the alleged large surplus.
Since then the result of six months’ work
is reported to be that expenditure is
another £336,000 ahead of revenue. The
imports of merchandise, which represent a
liability that the province has to liquidate,
increased by nearly £3,000,000, while the
exports which pay for them only increased
by about £200,000, therefore the province
is evidently accumulating new liabilities at
a rapid rate. The collapse in property values
has still gone on ; sales by auction of lands
and bricks and mortar are set down at
£450,000 for the year against £560,000 for
’95 ; £700,000 for ’94; £950,000 for ’93,
and 1£ millions for '92. The story every-
where is that property is almost as unsale-
able as any other white elephant ; and the
municipal valuations show that the decline
in values still continues. Nothing has been
heard of any improvement in manufacturing
industries, though it will be a year before
any complete returns under this head are
available ; nor has there been any visible
advance in wages. Except that there is an
extension of the area under wheat there is
no visible increase of prosperity anywhere ;
in every other respect the tendency seems to
be still backwards. The above figures are
taken almost wholly from the reports pub-
lished by the two Sydney freetrade dailies
on Ist January, therefore they constitute
the Freetrade party’s own account of its
own boom. It isn’t a very complete account,
certainly, but, so far as it goes, it deserves
to rank with the saddest boom narratives ever
put into print.
• * *
. T™ Bulletin doesn’t profess to assert
that .Protection alone would have made any-
thing very large and gorgeous out of the
unpromising materials which exist in this
mortgaged and BuLL-ridden province. There
never was a nostrum invented which would
cure everything. But what it does assert is
that the great Foreign-trade party put forth
its nostrum as a remedy which, taken alone
would, by its own blessed virtues, lift New
feouth Wales out of the slough of depression,
and it hasn t lifted worth a cent. The pro
vince has now enjoyed 12 months of the
most advanced Freetrade it has yet experi-
enced, and the result, as shown by the facts
announced in its own press organs, is that
1896 has been one of the worst years in the
record of the last decade or more, it the
'present situation of things is caused by
Freetrade then it shows that Freetrade is a
delusion and an aggravated disaster. It the
absence of prosperitv under Freetrade isn t
caused by Freetrade but by some extraneous
influences, then the common assertion that
the want of prosperity in V ictoria is neces-
sarily caused by Protection is an imbecile
mode of argument. Assuming that depres-
sion in Melbourne proves the fiscal policy of
that city to be utterly wrong, then the de-
pression of Sydney proves exactly the same
thing about its contrary fiscal policy. Mean-
while, the good time that is always just on
the verge of arriving is not visibly nearer,
and “the Depression” promises to be the
same reliable subject of discussion in 9i as
it has been for the last seven years or so.
* * * *
The one decent apology that can be made
for the failure is that the materials available
were too bad to make any sudden improve-
ment possible. This isn’t much of an apology,
seeing that the party now in power swept the
polls in 1894 on its blatant assurance that all
the troubles of the province were owing to
the black iniquity of Protection; still, if the
Foreign-trade crowd is willing to admit that
it was a liar in ’94, and a very bad Ananias
in ’95, it can construct some kind of an
apology for its failure. Australia has never
yet found its proper level; it is a country
with a short history, and so many dizzy
booms have been crowded into its record that
it hasn’t yet had an opportunity to ascertain
its real value. It began with a mild kind of
land-boom ; there was a whole continent to
be taken for nothing, and everybody could
be a bloated landholder who wanted to. It
had another good time when the pastoral
industry commenced, and it found that
immense fortunes were to be made by grow-
ing wool on land that cost practically nothing,
and sending it away to supply the perpetual
demands of Europe. The gold-boom fol-
lowed ; almost the largest and most glittering
boom in history. Then came the borrowing
boom, when Australia piled up in a handful
of years the biggest debt on earth in propor-
tion to population. There was a coal-boom,
in a small way, in the days when this happy
land had almost a monopoly of the markets
of the Pacific ; a silver-boom, when the
richest deposits on earth were discovered at
the Barrier ; sundry land - booms, when
almost everybody grew rich by swapping his
1.0. U. for the 1.0. U. of the next man; and
a glorious banking-boom, during which this
became the most overbanked country on this
earth. Then, last of all, came the Westralian
company-promoting boom—which wasn’t ex-
actly a gold-boom, for want of the gold, but
which brought a good deal of cash and paper
into Australia and took away no visible
equivalent.
* * * *
No other country on earth is ever known
to have compressed so much boom into so
short a time or into so small a population.
And now that some of the booms are gone
for ever, and the rest are gone, probably for
ever, but certainly for a time, and the
country has almost its first chance to sober
down in a whole century, New South Wales
is gravely informed by the more ardent
devotees of its two political parties that a
tariff adjustment is all that is wanted to
bring the good old days back once more.
The Foreign-trade party having the largest
and most loud-mouthed section of the press
on its side has had the best chance to make
a particular fool of itself in this direction.
Wherefore Premier Reid climbed into power
in ’94 with assurances that poverty, depres-
sion, want of employment, and ail other
distresses would cease almost immediately,
because he was going to turn his little penny
squirt on the ruins, and at the beginning of
’96 he got it fairly turned on, with the result
that the ruins, so far, haven’t budged an inch.
A Criminal Court of Appeal.
One of the most glaring defects of Australian
Criminal Law is the want of machinery for
attaining anything like equality in the sen-
tences imposed for similar offences. A glance
at the daily reports of the criminal courts will
reveal a great disparity between the sentences
imposed for the same or similar offences by
different Judges. No doubt the disposition
options of a Judge will, to an extent,
artect his sentences; though it is noticeable that
they do not seem to affect all the punishments
inflicted by him, but only those imposed for
certain offences. One Judge views with
especial horror offences against the person, and
deals leniently with those against property.
Another seems to imagine that society will go
to the dogs unless offences against property are
severely punished, while personal injuries are
of small import in his eyes. One Judge
exhibits a weakness for embezzlers ; another
treats them with merciless severity. Now
the iaw leaves a large discretion ’ in the
hands of the Judges—it fixes only a maximum
and minimum penalty-but each Judge exer-
cises his discretion according to principles of
his own, or according as his fancy or prejudice
directs. There is plainly no attempt whatever,
on the part of the administrators of the law, to
introduce any degree of uniformity into the
punishments for criminal offences. One would
almost imagine that the process of arriving at
the number M of imprisonment for a
me was— Heads, five years ; tails, two.”
Another defect is the want of a’ tribunal
fact Cl as Can n nterta f ln i appeals on questions of
tact, as well as of law. In N.S. Wales the
only method ot reviewing a j£y'. vercUct
where no .natter of law is involved, i, provided
by the Criminal Law Amendment Act, which
allows the Governor, in the event of any question
arising as to the prisoner’s guilt, to order an
enquiry before a Justice. There is, how-ever, no
tribunal to which a convicted person can ap-
peal as of right. The appeal under the Criminal
Law Act is seldom resorted to, because it is an
uncertain one, and because there is always an
appeal to the mercy of the Crown ; and the
latter is just as valuable and just as objection-
able a procedure as the former. The preroga-
tive of mercy is exercised by the Governor, in
capital cases, on the advice of the Executive
Council; in other cases, on the advice of the
Minister for Justice. So practically the prero-
gative is placed in the hands of one Minister,
or of the Executive. This is indeed a most
inconvenient device for reviewing criminal sen-
tences. The administration of justice should
be, as far as possible, removed from political
influences, yet here you have the prerogative of
mercy exercised by accidental persons whose
qualification for doing so is probably a political
qualification and nothing more. And how do
these persons exercise it ? The now notorious
Suffield case shows, in all its hideousness, the
iniquity of placing such a power in the hands
of professional politicians, and, it may be, of
political adventurers. Does any man in the
community think that, had Suffield been
an “ influential ” citizen, or one connected by
ties of blood or friendship with any person of
importance in the world of politics, his case
would have been so disgracefully shelved ? If
such a question as this can be asked, it must be
placed to the credit of the system which renders
it possible. The administration of justice should
be above suspicion, and, while the present sys-
tem is persisted in, it never can. It is impos-
sible for a tribunal sitting in secret, receiving
evidence admissible in no court of law, and
coming to conclusions for which it gives no
reasons, to command either the respect or the
confidence of the people.
* * * *
The remedy is not far to seek. The time has
come for the establishment of a Criminal Court
of Appeal, a tribunal which can review ques-
tions both of law and fact, and which will also
have power to revise sentences passed on
prisoners ; to this Court every prisoner should
have an appeal as of right. It is tolerably cer-
tain that the process of such a Court would
not be abused ; the power of increasing, as well
as of reducing sentences, would always be a
stumbling-block in the way of frivolous appeals.
Every sentence passed by any Court might
come before this body for revision. It is per-
haps impossible to impose on such a Court
rules regarding the exact amount of punishment
for each offence. An eminent English criminal
Judge once said : “A Court of Criminal Appeal
could only correct improper sentences when
passed, whereas the great object to be attained
was to prevent, as far as possible, such unjust
sentences as are constantly brought to our
notice being passed at all.” This is, no doubt,
correct; but the experience to be derived from
the accumulation of cases decided on systematic
principles by a Court of Appeal, would tend
largely to prevent the infliction of punishments
which shock the feelings of the community, and
which are too often a travesty on Justice.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
"Something” Should Be Done.
A correspondent of Melbourne Champion
writes :
I don’t want to insist upon details, but my proposition
roughly is that at this coining' general election the return
shall be secured of a number of candidates definitely
pledged to force the next Ministry to introduce a land-
values tax and apply its proceeds to decrease burdens
which do not affect the fiscal issue—say, to halving rail-
way rates or reducing duties on articles of general con-
sumption that cannot be produced locally. By “forcing
the Ministry ” I mean turning it out of office at once on
its refusal to do this, and then its successors after it, if
they too refuse. When the proposal is sent to the
Council and rejected, the Ministry must in the same way
be forced to appeal at once to the country on the matter.
They would certainly receive overwhelming support and
a direct mandate to go for the Council “ without the
gloves.” All this can be done if the requisite vigor and
courage are available.
The little Champion, which is a paper of
amazing vigor for its size, announces that it is
thinking of following this good advice, and
starting a crusade with the object of forcing
the Ministry to go bald-headed for the House
of 48 Rich Landlords. The trouble is that this
correspondent, like almost every similar person
down South, “doesn’t want to insist on
details.” Every Vic. Democrat knows that the
Ministry or the Assembly or somebody should
go bald-headed for the Leg. Council and wipe
that horror out of existence, and till that is
done every reform will either be cast out igno-
miniously, or else “amended ” to be as little re-
form as possible. Therefore the Council wants to
be “gone for,” and because in England and
most of the Australias the Upper House is a
nominee body which can be swamped by
new appointments if the Lower House
is sufficiently in earnest to do it, the
stereotyped Victorian, when he wakes up,
always wants to know why his Government
doesn’t do something. The Vic. Council is
almost wholly out of the Government’s reach;
there is absolutely no way provided by which
the Government can squeeze that recalcitrant
assemblage ; but it can be done in Hingland
under totally different conditions, and that is
enough. The Champion’s correspondent, like
every other one, “doesn’t want to insist on
details ” ; which practically means that he
doesn t know how to attack the Council, but
he proposes to insist on the Ministry' knowing
what he doesn’t know himself, and on throwing
it out if it doesn’t know what he is unable to
e I 1 1. It is a pity some individual of wealth,
like David Syme, doesn’t offer a big prize for
e best detailed plan of how to surely and
eltectively squash Victoria’s great obstruction.
Consider the circumstances of the case : There
are, it is said, only about 150 Victorians who
are eligible, by sufficient wealth and other
nngs, for seats in the Council, and who have
the leisure to go there. The great majority of
em aie lories ; the mere possession of suffi-
cient wealth is a guarantee of that. The
coun ry, to all intents and purposes must elect
ones, or mostly Tories, for want of a suffi-
ciency of other candidates—at least it appears


so up to date. Having been elected, they go
on “something awful.” The Ministry can’t
make them do otherwise ; it has about as much
direct control over them as over the white bear
in Greenland. The Assembly can’t ; it has just
as much control as the Ministry. To dissolve
the Assembly and get a mandate from the
people is wholly irrelevant ; the Council gets its
own mandate, which, for the above reasons,
is generally a Tory one, and it has nothing to
do with the Assembly’s mandate. Moreover, to
dissolve the democratic House and put it to
the expense and worry of an election because
the other House isn’t democratic is kicking a
friend because the enemy can’t be got at. Also
to throw out the Ministry because it doesn’t
know any better than those who made it what
to do in this particular deadlock, is wholly
ridiculous. Unless somebody can say definitely
what should be done, nobody has any right to
complain because somebody else doesn’t do it.
The British Parliament might possibly be
moved to do something to Vic. Council, but,
judging from Berry’s experience, it is highly
improbable. The Federal Parliament, if it
comes, might be endowed with power to amend
the provincial constitutions. The Government
might introduce a Bill radically amending the
Council by making it a paid body, and in other
ways, and stop supplies till it was passed ; that is
to say, it might stop the Assembly’s salaries, and
the Civil Service salaries, and the interest on
the public debt, and close up the railway ser-
vice through non-payment of wages, and make
a general stoppage of everything ; in which
case the Council might climb down or it might
not. As it would probably feel the trouble
less than most people there is no absolute
assurance that it would climb down. Again,
the Council might be attacked with waddies,
and red revolution might be let loose at the top
of Bourke-street, if anybody would revolushe,
which is doubtful. Or if £lOO,OOO were avail-
able some brainy men might work up a huge
crusade among the electors, and possibly get in
48 Rich Landlords who would be almost, though
not quite, as bad as the present ones. These
are the only things The Bulletin knows of
that can be tried, barring private assassination
or Thuggee, and it is a pity the people who yell
so much that “something should be done”
don’t explain definitely which thing they would
prefer.
The Great Cold-Tea Downfall. '
The M.L. Cold-Tea party is now trying hard
to find out what struck it. At the Local Option
poll in 1894 the Prohibitionists carried one
district only—Balclutha. This time Balclutha
has gone back on its faith to some extent, but
there has been a partial success at Hawera by
way of some compensation. The total figures
don’t show that the Prohibition movement has
retrograded to any serious extent; it certainly
hasn’t progressed as its apostles boasted it was
doing, or risen in an overwhelming wave, or
done any of the other glorious things expected
of it, but its solid band of fanatics remains
about as solid as ever. Yet the party which
returned 23 members to Parliament at the ’93
election now musters only 11 ; and, instead of
holding the balance of power, which it would
have done if its phalanx had remained un-
broken, and putting the Premiership up to
auction, it is only a lone fragment of no particu-
lar account. The party badly wants to know
how, with its adherents practically as numer-
ous as ever, it should have gone to such utter
smash as a Parliamentary force, and yet it
flatly refuses to believe the only feasible explan-
ation—that it was lost through its own aggres-
sive tactics and general unpleasantness. In ’93
the Prohibitionists did not pose as an en-
tirely distinct political organisation. The Cold
Tea candidate didn’t profess to disregard abso-
lutely everything save his dreary, indigestible
fad ; he was a Liberal or a Conservative, and
was willing to admit that ginger-beer wasn’t
the beginning and middle and end of all things
earthly. But the party grew inflated by its
partial success, and resolved to be a wholly
separate political power, and to abjure the
heathen who drank occasional beer, and have
no truck with the Amalekites, or some other
scriptural phrase to that effect. Its candidates
were, in as many cases as it could be so arranged,
men who frankly declared that the great issue
of Cold Tea overshadowed everything. They
would vote for Seddon or Russell with equal
facility, which ever would go for Prohibition
of the most uncompromising type. Their theory
was that State Socialism, land problems, old
age pensions, State Land Bank and Fire Insur-
ance schemes, finance, Protection or Freetrade
or Revenue-Tariffism, and all other matters
were side issues, and if the Cold Tea gospel
could be advanced by throwing any or all of them
overboard they should go overboard promptly.
And then the Prohibition party went down
with a crash, and great was the fall thereof.
Apparently the big success of 1893 was solely
owing to the fact that it kept the most aggra-
vated aspect of its faith out of sight. It was
carried in on the shoulders of the Liberal party,
whereas it fancied things were the other way
about, and when it resolved to stand by itself
and to invite the country to come and be cured
of all its ills by one fad alone, the fact suddenly
came to light that the Prohibition crowd was a
very ordinary and cheeky minority of no special
account. But for its lamentable tactics, the
teetotal regime might almost have arrived in
Maoriland ; as matters stand, it seems to have
gone back a dozen years or so. Consequently,
it revenges itself by using violent Dead Bird
language about all its opponents, and says, as
usual, that they are “ lost.” Every semi-pious
organisation, when it doesn’t come uppermost,
lays the pleasing unction to its soul that every-
body else is “lost” and will frizzle for un-
counted billions of years down Below.
The Beautiful “Business Man.”
The S.M. Herald, which always has an eye to
the boosting along of private enterprise, builds
a neat little sermon on the Victorian postal
department muddle. “ The irrigation scheme
has failed,” says the eminently right-thinking
paper ; “ the railway department has to take
the responsibility for useless lines which no
buxines* wan would ever have constructed, and an
enquiry regarding the management of the Post
and Telegraph Department has revealed,” &c.,
&c. The point of these remarks lies in the
assertion that no business man would ever have
constructed the unpayable railways with which
the State has burdened itself in Australia,
There are some discrepancies in the statement,
certainly, inasmuch as the people who built
these lines were mostly business men of one
kind or another—bankers, financiers, land and
house jobbers, merchants, importers, spirit-
dealers, and the like. Also, if Richard
Speight wasn’t a business man, what was he ?
Also, a business man is almost any kind of
person who sits at a table in an office, and as
the Herald can’t personally know more than a
fraction per cent, of the business men in exist-
ence its assurance that no business man would
have done the acts above alluded to is mere
reckless disregard for the Terewth In a
general way, however, the Herald’s meaning
isn’t more hazy than might have been expected
It is something to the effect that the people
who build State railways aren’t business
men, and those who build private ones
are, and no private railway builder would be
such a fool as to burden the country with such
lines as the State railway-builders construct
Yet last year every State railway system in
continental Australia and Maoriland showed
better profits than the average of the United
States, where business men have been running
the show all along on business lines. And the
United States are lavishly dotted with tem-
porarily or permanentlv abandoned lines—
abandoned because they didn’t pay for engine-
grease and other working expenses. Even in
England it is possible to find railways which
have paid no dividend for nine or ten years
and seem unlikely ever to pay one. The theory
that no business man would build a hopelessly
unprofitable line on private enterprise prin-
ciples is utterly knocked on the head by the
fact that these same men have built such things
over half the globe, and are still doing it Even
Melbourne’s dreadful Outer Circle railway was
never so ruinous as many of the works which
business men have constructed in the western
States of the U.S. Australia has certainly
muddled its national railway system, but, com-
pared to many samples of private enterprise, its
management has been a thing of absolute
genius. Duncan Gillies himself was a better
manager, if judged by results, than the
majority of the private enterprise railroaders of
the western U.S and, considering how very
little can be said for Duncan, it is gratifying to
a^e to sa y this much.

The Philosophy of the Gum-Tree.
a New South Wales bush district two boys
are awaiting trial, at this glad New Year
season, on a charge of murdering and carefully
cremating the old man. W hether they did it
or no L or whether it was the old man or some
ot her person who was cremated, it is certain
that somebody was so successfully disposed of
a bonfire that only a few buttons and boot-
nads and part of a skull were found among the
ashes. The two sons assisted the police to sift
remains of the alleged old man, and
s P ec ial note is made of the fact that they did so
without any sign of interest or emotion what-
soever. This doesn t prove anything at all
about the alleged murder, but it does help to
P rove the sad, depressed, unemotional casual-
ness of Australia junior, in the bush. Any in-
dividual with ordinary nerves would have been
a Bttle interested in the work of fishing out any-
body s remains from the ash-heap, but Aus-
tralia junior, as he is in the bush, takes that
matter as tranquilly as he takes most others,
exce pt horse. If the crime is brought home to
the accused pair, or to anybody else like them,
USUfd wail about the results of a godless
system of education may be expected to follow
a ® a matter of course. And the fact that the
dire monotony of the plains and the burnt ridges
and the gum-tree and the crow might have any-
thing to do with it will never be even alluded to
by the orthodox. Yet, in all probability, the
plains and the gum-tree and the crow are more
largely responsible than anything else, and the
dry creeks and the burnt grass and the laughing
jackass and the interminable wire fences also
bear a considerable share of the responsibility,
In o system of education, however godly, could
bear up against them, even if godly education
didn t consist so largely as it does of shudder-
some stories about battle, murder, and premedi-
fated disembowelment in Israel. Tragedy in
dr y bush regions of Australia has a charac-
er *ts °wn. Generally there is very little
motive > or visible motive at all, save the sad,
torpid looniness brought about by long com-
pression of the brain with the dead weight of
much gum-tree and kookaburra, and much
waterless immensity. It isn t done suddenly
or vvith anger ; there is a slow, dull method
about it, born of the fact that the world moves
slowly in the bush, and there is any amount of
leisure, and the corpse that isn t cremated to-
day can always be frizzled to-morrow. And the
person accused of the bush horror, whether he
* s acc u sed rightly or wrongly, is almost always
described as accepting the situation with abso-
late indifference. It is the custom to set this
down also to the godless system of education,
but it is gum-tree rather than godlessness,
Everyone grows to be indifferent in the second
° r tLird. generation of existence in the land of
l° n S> l° n S dusty roads and miles and miles of
monotonous wire or post-and-rail fence. Life
ouly means more gum-tree and dry creek and
burnt ridge or dusty, limitless plain, more crow
and kookaburra, and all the rest. Death means
no ° n . e knows exactly what; the person whose
s °ul i s deadened with years of plain and sad
eucalyptus hasn t any wondrous ideals of the
hereafter. Moreover, if he has emotions they
don fc sur f ac ®> f° r the ponderous
bulk of the eucalyptus and the dry ridge are on
top of them to hold them down ; therefore he
gets a reputation for callousness and indifference
—which may or may not be wholly deserved.
The proposal to awaken the selector’s off-
spring to a brighter state by teaching him in
his school days about the dry wastes of Syria
and the 40-years plodding of Israel through a
thirsty wilderness is sadly misappropriate,
An individual with so much dust and drought
in his own existence can’t be elevated by infor-
mation about the dust and drought of 4000
years ago. Irrigation and artesian wells and
green grass would do more to put light into the
soul of the young bush Australian than all the
arid Scripture which ever came out of waterless
Palestine.

The Federationist with One Idea.
A vigorous attempt is already being made to
intrude the fiscal ghost at the approaching
Federal Convention elections. It is to be hoped
that the country will put a large hoof on this
nefarious scheme and squash it flat. The busi-
ness of the Convention is to make a Federal
Government, and that Government will decide,
by direction of the people, the future tariff
policy of Australia. If the O’Sullivan or Max
Hirsch wants to talk Protection, or Freetrade,
or Single-tax to the people at this juncture he
should be ordered to grind his little pet axe
elsewhere. The fact that a candidate belongs
to some particular fiscal creed is as irrelevant
on this occasion as the fact that he has
a broken nose or red hair, or that he is
a boss footballer. No sane individual sug-
gests that the future tariff policy of Aus-
tralia should be prescribed in the constitu-
tion, and that this generation should seriously
attempt to tie down all future ones to its
peculiar tariff belief, whatever it may be.
Therefore, the man whose sole claim to con-
sideration is that he is a prominent Protec-
tionist or Freetrader or Single-Taxer or Pro-
hibitionist isn’t wanted ; he may know a lot
about these things, but none of them are going
to be in the constitution, and his knowledge is
of no use. He is as much out of place as the
man who wants to command an ironclad fleet
on the ground that he is a specially,successful
shoemaker. The work in hand is to make a
constitution which will have no large, unwork-
able holes in it, and which will be capable of
amendment at any time by the exercise of the
popular will. This latter is far more essen-
tial than that it should be perfect just
now. The constitution which is absolutely
without flaw at the date of its construction—
that is to say, which is wholly in accord with
the popular sentiment of the majority to-day—
would probably be antiquated, useless, and
utterly out of accord with the ideas of 50 years
hence. Therefore, if a perfect constitution is
made now, and so fixed as to be practically
incapable of amendment, the chances are that
it will lead by-and-bye to revolution. The con-
stitutions of Tasmania and Victoria, which
were adjusted with such beautiful ingenuity as
to put the Upper House practically beyond
amendment, are cases in point; they were very
respectable institutions when they were made,
and they are now awful beyond expression.
Therefore, the matter at issue is the making of
an elastic constitution—one which can be
amended to meet all manner of circumstances
which it is now impossible to foresee and defin-
itely provide against, and which won’tattemptto
rivet any fleeting enthusiasm of to-day onto the
people of to-morrow. In these circumstances
there is no use for the individual whose sole
claim for consideration is that he is an
able defender of only one particular brand of
anything, and he should be rejected without a
moment’s hesitation. The individual whose
whole life is devoted to the defence of one par-
ticular sample of something is almost always of
opinion that his especial nostrum is so beautiful
and such a perfect panacea for everything that
it should be embedded in the constitution
where no one will ever be able to get it out
again. This applies equally to the person who
has risen to note solely on the sectarian racket,
or the Cold Tea racket, or on Freetrade, Pro-
tection, Single-tax, the defence of “the Con-
stitooshon,” and all the other men of one idea.
They should all be left out, but as the men with
the one fiscal idea promise to make the most
trouble they should be left out even more en-
thusiastically than the rest. The only person
who calls for even more prompt rejection than
the man of one idea is the man of no idea at all.
The proposal of Copeland, M.L.A., to open
hotels during certain hours on Sundays roused
Parson Boyce, of the New South Wales Local
Option League, to much wrath in the columns
of S.M. Herald. Says Boyce :
Directly the Sunday closing law of 1882 came into force
there was a substantial decrease in the convictions for
Sunday drunkenness, and an immediate improvement in
the order and as to the peacefulness of the day. The
closing did immense good. As to convictions for drunken-
ness and “drunk and disorderly,” I ask especial attention
to the figures Mr. Gould very recently gave in Parliament.
He said that the convictions for last year for the above-
named offences on Saturdays were 5860, and for Sundays
only 1196. How great the difference ! In 1894 it was
notable that at 160 of our police-courts there were no con-
victions for those offences. In that year the Saturday
convictions were 5062, as against 1245 for Sundays.
Should Mr. Copeland’s Bill be passed, a large increase in
the convictions for drunkenness may be very confidently
anticipated. It is a gruesome picture.
This is an elaborate dodging of the whole ques-
tion, which is : Does Sunday closing diminish
the sum total of drunkenness ? If the number
of arrests counts for anything it doesn’t. In
1881, when hotels were open on Sunday, there
were 22,560 arrests. In 1883, when they were
closed, the arrests numbered 23,178. They
steadily increased till 1886, when they num-
bered 26,310. After that a decline set
in. Boyce, it will be observed, doesn’t
say that drunkenness diminished. He doesn’t
even say that Sunday drunkenness dimin-
ished. He simply asserts that after the hotels
were closed on the Day of Drought there were
fewer arrests for drunkenness on Sunday than
on Saturday, which is quite true. There were
also fewer drunks on Sunday than on Saturdays
in the days when the hotels were open, but this
fact is carefully suppressed, thereby creating an
altogether false impression that Sunday-closing
had something to do with the difference—a
point on which there is no evidence at all. If
arrests count for anything, then it seems that
drunkenness was steadily declining until the
Sunday-closing law came into force, when there
was a sudden and violent reaction, and it took
eight years to get over the shock and return to
the state of sobriety attained in the old Sunday-
beer days. There is no proof, of course, that
Sunday-closing caused drunkenness, but there
is abundant proof in the Government Statis-
tician’s figures that it didn’t prevent it. And
Boyce’s way of leaving the real question out,
and throwing in wholly irrelevant figures that
have no more to do with the matter than has
the diameter of Saturn, is a sorrowful example
of parsonical logic.
Premier Reid’s suggestion that the State
should resume all the Sydney wharves is a big
and statesmanlike idea if it is only carried out
the right way. But the first essential, before
any more large transactions of the kind are
attempted, is a law which fixes some kind of
relation between the tax valuation and the
resumption value, so that the State vron’t be
swindled more than is absolutely necessary. As
things are now, a valuer may swear for tax or
probate purposes that a property is only worth
.£lOOO, and a month later, when it is a’ case of
resuming the property, the same business gent,
without any fear of a prosecution for gross per-
jury, can swear that it is good value for
£lO,OOO or thereabouts. Also, the system of
leaving valuations to juries is unprofitable,
because the average jury is always agin’ the
Government, and the system of fixing valua-
tions by any kind of law proceedings is bad
because the ghastly item of costs swallows up
nearly everything. Every item of city pro-
perty has a value put on it for purposes of
municipal taxation, and every square-inch
of land is further valued for State
taxation purposes, yet by the weird sys-
tem now in force, neither of these valua-
tions bears any reference whatever to the
price the State has to pay when it wants to re-
sume. Further, if the Government is going to
borrow a large sum to pay for the wharves, it
would be interesting to know if there is to be a
sinking-fund attached to the loan, or if the
price of the present wharves is to be a perman-
ent burden on posterity, and if, when they
wear out, new ones are to be built also w r ith
borrowed money, and so on till the only assets
to show against millions of buried cash are the
memories of piles and timbers which rotted
away in past ages. There are places in Sydney
where three successive Government wharves
have worn out and gone to pieces, but the one
that disappeared 40 years ago isn’t paid for yet,
and its memory is still set down as a “ per-
manent and reproductive w'ork,” and the in-
terest is going on to this day.
Some N.S.W. country schoolmasters complain
that Sydney University will not confer degrees
without attendance on lectures. Therefore, no
matter what their deserts as tested by examina-
tion, country students are given no University
recognition unless they are prepared to spend,
at the lowest, £2OO or £3OO during enforced
residence in Sydney. So far as The Bulletin’s
experience goes, a Sydney University degree is
no testimonial of intellectual power or learning :
bright spirits and dullards seem to achieve it
with equal ease. But the label has still its
value in scholastic circles, apparently; and,
that being so, there is no reason why it should
not be tacked to all who earn it, whether lec-
tured at Sydney or not. The residence plan,
as applied at Sydney University, is a ridiculous
waste of time and money. In older countries,
and at an earlier day, University residence im-
plied a deliberate sacrifice of the student to his
studies. His whole life was under the scrutiny
of the officials ; his incomings and outgoings were
watched ; the formation of his character was
no less cared for than the development of his
mind. In Sydney, so long as he attends the
lectures, a student does practically as he
pleases. The University takes no cognisance
or control of his outer life. But the pretext of
lectures and residence is put forward to justify
the appointment of professors at extravagant
salaries which neither their work nor their
worth entitle them to. With some exceptions,
the Sydney and Melbourne professorships are
merely pools of stagnation. The holders work
to get them ; then they work as little as
possible to keep them. The results are dis-
gracefully small in proportion to expenditure.
A few rich men’s sons drift in and out of the
lecture-halls ; poor lads in Sydney may manage
to attend evening classes ; in the country they
are excluded altogether. It will always be so
as long as the State heavily subsidises an insti-
tution which it does not directly control, and
maintains it, not as an integral part of the
national educational system, but as that sys-
tem’s ridiculous and expensive excrescence.
Editor Bulletin, —The extraordinary scenes
that so often occur in Melbourne, the city of
sects, where fervent religionists accuse one an-
other of sexual immoralities, may tickle the
imaginations of a few, but they must make the
judicious grieve. The scoffer is apt to declare
that religion is proved an incentive to sensu-
ality, while the orthodox contend that sensuality
has demoralised religion. Either conclusion is
unsatisfactory. The fact appears to be even
worse, viz., that much religious passion and
much sexual passion are merely forms of hys-
teria. Among the churches and the sects there
are some that are more aggressively sectarian
than others—that is to say, they are given to
enthusiastic schism among themselves. They
disrupt at intervals, and, like certain micro-
scopic monsters when cut up, each portion im-
mediately acquires the power of separate or-
ganic existence. It is among these that you
find individuals possessed of a creed as poor
wretches of old were possessed of devils. It
is among such that salacious scandals thrive
at certain periods. In other bodies of less
centrifugal energy the scandals remain un-
noticed, or are hushed up. Of course, in many
of these sects, religion, considered as a high
spiritual or philosophic ideal, is almost un-
known. The motive-power, so to speak, is
only quasi-spiritual, and induced by sensuous
hymn-tunes and hypnotic sermons. Here is an
illustrative fact. The present writer has seen,
in a Ballarat hall, a thousand people on their
knees, singing ecstatically an emotional hymn,
eyes closed, hands clasped, bodies swaying as
if in a savage incantation; here and there
women became hysterical, while strong men
were rendered almost helpless by convulsive
sobbing. A percentage of such a crowd must
suffer mentally by abnormal excitement of this
sort. Having succumbed to emotions of one
kind, emotions of another will be less readily
resisted.—F.
Why there are unemployed in N.S. Wales :
During December “ that great country ” imported
oranges from Syria, lemons and dried fruits from
Europe, sugar from Mauritius, and wheat, tinned
meats, and flour from America. This is what is
meant by our “self-reliant spirit.”

After the vast outbreak last week, when
every Sydney theatre blossomed forth with a new
show for the season of the assassinated fowl,
theatrical matters have boiled down to a compara-
tive calm. The Cri. alone announces a change for
Saturday—Oscar Wilde’s “ Woman of No Import-
ance,” which should go well if it is anything like
the other plays by the same yellow decadent which
Sydney has witnessed. Meanwhile, “Nancy and
Co.” is still in possession, and the audience is
steadily falling in love with Miss Hope Mavne’s
pretty little brogue, and Mario Majeroni’s dazzling
front teeth shine forth like a lantern on a wreck.
The stately Majeroni is much less stiff than he
used to be, and promises to be a very valuable per-
son by-and-bye. The very latest feature about the
co. at present is Percy Ward’s laugh—a low, in-
ward cackle which he only lets off three times in
the course of the evening. There has been nothing
more remarkable in this line at the Cri. since
George Leitch’s maniac cachinnated there in two
syllables many years ago. Next week the co.’s
very newest new man, Edward Ferris, makes his
appearance, likewise Miss Florence Seymour and
Mrs. Bracy.
** * *
Westmacott’s pantomime at Her Majesty’s
Is as remarkable for Crackles’ multiplicity of hats
as it was in the beginning, and it is getting nearly
time that some person put these interminable head-
gear into a quartz-battery and pounded them
under the stampers. The management lately
arranged to have its audience photographed, but,
with true gentlemanly feeling, it gave notice of its
purpose by advt., so that the man who was sup-
posed to be at the office making up the half-yearly
balance had due warning not to take his bar-
Flossie there on the fatal occasion. A second
edition of “Sinbad” is in reserve to be produced
when occasion calls for it. Meanwhile, the old
edition is getting on well enough. The audience
has now become hardened against Crackles’
numerous hats, and they don’t take its attention
off the fine scenic effects so much as they did.
And even if Crackles had six times as many hats
as he has, it would be worth while to suffer them
all, and the other jokes as well, for the sake of the
pictorial dazzle of the show.
** * *
At Sydney Royal, “ Tommy Atkins ” still
rages furiously in his red coat, and the wicked-
ness of H. Diver is black beyond expression.
Diver is one of the most bowefless reprobates in
melodrama. He hasn’t the occasional moments of
remorse with which the ordinary villain is
afflicted ; he never looks back regretfully on his
mis-spent life, nor is he suddenly recalled to
thoughts of his innocent boyhood by the sight of
a red cow drifting across a paddock just as the
red cow used to do at home in childhood’s happy
hours. He looks a great deal happier, on the
whole, than the hero, and the ghost of his victims
doesn’t haunt him at all, and he dies quite as com-
fortably as anybody else. The moral which Diver
conveys is that, if you are unspeakably wicked
and habitually bathe in gore you may have
a good billet and a nicely - fitting uniform
all your life, and enjoy good cigars and
pleasant wines, and have the shiniest boots in
the regiment, and harry the good man all
the days of your life, and keep a harem, and
borrow money from the heavy father, and gener-
ally damn everything. And the other moral, as
conveyed by Plimmer, is that if you are a good
man you may be a curate at £2 a week, and after
that a private soldier, and wait ten years for your
best girl who has been ruined by the wicked cap-
tain in the interim, and the heavy father won’t
lend you a copper. On the whole, the wicked cap-
tain has so much the best of the deal that the
present writer gives up any attempt at being good
from this date, and proposes henceforth to be a
bloodthirsty desperado through his agent.
** * *
The man in woman’s clothes at the Lyceum
continues to moderate audiences —most Sydney
audiences just now range from moderate to awful,
there being far more theatre afloat than there is
any real demand for. Further consideration of
this personage induces The Bulletin to put him
even ahead of the Widow O’Brien ; he is plea-
santer and brighter and more womanly, and as a
middle-aged colleen he looks more real than the
real colleen— if there is any such person—often
does. The play, however, suffers considerably
from the fact that it isn t distinctly either one
thing or another or anything in particular. It is
partly burlesque and partly alleged drama, but
Scot Inglis embezzling the trust-money behind
a screen is obviously never intended to be
a real villain; everybody can see that he
will repent and become painfully good at
the first opportunity, and that Harrie Ireland is
only playing at being led astray and having hor-
rible designs on Ida Hamilton. After a feeling
remark or two from the Irishwoman these two
people instantly decide to marry and be noble
characters for ever afterwards, as they obviously
intended to be all along, and the old parental
forger swears off his wickedness in the back-
ground, and even the people who never aid any
harm resolve to be better than ever. If only one
bad person would remain wicked and walk out un-
regenerate, saying “ damn ” as he goes, it would
improve “Lady Blarney” considerably. It is
absolutely bursting with goodness at the end, and
one refreshing sin would be an oasis.
A number of smaller shows remain in un-
accountable good health, considering that not one
of the big theatres is crushing more than oaw .
to the ton. The gorgeous Palace and the livoii
are both well-filled, and the two Cineinatographes
earn a very respectable crust all the tune.
Jerusalem Cyclorama and Edison s Electric
Parlor also seem to lead a paying existence,
though the persistent demand for such a painTuiiy
ordinary place as Jerusalem, in this land of god-
less education, is hardly accountable. One or u o
small entertainments, however, have gone to tna
long home which is six feet by two, and their
departure has excited the less interest inasmuc
as they were not very definitely alive at any time.
Peace to their dust, if there was enough of them
to make any.

“ Matsa ” is unfolding its gilded splendor
to crowded houses at Melb. Princess’s, and thusfa,r
the pantomimic shortcomings of the I 1 irm s lates
thing in annuals have made no difference to busi-
ness. The spectacle alone is quite good enough to
go on with. When the time arrives fqr the per-
formance to pull itself together, and give further
assistance to the scenery and dresses, that neces-
sary improvement will probably happen,
the early samplers of “ Matsa ” will be persuaded
without difficulty to revisit ancient Memphis and
book more seats for the Rising of the Nile. An
Egyptian ballet at Memphis, preceded by the
arrival of Pharaoh and suite, is a very fine color
effect, and many other things that occur ages ago
are impressive in their way. Pharoah himself
can give points to Lord Brassey, also the four
attendant heralds, who blow barbaric blasts from
abnormal trumpets, seem to harmonise with the
surroundings. “Matsa” is much stronger in
bursts of noise than in outbreaks of merriment.
The bones of “ I)jin Djin ” —which was a pretty,
cheerful extravaganza —protrude from the present
entertainment, instead of being nicely covered
with new meat. Worse than that, the comic-opera
artists who shaped tamely in “Djin Djin” are
now more apathetic than ever, and the one live
success of the company is the pigmy comedian,
Little Gulliver. Leoni Clarke, who ranks as an
auxiliary force, gets applause for his performing
animals, but advance rumors re the Cat King’s
entertainment were a long way ahead of the
commonplace result. A procession of pantomime
animals in the second act is a much more amusing
item, anyway.
#* * *
Bland Holt’s military hippodrama at Mel-
bourne Royal appears to have caught on as per
expectation. Beginning with a stage fox-hunt in
several tableaux, and ending with an all-round
denunciation of the bold, bad Major, “ Eor Eng-
land ”is a thing to gaze upon and forget. The
Bulletin, in consideration of this being Xmas
time or very near it, is prepared to condone
Walter Baker's unnatural love for Queen Victoria,
likewise, on this occasion only, the British Flag
may be granted a special license to wave rather
more than usual. “ For England ” is a
lavishly - mounted perversion of the truth,
containing a splendidly - impossible bill - dis-
counter. A buoyant Hebrew character part which
introduces Bland Holt as a Zulu on the warpath
would be a fair set-off against the rest of the story
even if all the residue were Flag and flam. There
are, however, other acceptable people besides
Manasses Marks. Misses Elizabeth Watson and
Frances Ross, Mrs. Bland Holt, Charles Brown,
and one or two grief-stricken parents count for
something in the broken narrative, whilst the
hunting-scene deserves great credit for not having
caused any funerals up to date.

Melbourne Bijou is looking healthy on
“The Strange Adventures of Miss Brown,” the
comedy that might as well be “ Charley’s Aunt
disguised as a Boarding School Girl.” A long-
winded detective, played by Guyton Heath, is a
serious drawback to “ Miss Brown.” This alleged
expert from Scotland Yard bears no sort of re-
semblance to any “d.” that ever lived, neither
does he suggest a possibility of his being repro-
duced in the flesh between now and Doomsday,
and when he looms large in the public eye the eye
grows weary. A comedy able to thrive despite
such a heavy load of title and detective certainly
deserves to get on. Frank Thornton, as the red-
headed heroine, is not the sole support of the new
show, although it has no purpose apart from his
escapade in skirts. Some of the minor characters
are well-drawn, and the small cluster of girls in
the academy for young ladies was evidently
arranged by one who has been there. Miss
