The Irish major, proposing the health of the happy
pair in “ Adventures of Miss Brown,” never fails to raise
a laugh all round. “Mav your lives be long, and your
cares be little ones" Astonishing how quickly both sexes
“catch on” to these matrimonial suggestions.
A Melb. music-seller received the following note from a
Gippsland timber-getter’s wife : “ Plese send me at onst,
‘ Say a roarer not to-day,’ for a A flat voice.” And now
the music-seller doesn’t know whether to send, her a
broken-winded horse or the song “ Say Au Revoir,” etc.
Death recently removed an old Australian theatrical
identity in the person of Charles Wilmot. In 1859 he
supported G. V. Brooke, and later on William Hoskins
and George Coppin. After a tour in Maoriland with
Clarence Holt, he returned to England in 1868, and
became host of the famous old Coal Hole Tavern off the
Strand, where he died.
There is a realistic life-size figure of Christ on the cross
in the Lady Chapel, St. Francis’s, Melh. The spike
through the feet is kissed by hundreds every day and
night.
Melb. : At St. Patrick’s, this Christmas, there was
a well - arranged figure-tableau of the Holy Family.
Four Cupids hung from the ceiling, as angels, and each
had the orthodox bit of calico round his waist, so that the
Holy Family might be decent and respectable.
Paradis : “ Matsa only lacks the fun of pantomime,
the good old stuff that used to be put in by Harwood
and Greville. The eye is dazzled and drunk.”
Shifter : “ The actor who left the rim of his pewter-
pot on the morning newspaper is pretty well dead. The
boozy stage-hand may as well go too. He is distinctly
one of those persons on Koko’s list of people who are
wholly superfluous. In London and New York, nowadays,
they insist on the stage-hand having something of the
same professional feeling as the actor. He used to be
anybody; now he must be somebody.”
Apropos of Priscilla Verne’s outbreak at Perth when
she, with a large number of supporters, spanked a solitary
dude under circumstances not too creditable to Priscilla
—a similar attempt was once made by an ex-actress
well-known in Australia, a lady who used to be comely,
and who once promised to be a star of some magnitude.
Possibly it is wrong to speak of her as an ex-actress, for
she still breaks out occasionally, but as she is becoming
more “ex” every day the term is good enough. She
went forth in her young and reckless days to wallop all
editor, and by a grievous misjudgment she took with her
the two smallest and most peaceful members of her co.
as supporters. The trio marched into the editorial roon
the lady and the horse-whip in front. “ Sir,” she said in
a terrible voice : “ I’ve come to horsewhip you for your
statements in yesterday’s paper.” “ What ?” roared the
small editor, rising up with thunder in his eye. When he
rose it proved that he wasn’t nearly so small as he
appeared while sitting down ; he was one of these long-
legged, short-bodied men who are deceptive when in a
chair. The two mild men in the rear of the procession
took one glance at him as he uncoiled himself,
skipped silently down the stair, and then ran their hardest
for the nearest pub. But the lady, having no idea that
her supporters had deserted her, still faced the enemy.
“Sir,” she said, waving the whip, “you are a ruffian
“ Madam,” interrupted the editor, “ if you touch me with
that thing I’ll” Just then the whip came down. The
man of letters jumped for his enemy and got a second cut
—across the face this time—as he reached her. Then he
possessed himself of the whip, pinned the lady by th.e
neck with her face to the wall, and for two minutes the
proceedings consisted wholly of “swish.” Finally she
broke away and escaped down the stairs, and the editor
went back to finish his leading article on the Eastern
Question. That actress is never known to have assaulted
even the smallest editor since then, and has taken many
adverse criticisms in a humble spirit and with exemplary
patience.
It is alleged that Walter Baker, some 14 years ago, got
£25 reward for chasing a burglar four miles through the
bush at Dubbo (N.S.W.), and effecting his single-handed
capture. Though he is a good deal older and stouter, he
could do the same feat now, if called upon. Continuous
pursuit of Albert Norman through the wilderness of the
six-act drammer does a lot to keep Baker in condition.
When Fitzgeralds’ Circus crossed the Queensland border
a conscientious inspector insisted on oiling all the horses
and pomes, in case of ticks. But, though the indignant
Dan Fitzgerald pointed out for over half-an-hour that it
was clearly the inspector’s duty to oil the lions also, these
animals had to come through in their former infested
condition An attempt to persuade him to dip the tiee>-
also failed. If the tick does get into N.S.W., that per-
functory inspector will be distinctly responsible.

Some bitter language from Melb Punch: “Mrs. Cora
Urquhart Brown-Potter has gone from Melbourne, but
her influence remains, particularly amongst the girls of
St. liilda, where Cora ‘ put up,’ and vvhere she was
frequently to be seen in daylight, haunting the sands
fluttering in uncanny dresses, and with grotesque, dis-
hevelled hair, looking like a distracted banshee going
home with the milk. Scores of the girls of the suburb
have caught the Potter stare and the Potter straddle,
and they let their hair run wild and catch it back in a
reckless manner, cultivate pale yellow complexions and
eerie hats and lunatic gowns, and canter around, re-
sembling so many dyspeptic hens that have mislaid an
egg.”
Old friend Genevieve Ward plays Margaret of Anjou in
Irving’s revival of 11 Richard 111. This is the “ Richard
III.” that Shakespeare built (not Colley Cibber’s version),
in which Queen Margaret curses at large, and perfidious
Clarence declaims his awfuldream. Itisalways an interest-
ing production on account of the people who, knowing all
the Colley Cibber gags by heart, stare astonished at
Richard when he doesn’t cry “ Off with his head-so
much for Buckingham ! ” but merely answers “ That’s the
best news of all.”
Haresfoot: “Williamson and Musgrove’s programme
for ’97 is mostly a plunge into the romantic drama, with
the ‘ Prisoner of Zenda,’ ‘ Under the Red Robe,’ and
other pieces which have been tremendous London hits,
including the ‘Two Little Vagabonds.’ A Gaiety company
of some kind is also probable. The ‘ Artist’s Model,’ the
‘ Gay Parisienne,’ the ‘Little Genius,’ the ‘White Silk
Dre-s,’ and other successful plays of that kind should also
go well after their light and frivolous fashion in Australia.
Quite a torrent of new faces will be poured in ’97 on the
Princess’s stage.”
Miss Pollard was within an ace of being burnt, in
“Matsa,” the other night. But providentially our un-
combusted May is still with us.

Spagnoletti : “ Leoni Clark’s Cat Show makes me think
of a Swede who had a lot of performing rats in Paris. In
the midst of the show a little girl threw a bun among the
rats. They wolfed it. The proprietor cast up his hands,
exclaiming, ‘No more performance to-day. Only hunget
drives them to it.’ ”
There is an unwritten melodramatic canon which
decrees that the hero shall not smoke on the stage. The
cigarette is as necessary an adjunct to a stage villain as
evening dress and a top hat. The giddy society girl, the
panto, principal boy, the idiotic Johnny, the gay adven-
turess, and sometimes the heroine (as in “Trilby”)
indulge in a whiff. But the miserable hero, in almost
every case, is out of it.
Zangwill on the actor : “ Actors are not even parrots-
they are automatic puppets that move their limbs in fixed
fashions, and make squeaking sounds at prescribed
moments. There was a French Ministf r of Education
who drew up a most rigid time-code, which hung in his
bureau ; and it was the joy of his life to take out his
watch and say, ‘ Half-past three ! Ha! every boy in
France is now' learning geography’; or, ‘A quarter to
twelve ! Ha ! every French schoolgirl is now writing in a
copy-book.' I have the same sort of feeling about my
actor acquaintances. ‘Half-past nine? Ah! what is
Herbert doing? He is taking poison.’ ‘A quarter to
eleven ! Dear me ! Rose is craw ling under a table.’ And
these creatures want every privilege, forsooth! Fame,
gold, champagne, the best society and the worst. To be
of Bohemia and Belgravia, to make the best of both
worlds. If things don’t mend, to sit in a stall will soon
become an index of imbecility. It will be like being seen
at the Academy.”
“ Robbery Under Arms ” was recently played at Dundee
(Scotland), where a sad Scotch audience took it seriously
as a true story of modern Australia.
Trouble is apparently brewing between
the associated marine engineers and the Australian
steamship owners. The Association asked the
other day for more pay and other things, whereon
the owners bombarded every individual engineer
with a circular which instructed him to state at
once whether this beastly request met with his
approval, and significantly warned him to “give
the matter your most serious consideration before
jeopardising your position in the most highly-paid
shipping service in the world.” The engineers
rather objected to beihg menaced and cut off in
detail like this, and things at time of writing Were
sultry. The right of 24 hours’ notice to leave is
the best weapon—almost the only weapon—the
engineers have. It puts employers face-to-face
with this dilemma : they must heat the men’s
protests or lay up their ships—thebe is no time to
hire blacklegs. So, when the s.s. Wollowra’s
engineers in Adelaide, the other day, gave 24
hours’ notice, the wrath of the Adelaide Shipping
Co. was boundless. “Notify the Wollowra engi-
neers,” wired the co.’s secretary, “on behalf of
the co., that the current agreement with their
institute continues till 24th February. Summarily
terminating the same constitutes a gross and
dishonorable breach, and their conduct in desert-
ing their ship is disloyal to their employers
and unbecoming as officers, subversive of
all discipline, and a shocking example to
the crew, besides reducing their institute
to the level of an ordinary trades-union.”
The men were obviously within their legal rights,
or the co. would not have fallen to abuse. It is
amazed that its employees will not make war on
terms which will ensure it a victory. And the
fine old fetish-words “honor” and “loyalty”—
the claptrap which has justified currishness
and servility in every generation—are invoked on
behalf of Fat Man wounded in his tender pocket.
The engineers will reduce their institute “to the
level of an ordinary trades-union” —the trades-
union which has done all the fighting, has won all
the victories, has made independent labor possible
and tyranny difficult and odious ! In the annals
of struggling Toil there is no prouder position
than that—on “the level of an ordinary trades-
union.”

The Wild-Cat Column,
Treasurer Reid, of N.S.W., evidently intends
making 1 a great effort to get 18 months’ land and income
tax into the present financial year, and the ukase has
gone forth that all return' are to be sent iri on or before
lebruary 5. Considering that the Department is still
snowed up under a mountain of appeals and isn’t likely
to get last year’s land-tax put reasonably straight before
March, and also that some even of the Sydney and
suburban notices for last year haven’t been received
yet, this looks a little previous. The landmarks of G.
Reid’s finance are an interesting study. He came into
office about the middle of 1894, and the shortage for that
year he alleged to be entirely caused by his wicked pre-
decessors. For the half-year ended 3l)th June, 1895, Reid
first claimed a surplus ; then it turned out, by his own
statement in Parliament, that the deficit was £149,000,
while the Auditor-General showed it as £186,000.
The Auditor-General convicted Reid of trying to swell nis
accounts and so produce a surplus for himself and
a partially bogus deficit for his predecessor by including
some £400,000 belonging to past years as part of the
current revenue. This was about the worst thing of
which a N.S.W. Treasurer was ever convicted, and
it says a lot for G. Reid’s assurance and the som-
nolence of his constituents that it didn’t smash him
utterly. Next, for the financial year which ended 30th
June, 1896 —the first complete year’s accounts presented
by the present N.S.W. Treasurer—the alleged surplus
was £330,000, and the real deficit, as shown by the
Government Statistician (“Seven Colonies” for 1896 j page
363) was £635,000, so the discrepancy between the two
sets of figures was close on £1,000,090. The peculiar
dodge on this occasion was the “ cash basis ” device, by
which nearly a million of the year’s ordinary outlay was
added to the floating debt and not charged against
revenue at all. The next that was distinctly heard about
Treasurer Reid’s finance was a month ago, when it ap-
peared that the expenditure for the first five months of
the financial year was already £168,000 ahead of the
revenue. At the end of the six months the shortage reached
£336,000. The device by which the surplus is to be pro-
duced or the deficit minimised at 30th June, 1897, is
already obvious, and consists in collecting one-half
of the land and income taxes in advance, and thus
shoving part of the 1898 revenue into 1897. How the
dodge for 1898 will be worked is a mystery, but
probably Reid expects to be out of office by that
time, and to leave the trouble to his successor, while he
sits by and tells how much better he did things. The
land and income tax being already paid partly in advance
can hardly, then, be collected further in advance. The
“ cash basis ” idea by which a lot of the year's expenditure
is charged to next year will also be usod .up. The other
“ cash basis ” idea of charging a lot of 1 Current expen-
diture back to the “ Dibbs deficit ” will „e played out by
then. It is hardly possible to drag another £400,000 or
so of loan-money into revenue on the strength of an
alleged bookkeeping mistake of 10 or 20 years back. Alto-
gether it looks as if G. Reid was getting near the end of
his financial “ fakes,” and by another 12 or 18 months he
should be quite at the end of them.
« » * *
The New South Wales revenue for the half-year
ended 31st December is £228,074 worse than that for the
corresponding period of 1895. But if the customs duties
had remained as they were in 1895, and the land and
income taxes hadn’t been imposed, it is reckoned that,
other things being equal, there would have been an in-
crease of £47,000. The loan money spent on works
during the half-year has been £719,939, or at the rate of
nearly £1,440,000 a year. This is considerably more than
the rate for the last two years, though even then the
borrowed money curse went on at the rate of over IJ4
millions per annum. It seems to be an unwritten law in
N.S.W. that a million to I>4 million of borrowed money
must be got through annually, and, as the population
doesn’t increase at quite the same ratio as the borrow-
ings, the debt per head gets a shade bigger every year.
» * * *
The Victorian revenue for the six months just
ended is £118,827 better than for the half ended Decem-
ber, ’95, and as the abolition of the primage duties in-
volved a loss of £43,000, the increase, on last year’s basis,
should have been about £162,000. This is the iirst notable
improvement since ’B9.
» * » *
Tasmania’s revenue for the year is .£45,000
better than for 1895.
* * * •
Treasurer Fysh, of Tasmania, reports in a proud
tone of voice that his surplus will probably be £70,000.
Considering that last year Tasmania charged £llO,OOO of
ordinary expenditure to loans, it certainly should have a
surplus. In fact, seeing how beautifully simple it is to
pay ordinary ex.’s out of loan funds, there is absolutely
no reason why the surplus shouldn’t be £790,000 instead
of only £70,000.
* * * *
Once a year or so Victoria issues a list of its
railways, divided into three classes. The first lot are
those which don’t pay working expenses; lot No. 2 pay
expenses, but don’t fully cover interest on cost of con-
struction ; No. 3 yield a profit over and above working
expenses and interest. Every now and then a line crawls
successfully out of list No. 1 and into No. 2, and
there is gladness all round. Then the country watches
with breathless interest its snail-like progress through
No. 2 list into No. 3. The Beechworth-Yackandandah
line is now within £33 of getting out of No. 1 list alto-
gether, and the Murtoa-Hopetoun line has just struggled
out of No. 2 and got into the paying category. That
awful noise in the distance is the Vic. Treasurer cheering
himself hoarse as a consequence.
• * * •
The S.A. State Bank, the best-managed con-
cern of its kind up to date, has now made advances
amounting to £llO,OOO, and approved of £65,000 more.
Applications are coming in at the rate of 70 to 80 a day.
Also, the institution has paid expenses from the start, the
f>remium at which its bonds were sold covering all pre-
iininary charges. A State Bank which pays its way from
the first day of its existence is something for Kingston to
be proud of.
* • * •
The National Mutual Life Association has just
finished a much better year than some it has recently ex-
perienced. The renewal premiums (£190,919) were £3OOO
better than last year, and new premiums ( £22,816) showed
an improvement of £4OOO. Also there was a remarkable
rise in the interest on investments, the receipts under
this head being £79,071, or £BOOO more than they were in
1895. This means a total improvement of £15,000 in the
income as compared with last year. The interest on in-
vested funds, which sank to 471 per cent, in ’95, rose
again in 1896 to 4*91 per cent., and the working expenses
dropped about ten shillings per cent. Also one or two
other agreeable things happened. The amount added to
the funds (£102,168) was £7OOO more than last year’s
saving, and the funds now stand at £1,662,391. Also the
reserve for depreciation has been raised from £35,000 to
£70,000, which is an eminently wise precaution. Taking
everything into account, the National Mutual has done
unusually well in every respect.
The assets leave no very great room for com-
plaint. Accrued interest represents about £14,000, and,
as nothing at all is said about interest in arrear, it is pre-
sumably lumped in with the “accrued.” In that case,
the amount seems moderate enough, though the details
might be a little clearer, and it would appear that the
assets are sufficiently sound and paying up the
interest with that regularity which holds forth
promise of the principal being all right. The
National Mutual has apparently sold out its hold-
ing of Government securities and is going in chiefly
for mortgages, which may or may not be wise policy, ft
has £70,000 invested in Victorian building society shares —
the name of the society or societies, as usual, doesn’t
appear, and the amount is steadily increasing. Pre-
sufnably, however, the £70,000 isn’t in any of the
more awful institutions. Also “ deferred instalments
of annual premiums in force ” still appears as an
asset to the amount of £45,000, and further debate as to
the value of this item is hereby held over. At all events,
the large increase in the business done, the improved
rate of interest realised, the reduction of expenses, and
the handsome addition made to the reserve fund are all
signs of prosperity and good management.
* * * *
Melbourne Perpetual Trustees and Executors
Association, whose balance-sheet comes to light some-
what late in the day, has a profit of £B5 for the year
ended 30th September, equal to 6s. percent, per annum.
Last year there was a loss of £197, and the previous year
a loss of £204, so things are not quite so bad as they were.
Still, they are bad enough. The reserve-fund of £lO,OOO
vanishes suddenly ; £3777 is written off to cover depreci-
ation, and £6223 carried to a contingency account in case
of more depreciation coming to light. The co. has £38,000
of its own cash invested (610,000 in State securities, and
the rest mostly in mortgages), and assuming this yields
3J4 per cent, all round—a very moderate estimate indeed
—it would seem that the Trustee business is an actual
loss of about £1250. Under these circumstances it would
surely be better to give up the Trustee business altogether
or else amalgamate. Things are doubtless improving in
Melbourne, but it may easily take 10 or 15 years before
that city is again in a position to keep its five trust com-
panies going profitably, and the dead horse some of them
are working off will be in a dreadful state of decomposi-
tion by then. Dead horse doesn’t keep well in this hot
climate.
* * * *
That big trading concern, James M‘Ewan and
Co., Limited, publishes a balance-sheet with a lot of thrill
in it. Total loss for the year over £12,000. Business
didn’t cover working expenses and bad debts, and conse-
quently debenture interest is all dead loss. Accumulated
shortage in the assets £117,000, equal to 30 per cent, of
the capital; £lOO,OOO of capital to be written off as lost.
No dividend.
* * * »
Melb. Storage Co. states that it has made a loss
of * 270 for the half-year, and puts in the same gladsome
footnote as in last balance-sheet to the effect that the
freehold property niust be regarded as a liability rather
than an asset. Said property is set down at a book-value
of £103,000 less a mortgage of £53,000, so that it is a
nominal asset of £55,000. As the footnote evidently
means that it isn’t now worth the mortgage money, it
apparently follows that this nominal asset is at present
purely bogus, and in that case it would appear by the
figures that the co. is insolvent, its paid-up capital and
reserves not being enough to cover the deficit. The Melb.
Storage Co, seems to have almost the only absolutely
candid and truthful directorate in this country just now.
* * * *
At the end of December there were 26,530 tons
of American grain and 17,960 tons of American and Chinese
flour afloat for Sydney alone. Yet the present harvest is
an abundant one, and the population has gone on the
land so much more than usual that N.S. Wales, for the
first time since it began to have agricultural statistics, can
fully supply its own requirements. With nearly two
months’ extra supply being dumped into the province,
saying nothing of possible future shipments, some inter-
esting results should ensue.

In 1889, auction sales of real estate in Sydney
ran to £7,000,000; 1890, £1,250,000; 1891, £1,075,000 ;
1892, £1,500,000; 1893, £950,000; 1894, £700,000 ; 1895,
£560,000 ; 1896, £450,000. What do Sydney auctioneers
live upon these times ?
* • * *
Bennet and Woolcock, Limited (Melb.), a large
concern in the meat, wool, and tallow line, states that its
loss for last year is £8452. The capital is £40,000 and
reserve only £lOOO, so this means a-fifth of the entire co.
vanished in one year, as a sort of Xmas box for share-
holders. Further, two of the assets which represent the
alleged £40,000 capital are net loss up to date £7829, and
goodwill, £11,500. Naturally £11,500 is about a fair thing
for the goodwill of a concern which is £7829 to the bad.
* * * «
The “coal ring” whose existence has been so
often denied, was evidently a pretty solid reality a few
years ago. In 1889 the average price of coal at New-
castle (N.S. W.) was Bs. lid. per ton, and the lowest price
at which the Victorian Government could obtain house
coal by tender was 265. 6d. In 1894 the price at New-
castle had gone down to 6s. 4d., a drop of 2s. 7d. per ton ;
but the price at which it was offered to the Vic. Govern-
ment was 13s. Id., a decline of no less than 13s. sd. Con-
sequently, the Treasurer reckons that the “ ring,” until it
was forced down by the pressure of local competition,
“ had ” Victoria for 10s. 6d. per ton, and would probably
have it again for the same amount if the Victorian mines
could be closed up. In those good old days the ring
decided which of its members should get a certain tender
and at what price, and, as no other member competed
against him, he naturally got it. The old coffin-ship
which wheezed with its big coal cargo along the coast
used to earn its oats those times. Now it makes little
more than engine-grease and expenses.

Cultivation of tobacco in Queensland has ceased
in 16 out of 37 districts growing tobacco in ’95, and the
tobacco area has declined from 2141 acres to 1523 acres.
Notwithstanding decreased area cultivated, cured leaf
produced from 1523 acres in 1895-96 was 1,507,9501 b., or
Bcwt. 3qr. to the acre, as against 1,620,7321 b., or 6cwt. 3qr.
to the acre from 2141 acres in 1894-5.

* * * *
Out of <£95,118 received in S.A. as income-tax
during 1896, £12,412 was contributed by 107 persons earn-
ing each over £2200 annually from personal exertion, and
£23,064 from 70 persons who derive more than £2200 a
vear each from property. Persons receiving less than
£3OO a year yield £17,926, and the total number of
income-taxpayers is 14,012. The exemption in 1896 was
£125, and this has been raised to £lso—a change which
will involve a loss of £7OOO a year.

Brown’s Creek mine (N.S.W.) is a scene of

modern mill is idle. The pump only is working to keep

his crowd, must have gone down for at least £30,000 over
this deal. Whispered now that the mill was got by the
issue of 10 per cent, debentures, and that the French
money-lenders have got the mine and mill to repre-
sent principal and interest.

The claim is too small, and in the wrong place to boom.

Lady Burdett Coutts : “ There is 15in. of very
good stone going down at 325 ft. level, which is fully 3oz.
stone, and might go 6or 7oz. It is so full of mineral
that I cannot see what gold it carries, and it is the best I
ever saw in the mine.” This is a N.S.W. mine report. If

down to Chris-hic-mus !
» * * *
The North has now the most attention from the
touts on the B.H. field. Its shaft, from which a long
western crosscut has been unsuccessfully put in, with the
object of picking up the Junction and Junction North
body, is declared to be too centrally situated for the pur-
pose, and the exploration is now being carried on from the
Junction North’s most northerly workings. Up to within
a few feet of the North boundary good sulphide ore has
been carried ; and there seems to be every probability
that it will be followed into the North’s ground.
The course of the lode and its value are, how-
ever, most uncertain hereabout : if it extend into the
North it will be plain that a huge displacement of the lode
has occurred, and the elbow theory to account for the
rich deposit in the Junction will be sustained. But in the
region of such disturbances persistent bodies of rich ore
are not to relied upon. Even in the Junction and
Junction North the lode—or, more strictly speak-
ing, bunch —cannot be relied upon further than
the eye can see. Block 10 has such another
bunch (only of very rich kaolin), and it disap-
peared as dramatically as it “ made.” On the whole,
therefore, it will not be a very reassuring thing for the
Junction and Junction North if the lode does turn back
into the North ; while, as for the Junction Consolidated,
Silver Hill Junction and that group, it will be a very un-
pleasant development indeed.
Even if the North does get it, the rapid under-
lay to the west (out of the North ground, that is) must be
kept in mind. On the whole, then, and notwithstanding
the sharp advance in Norths, the present writer would
prefer to tip the winner of next Melb. Cup than, upon
present knowledge, to advise investment in any particular
mine of the Junction-North-Consolidated group. One
thing, though : nothing that this exploratory work dis-
closes ought to advance either Junction or Junction North
shares; for their values have been determined in a market
which all along has pooh-poohed the idea of the rich ore
passing back into the North.
* * • •
The Junction North has entered into contracts
for 1897 for the supply of 500 tons of high-grade ore
weekly ; and there are also arrangements for the supply
of lower-grade stuff. The haulage of ore is now simplified
through improvements in the shaft.

shares began 1895 at Is. 4d. ; closed It9l at 605., touching
89s. between times. Junction North, unsaleable at the
opening of 1895, closed 1896 at 305., and touched 365. in
Aug. Block 10, opening at 55., went to 865., and closed
at 635. 6d. The least variation was in B.H. Proprietaries,
which opened 1895 at 365. 6d., went to 635. in Feb., 1896,
and closed the year at 50s. But, then, the B.H. Proprie-
tary is not exclusively a sulphide-mine.

The output of ore for local and seaboard smelt-
ing, local concentrating, and export abroad from the
Broken Hill mines now averages 20,000 tons a week. Of

* * * *
That almost infallible mining authority, the
Sabbath tipster, says: “It is much to be deplortd that
Sydney has not entered into the W.A. mining business to
any great extent. Adelaide absorbs most of the good
things, but there is no reason why we should not also be
participators of some of the richness which will come
eastward from Kalgoorlie and Menzies. People who are
not eaten up with pessimisim, and who are really in a
position to judge, from personal observation, do not hesi-
tate to state that the output of W.A. goldfields will be
enormously increased during the current year.” But
that was exactly what was said this time last year, and
the year before. Adelaide has three or four of the good
things—there are not many altogether. It has also more
than 20 “cats” to one g'old-mine, and the stench there-
from is so bad that the chief business this year will be the
liquidator’s burial service. Sydney has kept fairly out of
the business, and little wonder. Everything it touched,
with one solitary exception—and many thousands have
been sent W.A.-wards—has proved a hopeless failure.
The list is a fairly long one, too.
* * * *
Speight, ex-Vic. Chief Railway Commissioner,
is in Melb. with a scheme for running light tramways
around the mines to enable low-grade ores to be taken
down the Coolgardie line for treatment where there is
plenty of fuel and water. The thing reads well—like
every prospectus. Probably there are a few mines in the
country that would pay worked on this system, but the
“ white quartz” mines of Westralia —and they are mostly
that —are either rich enough to stand all the natural con-
ditions of mining in the country, or are too poor to pay
under any conditions. There was more gold in a ton of
ore from the rich streak in Bayley’s reef than in 1000 tons
of the almost barren quartz beside it. So with the Lon-
donderry, Wealth of Nations, and a score of other W.A.
mines which have batteries but no payable ore to crush.
* * * *
Modest Maryanski, having left for London, is
escaping the hostile criticism thrown at him in reference
to the discovery of tellurium at Kalgoorlie. The heavy
telluride talk had ceased altogether when M.M. got out
there, and he was evidently under the impression his
was the first find, whereas tellurium had been found many
months before, in Block 45 and elsewhere. What has
become of all the other rich telluride finds is quite
another question. M.M.’s declaration that the presence
of tellurium is proof of the permanency and rich-
ness of mines, etc., rather shows him not the man he
is cracked up to be. The world’s experience is in the
opposite direction. There are only one or two places in
the world where gold is worked in the form of tellurium,
though its occasional presence has been observed in a
great many mines. Already the experience of Kalgoorlie
has apparently been sufficient to show that if the field
had to depend on the gold in its telluride ores it would be
a case of—Uordelpit !
* * * *
Frazer’s mine, Southern Cross, W.A., 900 tons,
5600 z. Quite good enough. Many hundred times have
the Southern Cross mines been thrown at the public as
argument in favor of low-grade mining in that waterless
waste, of which Southern Cross is an average sample, or
a sample not much above the average. Yet the explana-
tion is simple enough. The lake at this camp is about
enough to keep two or three mines running by pumping.
There are only two or three mines running. Had they
found more mines, or had the public been fool enough to
work lizard’ blocks the water would be scarce all round
as it is m other places.
* * * •
■ Disowned. The public of Perth are warned
that the Mt. Malcolm Proprietary mine (W.A.) is often
confounded with a Mt. Malcolm mine whose shares are
about a penny. It has no connection with it, and the
public are asked to see that they get the right brand.
The Proprietary is not without troubles of a financial
kind. Some one has cleared with the ready cash. Still
the nune is said to be all right, which is more than can be
said of the Sydney-owned Mt. Malcolm.
* * * *
The beginning of the end. Hit or Miss South
nune, W.A. First call, 67,000 shares forfeited ; no funds
in hand, and the chairman appealed to the meeting, held
in Brisbane this week, to pay up or the mine was liable to
be jumped after the 16th, when the exemption expired.
It was decided to appeal to the paid-up holders to come
to the rescue and keep the thing alive. Better let it die,
considering the collapsing condition of the Hit or Miss
mine itself, on the strength of which the South was
floated. Lady Lamirigton -another Brisbane-owned W.A.
mine in the same trouble; 18,000 shares forfeited. The
funds in the bank will allow the fun to go on a little
longer. There will be more W.A. mines floundered than
floated in ’97.
* ♦ * *
Published in Perth that the Murchison goldfield
has now 345 head of stampers at work, that the yield this
year will exceed last by r 35,0000 z., and that, after a longer
trial than Coolgardie, the field is beginning to reward
those who retained their faith in it. Three to four years
is a long time to wait for the beginning of a reward. The
alleged 345 stamps should crush about 1000 tons of ore a
day, which, at an oz. to the ton, the average of Charters
Towers, will exceed its output. Yet the Towers this year
put out as much gold Westralia altogether.
Meeting of the Golden Lode syndicate recently
held at Kanowna. W.A. : “The chairman reported they
were convinced that the gold in the dump did not come
from the lode, but had been placed there by some un-
known means ; therefore, the new find, of which so much
had been said, did not exist.” Reefers’ Eureka, Kal-
goorlie : “ The directors have received information that
the two zones of country of great width, and reported to
be worth an oz. to the ton all through, did not carry any
gold, and the stone sent from the mine had been salted.
This had been confirmed by an examination made by Mr.
Maitland.”

For the eleven months ending November the
gold-yield for Queensland was 570,1650 z. The Westralian
Premier plumed himself the other day on an output of
300,0000 z., or about half-a-year's yield for Queensland.
For the eleven months the Towers conies first with
207,5580 z. ; Mt. Morgan, 138,4470 z. ; Crovdon, 80,4420 z. ;
Gympie, 64,4430 z. ; and the Etheridge, 25,5230 z. No less
than 30 small cy'anide plants have been recently erected
on the Towers for treatment of tailings. Public atten-
tion is at present centred on the Deep Levels, which
recently cut the reef in the cross-cut at 2340 ft., from 2ft.
6in. to 3ft. thick, with good-looking stone.

COOLALIA
CAMEO
A real boom cyclone struck the sandy
township of Nevertire —near Gehenna (N.S.W.)
the other day, and the single enthusiastic
resident who spoke of Nevertire as a rising
place found it falling simultaneously about
him in all directions. Churches and liquor
stores fled across the dusty plains together
in brotherhood and left their fragments spread
over a vast area, and there is a legend of a
J.P. who flew 15 miles on a sheet of galvanised-
iron and landed on the top of a telegraph-
post, whence he sent a wire to Premier Reid
announcing the disaster and stating that the
increasing difficulty in finding people to shout
drinks for the honorary magistracy was the lion
CROWDED OUT.
“Do you think my voice filled the room?”
“By the way the people came on the verandah, I should say it overcrowded it.”
rn the path of federation. With this exception,
the cyclone doesn’t appear to have done anything
to be particularly proud of—unless it was hitting
Nevertire in the first instance. Many other
cyclones have fired round Australia at intervals,
but none of them ever succeeded in shooting a
township before—probably because Australian
townships are so scarce ahd lonesome that it takes
a great deal of practice to go anywhere near them.
When this expansive country gets thickly popu-
lated the cyclone will probably insist on having
every Sunday out, as it were.
* * * *
The unreasoning perversity with which
Providence wantonly destroys the earthly mansion
of the collection-plate and the stentorian iron bell
was sadly illustrated at Nevertire during the
tornado. Ihe two churches m that now much-
disseminated township were carried away bodily
by the wind and distributed over the adjacent
landscape in the form of fire-lighters. Of the
two hotels one was partially wrecked, but in the
case of the other it is melancholy to read that
“ the bar portion of the hotel was not touched.”
The need of a good, reliable arrangement by
which the churches will always remain unscathed
while the wicked pub. is blown to matchwood is
becoming more apparent every day.
* * * *
A fearsome case has just struck Sydney in
the form of a German libel-suit. One Roth last
week proceeded against Arno Thierman on a
charge of sending him a post-card on which was
written in staggerful characters the words “ hoch-
stappler and betreuger.” These terms, com-
plainant meant “arch swindler and
thief, impostor, cheat, deceiver,” and other
calumny. Large numbers of German importers
(they all seem to be importers) gave evidence as to
the awful character-blasting strength of hoch-
stappler and betreuger, and defendant was com-
mitted for trial. Evidently hochstappler and
betreuger are words of many ton objurgation in
the Vaterland.
* * * *
The first woman ever arrested in Australia
for being tight in knickerbockers was fined the
other day at a Sydney police-court.

A voice from the far West. Out by the
Dawson a dead man was reported with two horses,
presumably his property, feeding close by. The
usual policeman went out and disposed of the
remains and reported : “I buried the remains,
yer Washup, and brought in the horses.” “ What
sort of man do you think he was ? ” “He was a
rejoosed gentleman, yer Washup, and accustomed
to luxuries. He had a fork in his swag, yer
Washup.”

THUD!
“It is reported from the neighborhood of Dan-
daloo that a new arrival, fired, it is supposed, by
Lord Hampden's advice to young men, procured a
horse and at once proceeded to settle on the land. ”
Sample Westralian summer-
season joke. Button-holing your
dearest friend, you say: “Oh, by the
way, you know Jenkins, of the Brown
Hill?” “Yes, what about him?”
“ Why, he has had the body of his
wife in his tent for the last month,
and neither police nor magistrate
can get him to remove it.” “Good
Heavens, what’s his reason?”
“ Well she’s not dead, you know.”
After that the willy-willy.
* * * *
TheWesleyans, Presbyterians,
and other groaners who object to
Commandant Booth’s proposed mis-
sion to the South Sea islands, sim-
ply show the operation of the law of
seif-preservation. Booth’s glaring
jumble of good and bad, wisdom
and folly, ignorance and know-
ledge, heaven and hell, is bound to
lift the niggers off their feet. The
big drum itself will knock the prosy,
common, sleek parson right out of
the field.
* * * *
Hamilton (Vic.) Borough
Council lately refused the prayer of
307 ratepayers to be allowed to hear
a band play sacred music in the
public gardens on Sunday evening.
Hamilton is a place where loose-
haired females with no teeth bang
tambourines on Sunday to the
tune of “When Johnny Comes
Marching Home Again,” and fish-
eyed men with triangular skulls flop
imprecations at the Deity to a cor-
net accompaniment of “ The Man
that Broke the Bank at Monte
Carlo ” ; but the Council isn’t going
to be immoral enough to let Sabbath
ears be polluted by Haydn and
Beethoven.
Second verse of the first “ war song ” on a
hymn sheet distributed by Maoriland Cold Tea
Party, just before the poll, on the drink question—
Whatever thy danger,
Take heed and beware,
But turn not thy back
For no armor is there.
The prohibitionist’s lack of protection against
Maoriland kickers goes to show that Ironsand
Smith has ample reason for his determination to
melt Taranaki into pig-iron or die in the attempt.
“ If it is not a dirty business,” yelled the
Temperance orator, “ why do you license it ? You
don’t license your butchers or bakers or grocers or
drapers.” Small voice from the back seat: “ But
you do license yer auctioneers.” And the Cold-
Tea man grew very red.

Holdar M. : “The tale of the Chinese
alleged convert and his instructress has been told
before-whilst deadly indifference prevails it can-
not be too often repeated. The other after-
noon, in a Sydney suburb, the yellow
man and his dear Christian tnends
went out on a muggy - muggy
razzle. Two wide circles, thickly
packed with Chow, white girl and
Mongrel, conducted operations to
an atrocious chant of “A-hunting
we will go.” The principles of the
pastime baffled the writer’s grasp,
but the effects were sufficiently ap-
parent. Teacher and pupil grabbed
each other with a great grab, and
suddenly bouncing round in oppo-
site directions embraced some more
brother and sister with fervent con-
fusion. A stiff north-easter dis-
playing quantities of female under-
wear heightened the spectacular
value of the romp, and the male
Klistian’s orbs fairly twinkled with
appreciation. White women, beside
their Celestial consorts, sat around
suckling their piebald progeny,
while envious onlookers of the
‘ push ’ variety were kept at a dis-
creet distance, and seemed to feel
the position acutely. One plump
and frolicsome maiden presently
collapsed ignominiously in the ring’s
centre amidst the coily caehinna-
tions of the Yellowskin, several of
whom bounded to the rescue of
their flounce-entangled Missee. It
became time to quit the hybrid
horror. ”
Melb. Women’s Hospital pharisai-
cally bleats that its staff, in proportion to
the patients treated, is larger than that of
any other similar charity in Australasia. As
it admittedly works its nurses over 12$ hours
per day, and gives them only half a day off per
week, it is, on the face of it, a sweater and a
whited sepulchre, and, if its staff are best off,
what must the condition of the Florence Night-
ingales of other hospitals be like ?

LEILA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The nodding plumes steal slowly by,
Fair women turn their heads aside—
And yet the purest there must die
As poor love Leila died.
In town, a boy who never knew
Of better love than this
Is mourning Leila’s eyes of blue,
And lone for Leila’s kiss.
A horseman on the burning plains
A hundred miles north west
Bends gently o’er his bridle-reins
And prays for Leila : “Rest.”
A man who buried all his dreams
Of Love long years ago,
Has dropped one other tear where gleams
Love-Leila’s breast of snow.
All virtuous the world appears—
But those who turn aside
May never win such honest tears
As fell when Leila died.
GLENROWAN.
LIFE.
The guests at Life’s carousal one by one
Steal silent out before the wine is done,
But others entering take their chairs, and so
The feast ne’er flags howe’er they come and go.
Brisbane.
ARTHUR A. D. BAYLDON.

THOMAS P. PURVES, A.1.A., General Manager,


Publichouse acknowledged to be “the leavings
from the glasses. Every barman in every land
knows, or ought to know, that it’s a common cus-
tom of the trade ”to empty all the drainings
ostensibly into a sink, but really into a cask. They
all go in together—brandy, whisky, rum, gin,
beer, shandygaff, wine—everything takes its
chance. The cask is a lottery, for no one knows
how the contents will turn out; it depends on
which drink predominates and oh the weather and
the rats and other things. But everything comes
in handy for blending purposes, and many strange
accidental drinks come out of that cask. Mostly
they are awful or worse, but now and then it pans
out for a wonderful liquor, the recipe of which is
always lost, for no one ever knows how much
whisky and how much gin and rat and stale beer
is in the compound, and how stale the beer is, or
whether the cigar-ash which got into it was Cuban
or Manilla or Mexican. The cask in the cellar is
the most absolutely uncertain gamble on record,
and the drinker unconsciously bets his stomach on
it, and mostly loses.

* » * *
Melbourne is full of dead churches. They
are of all denominations except the Catholic ; it
is one of the cast-iron principles of the astute
Roman never to run in debt or mortgage the
sacred fane in any way, and therefore his is the
only tabernacle that isn’t going broke. But the
other sects are in a bad way. Bishop Goe wants
to abandon St. James’s. Torrance endeavors to
galvanise the half-dead St. John’s, but John’s is
evidently past saving. The Presbyterian Chalmers
Church, and the Ramsey Church, Collins-street,
botli big places with towering spires, have knocked
under. John Knox Church, Swanston-street, has
been sold cheap to a rival sect, and St. Andrew’s
is about as dead as it can be. Wesley Church is
a horrid white elephant. The Independents have
slaughtered two churches—that large one in
Lonsdale-street East, now a tin-factory, and the
still larger and finer Eastern Hill church. “ This
desirable tabernacle to let cheap ” will soon be a
common, everyday advt. in the city of Smellboom.
« • * *
The Melb. police are on the track of a
novel sort of Jack the Ripper. His passion is to
go about kicking women, by night. It is recorded
that he never kicks the same woman more than
once.
* * * «
Melbourne is going to be purified on Social
Gimlet lines. All the seats in dark corners of the
parks and gardens are to be removed and placed
in the light, so that the social purists can get a
better view. The social purity crowd consists
largely of ladies, some of them quite young ladies,
and one of their duties is to go round the gardens
after dark, and, in the words of a lady-member,
“see sights which would make the worst woman
blush.” If they didn’t belong to the Society for
the Prevention of Immorality they couldn’t go
and see those things and be respectable ; being
members they can. And thus the girl-graduates
learn quite a lot of undraped facts which they
wou'd otherwise have to find out by reading Zola.
In Hobart,
recently, two
workmen were
coming home
late and saw the
roof of a mansion
on fire. One
climbed up the
verandah and
started to beat

while the other

One of Hobart’s
principal society
dames came
down in her
dressing- gown
and realised the
situation ; like-
wise she found
that the clothes
of the man on
the roof were
completely ruin-
ed. In a burst
of enthusiasm
and thanks she
offered them a
shilling between
them after the
fire had been
thoroughly ex-
tinguished. The
next Hobart
mansion that
takes fire will
probably be al-
lowed to blaze,
so far as the
local toiler is
concerned.

At Wellington (M.L.) election, the other
day, a large, frowsy girl with reddish hair walked
up to the returning-officer with a note from that
free and independent elector, her mother. It
read : “ I ain’t got no boots, so can’t come to the
pole. Please let Sally vote forme.” It was ex-
plained that the Act, unfortunately, wasn’t built
that way, so the bootless parent’s suffrage was
lost.

A would-be contributor lately called on
the proprietor of an Australian magazine to learn
the fate of a 20-page MS. which he had blasted
out of his inner soul after three weeks’ labori-
ous thought. “Yes,” said the proprietor, “I
like your story very much and will put it in.”
“And about payment?” timidly insinuated the
author. “Why, you don’t expect to be paid
surely. Most of my contributors are so glad to
THE AUSTRALIAN POINT OF VIEW .
SCHOOLMASTER (to yeoyraphy class) : “ What is Manchester famous for?”
YOUNG AUSTRALIAN : “ W’y, ain’t that where we won the second Test-match?”
get into print that they bring me illustrations
along with their stuff.” The author looked
dubious, and there was a pause. Then the pro-
prietor rubbed his chin and said slowly : “I will
give you £1 on publication.” The writer sadly
shook his head. Another pause, and the proprie-
tor, with sudden animation, leaned across his desk
and said in a triumphant whisper: “See here,
I’ll buy it for ss. cash down.” And yet people
wonder why authors take to drink. It is the
cheapest thing an author can take to.

bogus charity of Christmas grub. Good people

The case of undertaker Jones, who was
recently made a clergyman in Melbourne and thus
qualified to read the burial service over the firm’s
“ stiffs,” has raised a big commotion. In Victoria
any man can become a legally - recognised
parson if 40 persons sign a petition that they
want him appointed to attend to their spiritual
wants. Then he is entitled to marry people
(which doesn’t matter much so long as the knot
holds good), and to bury them (which doesn’t
matter at all as the deceased takes no interest in
the matter); also, the 40 casual signatures qualify
him to know all about Genesis, and the material
perdition, and Jonah, and St. John at Pat-
mos, and the mark of the Beast, and similar
mysteries. In fact, it seems that 40 people who
know nothing at all can, by signing a piece of
paper, fill a man who knows next to nothing with
a comprehensive knowledge of everything. Jones,
it is reported, has no tabernacle and no congrega-
tion, and some of the people who signed his bit of
paper had the haziest idea what it was about, and
the address of the alleged kirk seems to be a piece
of waste ground infested with kerosene-tins and
dogs, and Jones calls himself the Reformed Church
of England—which is regarded as a very large
order indeed. Genuine reverends with the name
blown in the bottle rather appear to regard Jones
and his kind as a burlesque and a carnal grab-
bing at the fees and perquisites. But then the
average parson is also a good deal of a burlesque,
and his monkey-shave makes it practically im-
possible to take him seriously.
* * * *
The bones of the original Superfluous
Woman were lately found near Plimmerton
(M.L.). She was lying in the bush in company
with a male person, also deceased. Each had the
perforation of a bullet in the skull, so the case is
set down as a romantic suicide. Along with the
remains were two revolvers, a bottle, and a purse
containing £7 and a memo., which set forth that
the money was for burial expenses ; also the male
skeleton was accompanied by a travelling bag
containing a Wellington Times dated October,
’94. Some people set down the tragedy to the
dreariness of the leading article, but this is mere
conjecture. No one knows anything of two people
haying gone amissing about that period, therefore
it is assumed that the lady was the real Super-
fluous Woman, and being superfluous her absence
wasn’t noticed. The police report says that
the pair had been “considerably disturbed by
cattle,” so their troubled career seemingly con-
tinued to be troubled even after death. * They
were worried in their lives, and harassed by cows
when they were dead, and there was no rest any-
where.

Kjonson lives in a Sydney suburb and is
three weeks back in his rent. He merely “ lodges.”
He has been keeping up military “ stand-off ” with
his Dragon ess by the Old Peninsular style of
“masterly inactivity.” But this failed. The
Dragoness—a thin, little woman worth a dozen
giants as “ a garrison ” —caught him, so to speak,
en enfilade. “When are you going to, &c., Mr.
Kjonson ? ” “ Really, madam, you do not, surely,
expect any rent from me this week ? ” “ And why
not ?” _ (This with awful meaning.) “ Why, this
week is Self-Denial Week, you know.” “Yes,
but what’s that got to do with it?” “ Well, I'm
denying myself by not paying you your rent ! ”
“ But / lose by that ? ” “My dear lady, somebody
must lose by any self-denying action ” —and,
before the Dragoness had time to collect her wits,
Kjonson was 20 yards up street. But when he
got home !

There is no offence under the Master and Servants’
Act which renders a servant liable to imprisonment, and
the master to fine only. But it is an offence punishable
by fine, and, in default of sufficient distress, by im-
prisonment, if a servant, who contracts to serve, does
not enter on the service according to his agreement, yet
no provision is made for the master who breaks his con-
tract. In such a case the servant would have only
a civil action for damages for the breach —the master
isn’t even liable to fine. A servant is also liable to fine
or imprisonment for absenting himself without leave—a
master cannot commit the offence.


A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, Jan. 5, 1897.
Mr Dear Myee—
The high and mighty residents of Bowral and
Moss Vale have been celebrating the holy Xmas
season by having a little “ Mountain Misery on
their own account in the matter of balls. Ihe
jealousy existing between Caesar and Pompey, or
between Melb. and Sydney, is as nothing to the
wild envy that surges up between the Nicest of
the two adjacent villages. So when the Moss
Vale Tennis Club gave its annual ball on New
Year’s eve the expected Bowralites refused to be
dazzled by the glare and electric-lights in Ben-
nett’s Hall, and stayed away in large crowds from
the festivity, which was graced only by local
celebrities and some Sydney visitors. Govt. House
was also absent, having the Freeman Thomases
and other guests to entertain at Sutton Forest.
Next night the Bowral dance happened, and the
offended dwellers in the Vale retaliated by not
appearing thereat, but as liberal contingents
arrived from Mittagong and the regions lying
round about Burradoo and Bong Bong, there
would have been hardly room on the floor for
another set of ten light fantastic toes.
Moss Vale dance was brightened by a plump
and , pleasing matron from town who, attired in
the latest ball-room notion —embroidered net,
white on black—worked through a long programme
with the ardour of just-out 18 ; also a tall, local
youth, with an English manner attached, whose
collar was so lofty that it made him look as if he d
come out without his head. The hon. sec. waltzed
gracefully in white ducks.
Bowral decorated its School of Arts for the
festive occasion—every country town possesses one
of these useful institutions, so named because it
isn’t a school and there are no arts about it except
those of the ball-room angel—and entered into the
spirit of the thing by tying tennis-rackets, nets,
and balls on the walls, and hiring a mild young
man from a far distance to thump the piano loudly
enough to be heard above the riotous romping of
kitchen lancers. A tall married goddess in white
satin looked about as nice as anybody there,
though the hon. sec. and her many sisters and
cousins kept up with the beauty procesh most
successfully. A charmer from Windsor wore white
adorned with poppies.
The girls were a marked feature of both these
social convulsions ; their looks were quite up to
the average, and their incomes much beyond thau
mark. The whole region ought to be a happy
hunting-ground for the ambitious society man, as
the loud warbling of the oof-bird can be heard on
all sides, and local belles are divided into three
classes well-off, rich, and bloatedly - affluent.
With these inducements it is not to be wondered
at that the dances in this favored district are like
the new style of drama where all the action
happens “off,” and towards the end of the even-
ing or beginning of next morn resolve themselves
into sitting-out contests. Meanwhile a wondering
and sleepy musician gazes at three or four revolv-
ing coupies in a deserted room ; the chaperons
slumber around the walls, and there is “standing-
room ” only abound the §eats in the adiacent
grounds. <
Another theatrical row is darkening the atmos-
phere. This time it is two variety artists, who are
not playing in each other’s yard. R. G. Knowles
claims that dreary gag, “ I’ve got another hat,” as
his own and as used by him on a London audience
for many nights with surprising effect, and, now
that Crackles is trying the patience of Her
Majesty’s patrons with it every evening, the
original author is cut out of his rights. It’s an ill
wind that blows nobody any good, however, and,
now that it has become stale at the panto., the
original owner will not be able to spring it on a
long-suffering audience on the other side of Pitt-st.
At the Palace on Saturday evening H. Lee burst
forth in the gorgeous trappings of that other
lightning-change artist with a European reputa-
tion, Willie Hohenzollern. The audience was
high-toned and gave a London touch to the Pitt-
st. hall of Oriental splendor by being as fashion-
able in the stalls as up topside in the circle. All
the garments worn by Lee in his various imper-
sonations are most accurate, and alleged to be
copied, at vast expense, from waist measurements
and collar patterns supplied him by his “ great
men’s” tailors—barring the doublet-and-hose
artist of the Immortal Bard’s, whose tailor s pat-
tern-book shows measurements which can’t be dis-
entangled satisfactorily by experts from Bacon’s,
so William’s figure is treated as per tombstone
ideals.
To help Lee effect his numerous changes, five
men are stationed behind the scenes, and rushing
at Henry simultaneously, when he comes off the
stage, one grabs the artist’s wig while another
tears wildly at his waistcoat ; a third menial
violently removes his boots, and two stand by
with fresh garments which are hurled on him in
hot haste, so that when the wild, weird music
ceases to interlude and the curtain rises, the poor
man is quite breathless, but disguises the fact
from the audience by speaking slowly. If the
“ change ” could be done in view of the front of
the house, the turn would go with more rush and
bustle and less sepulchral solemnity. It is im-
mensely clever, but wants brightening badly. Kip.
takes best of all, because he is done in a lighter
vein, and also because he is the only one in the
collection who has a local interest—having once
spent two whole days in Australia.
Again, encore, once more, yet another time is
the same old discussion raised whether or not a
young woman should help to swell the family
income by earning some coin on her own account,
or sit at home darning her father’s socks and sew-
ing on his buttons until an eligible man happens
along and Pa hands her over to him with a sigh
of relief at having got another girl off his hands.
The present aspect of the discussion is fashioned
by the new regulation of the Civil Service Board,
which is about to admit a flood of feminine short-
hand writers and typists into the Govt, service.
A daily paper correspondent throws rather a new
side-light on domestic matters by asserting that
the Australian father, as a rule, jibs at supporting
his grown-up daughters, and if one of them asks
him to foot her dressmaker’s last little l,;n
in the habit of remarking that that way madness
lies, or how sharper than a toothless tank it is
to have a serpent’s child, or words to that effect,
and slamming the front door loudly on himself as
he rushes out into the wide world away from the
filial demand for cash. This is practically the
case unless the old man is a millionaire, and the
only remedy I can suggest is that every g‘ri
ought to cake great care to be born into a
family wherein she can have a good time all her
single life at somebody else’s expense.
“ The Alabama (U.S.) Legislature is preparing
a bill prohibiting women from wearing bloomers,
shirt-waists, or any article of man s apparel.
News Item. In view of the possibility that ttus
kind of legislation may spread to Australia I have
prepared the following piece of verse :
Two Parramatta “first families” were more
closely united by matrimonial bonds last week,
when Miss Esther Weston, daughter of the late
F. Weston, was legally tied to a son of the late
Charles S. Oakes, by Rev. Gunther, at St. John’s.
Other unions during the week, which was a busy
one hymeneally, were Miss Nellie Selfe and Mr.
Jack Williamson, at Neutral Bay—-an affair with
any amount of thrills and frills in the way of
decorations and garments ; and Miss Ethel Lloyd
and Mr. Leslie Wade, of Ashfield, at Parramatta.
The time is ripe to suppress with a remorseless
hand the regulation wedding-report. “ An ex-
tremely pretty wedding” is always celebrated;
the church is always decorated by “ girl friends of
the bride ; ” the bride always enters “ the sacred
edifice upon the arm of her father by whom she
was subsequently given away.” If the church
were decorated by the “ contracting parties,” or
the father were to enter upon the corn of one of
the “ girl friends,” or if the best man were to be
supported by the bride, it would be an exciting
variation upon the hackneyed story. Then the
“ profusion of flowers and ferns ” erected by the
“girl friends ” might be allowed for once to wobble
tipsily (these vegetables always do) from pew
ends, and tickle the spectators’ necks and heads
as they wilted in the stuffy interior. Further, it
would be a refreshing change if a very ordinary
wedding would happen once in a while ; and what
is the matter with having the church decorated
occasionally by some middle-aged ladies who are
with great difficulty smothering their animosity
for the bride, instead of the regulation “ girl
friends.”
A wedding-gift boom is sometimes enthusiasti-
cally worked up around an impending marriage,
as in the case of a damsel from the backblocks
who came to a rich relation’s in Sydney to have a
send-off worthy of a niece of Nice families. The
energetic aunt issued invitations, and spread such
highly-varnished reports of the gilded saloons in
which the dear bride’s intended habitually moved
that the wedding-guests went without butter for
a week rather than give a shabby donation to
stand out conspicuously among costly offerings on
the wedding-morn. So the happy couple raked in
a whole stock-in-trade of platedware, and, among
other superfluities, a visitors’ book in red morocco,
to lie in the hall attached to the gilded saloon.
And the same turned out to be, in reality, a plain,
unvarnished bark hut. There was no castellated
pile, or ancestral hall with hollow echoes in the
vast corridors ; in fact, the much-boomed bride-
groom was but a boundary-rider on the station
alleged to belong to him, and the red morocco
tome came in useful to prop up the damaged end
of a colonial sofa.
The newly-married Mrs. Scot Inglis has a taste
for curious pets. Her favorites are two snakes.
One is a very small and affable wriggler, who can
be carried about comfortably in a glove, and who
goes to sleep curled up on the lady’s palm. The
other is a robust streak of wrath, 9ft. Bin. long,
and said by connoisseurs to be two centuries old|
or thereabouts. The long snake is not wholly
affable ; when he is disturbed in his box he uses
language of the Louis XIY. period, and he has a
powerful grip when he can get the necessary
leverage.
Anguished hospital inmates have quite lately
had their pains intensified by an organisation
banded together to amuse the sufferers with song
and recitation. A nicely-worded prospectus
handed round to the charitable shows a graduated
scale of hilarity adapted to various degrees of grief
and pain. They sort out their mirth -raisers
systematically, so as “ neither to depress by undue
pathos, nor bring the sense of violent contrast to
bear too strongly, by unseemly jesting.” For
instance, it wouldn’t be wise to chant “ Bally-
hooley” in the dipsomaniac ward, and “The
Raven would probably be a depressing reeita-
tj°n m a room full of hysteria patients. But in
the casualty ward the day after St. Patrick’s, it
would be an interesting experiment to plav
Boyne Wa-ter ’ softly to see how the cases
would stand it. Prince Alfred hospital was the
first scene of operations by the new society, and
the entertainment was submitted to by the
inmates with Christian fortitude.

A ball was recently held at Bowen (Q.) which
,afe anew and original idea far -ummer dances.
With an outside temperature of anything
lOOdeg. in the shade, the festive gathering was
codec? in the chilling-room of the local meat
works, tastefully decorated with snow-wreaths and
icicles.
An up-country parson was anxious to get up a
little wind-raising flutter with the usual ch " r ? l ?'
repairs or inanse-buildmg m view. Coe ot h
flock, a lively girl, who had learned skirt-dancing
and other vanities, undertook to whoop up some-
thing entirely novel if he would give her
carte blanche, assuring the doubtful cleric, with
a confident gleam in her star-like eyes that
“ variety ” biz. is the only thing that pays
nowadays. Permission was granted, and the sub-
sequent show was a «ild and rapturous success,
consisting of alleged comic songs, step and buck
dances, and other exciting turns. At the end
thereof, the benefiting and ungrateful cleric arose
to pronounce the benediction, and placed his
official ban on the proceedings, assuring the
assemblage of his entire disapproval of that style
of entertainment, which he only submitted to in
order to scoop in the coin. He would take the
cash, but not the responsibility of the worldly per-
formance.
My Melb. correspondent writes: —
“A young lady who has done a good deal of
decent work in prose and verse, and whose signa-
ture has appeared at the foot of many respectable
wattle poems, is now going to ‘ give it best. Ihe
province of art and poetry which she has hung
around so long yields no remunerative return for
her life-long devotion, and she proposes to become
a waitress in a tea-room, or something equally
sordid, for a livelihood. No one can live in Aus-
tralia by dwelling in aculchawed fashion on the
outskirts of literature. In England such a person
as the historic ‘ Janus Weathercock ’ —the feathery
litterateur who murdered his sister-in-law ‘be-
cause her ankles were so thick,’ and became
known as Wainwright, the convict —could live by
writing pages of airy nothings about the glitter of
the bead on the sherry and other dreamy things
of the kind ; but here the person who merely
frivols nicely in the domain of letters makes
nothing by it.
“ Miss Ethel Levien, the pretty daughter of the
lon. Felix Levien (Melbourne), is to be married

“Professor Marshall Hall is regarded in Mel-
bourne society as an enfant terrible. For brusquerie
and unconventionality of manner he stands alone,
and only Minister Peacock and the laughing
jackass can do anything to approach his shriek of
merriment when he is in a festive humor. In
society at which Peacock draws the line, Marshall
Hall’s wild guffaw is unequalled.
“ Charles Magrath, who knocked the Australian
girls considerably when he came along with the
Belle-Cole Co., is now playing the name-part in
the Irish opera, ‘ Shamus O Brien.’ He is around
the Great Britain neighborhood. Magrath, by
the way, was presented by a love-sick Brisbane
girl with a swell emerald ring ; but he is a very-
much-married man, like Douglas Powell and most
other warbling pros, of whom damsels dream.

chum admirer is now said to be all off, and the
lively Hebraic prima-donna’s engagement to an
Auckland doctor is in much the same condition,
owing to family objections.
“ A par. lately published in a Melb. sassiety
journal re the approaching nuptials of a go-ahead
Melb. girl with a well-gilded son of Erin, roused
the ire of the bride elect. She visited the editor
in his dingy sanctum, and stated her opinion of
him and his paper in language which was frequent
and free, not to say tremendous. “ Filthy rag ”is
about the mildest epithet she now applies to the
once beloved chronicle of small beer, and the
damsel’s denunciations of the scandalous person
who gave away particular - which she would rather
have kept quiet prove very entertaining to her
dearest friends.—Yours affectionately, Cleo.
This last decree of truant man
Has plunged my soul in woe ;
For garments fashioned on the plan
Of manly togs must go.
I mustn’t wear his sailor-hat,
His collars or his studs ;
I don’t know what he would be at
In docking thus my duds.
The law won’t sanction rubber shoes,
So tennis now is gone ;
My bike is rusting out of use,
I can’t put bloomers on.
I can’t sans pants, on horseback rub,
Nor golf without a “Tam,”
From every sport debarred I’ll hide,
And murmur softly “ bother it! ”
My waist I’ll duly now begin
To clothe with legal tact,
For belts and arms both come within
The meaning of the Act.
I’d gladly ransack Eden’s code
To find a fashion new
Of wearing fig-leaves it la mode,
But Man has been there, too !

A ’cute Townsville dairyman ropes his
calves, ties their legs, and leaves them in the hen-
house for an afternoon a week, when the fowls eat
up the vermin on them. Also, he has a few special
fowls trained to perform his. cows’ toilettes while
in the bails at milking-time. A suggestion to be
acted on in tick country.
Now that a whaling revival is talked of, it
is interesting to know that one of Queen Victoria’s
imperial privileges is that she may, if she likes,
claim the half of every whale caught in imperial
waters. And with typical modesty the old
ordinance prescribes that Her Majesty’s shall be
the half with the head, on account of the value of
the whalebone.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
F • “ How truly Wordsworth said. • Scorn not
th . ’ The cricket was in danger of being scorned
f>n the first day of S.A. v. Vic., when Bruce and Graham
were*slain by Jones, when two wickets fell for 0 and three
for 4 There was nothing to watch save the venomous
bowline of Jones. H. Trott provided an interval-but
42 runs merely reduced the state of the score from
disastrous to calamitous. As for Harry, the veteran,
funk did the business for him, and Jones assisted funk by
cannonballing Harry: first in the waist, second in the
W Trumble also appeared for a brief season in sore
distress, and people will call him henceforth Tremble.
People knew the Victorians could never make 150 ; they
reckoned the innings would finish about 3.45 p.m.
There would be an interval, and Lyons would then
take the bat. But suddenly the innings found its
saviour in the almost unknown O’Halloran. With
iov it was observed that he scored his double
figures with grace and confidence. He reached Trott’s
score of 42, and passed it. The last man in was Johns,
one of the passengers of the late Australian eleven.
Everybody knows that Johns can’t bat. That is probably
why he put up a good score bv good play. Meantime
O'Halloran was well up in the nineties—and the total score
was almost 300. Next the new great man passed his
century, and severely hit George Giffen for 5. At six
o’clock the score, which was desolate, had got well on the
sunny side of 300, with one wicket still to fall. And there
was a popular rumor abroad that O’Halloran was the
greatest batsman in the world.
Harry Musgrove’s report to the A.C.C. intro-
duces the old fiction that Australian Elevens are over-
worked “It seems to me, too, that to play cricket for
six davs a week for 17 weeks is too much for the strongest,
and there must come a period of staleness sooner or later,
which three days’ holiday now and again would avert.”
Thus manager Musgrove. The Bulletin is tired of quot-
ing figures to prove that the Australian bitsmen and
bowlers make fewer runs and take fewer wickets during a
season than the leading English ditto. Why this eternal
assumption that Our Boys must necessarily get stale?
They don’t play six days a week for 17 weeks—not if they
do their duty bv polishing off the second-rate county
teams in two days. Then there is the occasional day’s
rest “in consequence of rain.” Furthermore, an Aus-
tralian Eleven numbers 14 players, and each man in the
crowd could get three days’ rest (exclusive of Sundays)
every second week if his health required it.
J * * * *
South Australia has the youngest interpro-
vincial cricket-team this season. Homburg, Bailey, Evans
and Hill are all youngsters not out of their teens, and
Travers is only just over 20. An illustration of the revo-
lution in S.A. ideas. Two years ago the S.A. team was
nearly toothless.
• • • •
Fred Jarvis, one of the best bats in S.A. on
club form, has been dropped out of the first eleven.
Reason, absence of nerve in big matches.
* • * •
Cricket ideas have changed considerably since
the time when a patient crowd would watch Alec Banner-
man’s “ sturdy defence ” with admiring interest. Donnan
seems to be voted, on all hands, a dreary bore.
* * * *
There was a strong muster of blackguardism in
the half-crown reserve at the M.B.C. New Year’s meeting,
Judging from appearances, the “push” of foul-mouthed
expectorators gathered in the neighborhood of the dress-
ing-room had not paid two arid-sixpence to get there.
They were deadheads—bike-riders, trainers, and hangers-
on. If the sport is to remain prosperous these alarming
items must be shut off as much as possible from unini-
tiated spectators. A very slight acquaintance with “the
scum of the track” is sufficient to take away one’s appe-
tite for bicycle-racing, which already seems doomed to go
the way of’professional pedestrianism and other played-
out games whose bullet-headed professors brought them
into contempt.
* * * *
Half-a-dozen riders at the M.B.C. summer
meeting at St. Kilda on Jan. 1 and 2 did more to kill cycle-
racing in Australia than anything that has yet happened,
only excepting the pusillanimity of the stewards who
bossed the show. On the first day there were a score of
instances of cronk running, perfectly obvious to every-
body who knew anything about the game, and quite as
glaring as the average work by which a jockey earns dis-
qualification on the worst-conducted pony-course in the
province. Yet the officials did nothing vehemently, and
apparently it was only the bustling they got from the
Saturday’s morning papers that impelled them to fine
Martin and Kellow £5 apiece the second day.
Kellow started in a mile heat with M'Donell,
his only opponent, and led until 100yds. from home. The
“ Unpaced King ” then came past at about a two-minute-
to-a-mile bat and the Austral winner let him go, pursuing
the even tenor of his way at the same old pace. Kellow
didn’t seem to try, but why he didn’t is not apparent.
Bilmartin’s lapse was similar, but different.
Bill started a hot favorite in a heat where he was pitted
against Middleton, J. Carpenter, and Boidi—a fact not to
be wondered at, for the race looked all his own. When
the sprint came—what there was of it—Middleton lashed
out at a terrific pace, something like that of a traracar,
and won by 20 lengths. Bill says he could not get out of
a pocket to respond to Middleton's spurt, but two men
can’t form much of a pocket anyhow, and some people
think it would take 25 to block the iron-jawed plugger if
he wanted to get out. Bill’s further excuse was that he had
been suffering from dysentery and had not recovered—in
proof of which he put in two startling sprints later in the
day, getting second to Porta by a wheel in the Brassey
Stakes and winning the 10-Mile Scratch. However, the
committee reckoned that neither of the explanations was
satisfactory, and, after an hour and a half’s deliberation,
decided to —well, fine each of the offenders £5.
The officials who decided the business were in a
difficulty. Bilmartin was more energetic than all of them
put together, and if they had made it worth his while to
serve out stoush freely, he was muscular enough to kill
the whole committee. Also, they had a proportion of
League men among them, and the League doesn’t want
to spoil the prospective Lewis-Martin match, which is esti-
mated to return that bod 3' many shekels. On the other
hand, the crowd looked as if it thought something
should be done, and the crowd, which provides the
shekels, must be considered.
* * * •
Another man was dealt with by the stewards
during the afternoon, but as he was not one of the top-
notchers, his case did not require so much “ earnest con-
sideration.” He was brought before the august body as
soon as he got off his machine, and when he heard the
complaint he degenerated into “blanky” language and
was promptly sent up for six months, the whole affair
being accomplished in 2min. 55 l-ssec.

into his 49th year.
A. G. Taylor, who said he would never
again lift a journaiistic pen, is now toiling at the
old game on a Parramatta newspaper.
It is generally understood that Bishop Goe
wifi shortly return to England. All the bishops
do. This end of the vineyard has no permanent
attractions.
On the Monday afternoon, when the
N.S.W. v. Vic. match was stopped by rain,
visitor Premier Reid improved the gloomy hour
by taking a quiet snooze. Blessed is the fat man
who can fall asleep at will.
Lay to your book. A Melb. railway-
porter lost both legs, last week, through obliging
another by doing his job for him. In the Windsor
(Melb.) railway collison, the slain fireman only
went that one trip to oblige a mate.
Minister for Education Jacob Garrard is
guaranteed to employ at least twice even in a
small address the whited-sepulchre phrase,
“ every right-thinking person,” as corroborative of
some of his own precious platitudes.
