Superintendent Sadlier, of the Victorian
police-force, has retired on pension, after 44 years’
active service. He joined in 1852 as a cadet, a
■class to which Hare, Lydiard, Fosbery, Nicholson,
Akehurst, and some others belonged, and is about
the last of that dignified order, promotion in the
police-force now being from the ranks.
Sedgwick Cowper, an Australian native
who should have made his mark, died last week
in Sydney Hospital from lung disease. He was
the youngest son of the late Archdeacon Cowper,
•one of the early chaplains in the “System,” by
his third wife, and was brother to ex-Premier
Charles Cowper and Dean Cowper, also uncle to
the Sydney ex-sheriff.
One of the most charming works of the
late poet, William Morris, is a scented romance
entitled “ The Wood beyond the World,” which,
in Sydney Public Library, is catalogued as
“Morris, W. : Wood beyond the World, The.”
The book is hardly read, most people thinking it
a scientific work upon the existence of timber in
other planets. This is fame.
Q. “Mines and Railways” Pliilp, some
time ago, severed his actual connection with the
shipping firm of Burns, Philp, and Co., Ltd. The
jibe still appearing in Q. newspapers that he
gets £lOOO a year from the co. and £lOOO a year as
Cabinet Minister is, therefore, unwarranted. Philp
may be a smart and energetic business man, but
he is a lame and unobtrusive legislator and a no-
account orator.
Ex-Justice Windeyer’s hopes of a seat on
the judicial committee of the Privy Council have
been shattered. The Imperial Act required that
at least five provinces should be agreed as to the
representative chosen, and in Chief Justice Way s
nomination this condition has been complied with.
S.A., Westralia, Tasmania, Victoria and Maori-
land all support the little South Australian and
his Pomeranian pup.
Sydney Maxted, grand boss of N.S.W.
charities, was, 30 years ago, editor of the Cumber-
land Mercury. Other ex-editors who are doing
well in notable billets are Conciliation Board
Clegg, now registering a number of abstract facts
which someone some time may like to know about,
and whisky’s valiant friend, the rotund Bevill.
Clegg edited the old Sydney Echo, and Bevill
once conducted the “we” department of a sheet
in India.
“I saw Duncan Gillies at a smoke night
of St. George’s Club,” writes a London corres-
pondent. “The resigning Agent-General looked
anything but resigned, and I doubt whether he
could be fitter or stronger or in better trim for
making the weary Turner sit up. He is anxious
to return and once more take the place now kept
tepid for him by the MTntyre. There will be a
fluttering in the dovecote at the top of Bourke-
street when Duncan’s stony, impassive stare once
more falls on the inmates.”
The patriotic editor of a paper in a
northern town, lately connected by rail, point
blank declined to insert an advt. from N.S.W.
Railway Commissioners re facilities for travelling
to the metropolis on holidays. “My paper,” he
said, “ refuses to aid the city octopus in sucking
the life-blood from the town that shelters me.”
An old Sydney identity died last week in
the person of Mrs. B. C. Rodd, of Barnstaple
Manor, Five Dock. The deceased lady was a
sister of “ Old Jack ” Robertson, and her husband,
Mr. Rodd, is the oldest attorney on the Australian
rolls, having been admitted to practise as far back
as 1834, during the Governorship of Richard
Bourke.
A certain Fat Kee, of Brisbane, was re-
cently haled before the beak and charged with
selling a shilling’s worth of opium in contraven-
tion of Section 9 of the Sale of Drugs and Poisons
Act. It transpired, in the course of evidence,
that Fat Kee paid the revenue no less than =£looo
per annum imported duty on opium. God bless
our leper.
A GENTLE HINT.
“Yes I but what on earth made you buy a tandem? ”
“Oh, I didn’t. It teas a present from the ewe lambs of my congregation.”
Professor Baldwin Spencer, of Melbourne
University, and F. J. Gillen (brother of the late
S.A. Lands Minister Gillen) are at Alice Springs
(8. A.), engaged in writing a monumental work on
the natives of Central Australia. Gillen has lived
for a-quarter of a century or more among the
people about whom he is writing, and probably
knows them as well as any man living. The
Australian aboriginal is fast becoming an extinct
subject, and is also rapidly rubbing off his most
distinctive characteristics by much friction against
the white man’s public-houses ; therefore, if Gillen
puts all he knows into his book, its value some
years hence should be enormous.
THE EFFICACY OF PRAYER.
This is the picture of a hop-beer vendor (locally known as a
“shy poo”) at Cue, W.A., who recently made a large stock of beer
in the expectation that the weather was going to be darned hot. It
immediately became darned cool, and the shypoo thereupon took the law
into his own hands, and prayed for a blast from Gehenna, as shown in the
picture . But Gehenna was “off.”
Premier Turner, of Victoria, is the only
Australian knight announced among the New
Year degradations. Turner is one of the few
Australian politicians who can take a knighthood
without incurring political disaster. He never
said that he was a republican or a democrat or
that he wanted to pull down the Throne ; also he
never professed to have any veneration for that
gilded institution. He was never suspected of
asking for a title, and he never said he wouldn’t
take one if it came. In all his speeches Turner is
hardly known to have made one allusion to the
Crown or the British Flag or the H’empire ; he
let ail these things drift as matters in which
he took no interest. And, if his past record
goes for anything, Turner, K.C.M.G., in his old
suit will defend the everyday horse-thief just as
before, and the shabby little man will probably
lose his gewgaw, or w r ear it askew, or drop it
absent-mindedly on the floor and stand on it, and
hereby, The Bulletin trusts, bring it into even
more obloquy than it has hitherto attained.
J. F. Thallon, till now general traffic-
manager of Q. railways, has been appointed
deputy-railway commissioner, which means that
he will be “Bob” Gray’s substitute when the
latter’s liver is bad. Thallon has been 16 years
in the Q. railway service. “ Bob ” Gray, who was a
brother-in-law of the late Joshua Peter Bell, seems,
to old Bananalanders, to have been always in the
public service, in some comer or the other.

Drury, of the Q.N. Bank, died reputedly
worth over ,£70,000. Unless his assets were of
the same character as those of the scented institu-
tion which he managed, Drury evidently did a lot
better for himself than for his depositors.
How Governors save money. A Melb.
chemist shows in his window a small free-franked
envelope, “On Her Majesty’s Service.” He
proudly adds that it contained a request from the
present Governor of another province for some of
his unrivalled insecticide.
Foster, Vic. Minister for Mines, was a
Gippsland storekeeper in bygone years. Quite
recently, when Foster in all his glory was touring
Gippsland with some other Ministers, one of his
old customers suddenly recognised him, and there
and then paid him 7s. 6d. off an old account for
flour and tinned fish.
Gov. Hampden does not part with his coin
in response to private appeals for charity as easily
as did his predecessors, Jersey and Carrington,
A derelict musician who “ lives on the game ”
recently wrote a pathetic begging-letter to His
Ex. asking for relief m his necessities. His Ex.
wrote from Moss Vale that he would communicate
with the applicant m a few days, and the derelict
concluded that his little spec, would pan out at
least a tenner. But it only panned out for a
lithographed circular which was partly “ haw ”
and partly an intimation that the applicant had
better try the Charity Organisation Society.
Ex-Governor Loch was engaged to preside
over Duncan Gillies’ farewell dinner at St. George’*
Club this week. This should be an affecting cere-
mony. Loch, the boom Governor of Victoria,
proposing the health of the shiny little fat man
who ran the boom Government, and predicting
(loud cheers) that the return of Gillies to the scene
of their former greatness would inaugurate an-
other era of prosperity, &c., &e.
“THE BULLETIN'S ” NEW YEAR CARD.
Vic. Judge Hodges’ luck has been bad of
late. He had three children very ill with diph-
theria, and his wife was also on the sick-list
through overwork and anxiety. Then Hodges
started for the scene of his judicial duties on his
bicycle, and another bikist ran him down, the re-
sult, so far as Hodges was concerned, being a
broken leg and a dislocated ankle. He was
brought home, and that evening the family cook
also laid up with diphtheria.
The most intimate friend of a well-known
Australian politician sums .him up thus: “He
can’t help lying ; it’s constitutional, like gout or
insanity or kleptomania. He doesn’t lie for any
purpose, but merely because he can’t help it. A
thing may be true although he says it is, but the
chances are always against it. His accuracy lies
in the fact that you can generally rely on things
being what he says they are not, but of late he
has told the truth once or twice, and this is
making people lose confidence in him.”
A Cairns (Q.) Asiatic sugar-planter and
capitalist was recently arrested by the police in
connection with a cane-burning case, and dis-
charged “without a stain on his character.” Now
this cultured, gentlemanly Aryan was carted about
chained to sundry Kanakas, and there is the very
devil to pay. The Asiatic gent, is naturally indig-
nant at being manacled to savages and cannibals.
And the Kanakas who despise Asiatics are natur-
ally also wrathful. The distinctions of class and
color in Bananaland are truly wonderful.
Vague reports of Thomas MTlwraith’s
further relapse come in by cable. If M ‘llwraith
is well-advised he will continue to relapse till the
Q.N. Bank trouble is definitely over. The Nelson
Government may have all the goodwill in the
world to smother the ghost of that wonderful in-
stitution, but the balance of power lies with the
British depositors, and the spectre may rise in all
manner of unexpected places. The wisest thing
would be for everybody concerned to fall ill and
remain unconscious for 12 months at least.
Ex-Argus leader-writer James Smith
claims to have made a wonderful discovery (which
he will publish) that will further settle the settled
question as to who wrote the letters of “ Junius.”
“ Junius,” however, is rather out of date —if the
eminent journalist could but settle the question as
to who wrote certain anonymous letters to Mrs.
Aubrey Bowen (Money Miller’s daughter and
banker Edward Miller’s sister) in reference to
alleged obligations acknowledged by the spirit of
her deceased husband, he would clear up a matter
of much more interest to Melbournians.
Powell, the retrenched boss of the Sydney
Custom House, is probably well-advised in not
moving for an increase of his pension. His pen-
sion—or gratuity, if he likes it that way—is valued
at about £2OO per annum. His merchant friends
have handed him £l2l in a purse. He has been
appointed member of the Board of Customs at
£lOO a year, and member of the Health Board at
another £lOO a year. Mr. Powell may deserve
the billets which have rained on him, but even
though he didn’t deserve them a man with his
numerous political friends would probably get
them all the same.
Caledonian Liqueur Whisky

CHIEFLY IN MELBOURNE.


A STATUE OF GOV. PHILLIP HAS BEEN EXECUTED FOB THE DOMAIN.


POLITICAL POINTS
Brisbane Telegraph brags that “ Queens-
land is likely to be the tail that will wag the Aus-
tralian-federation dog.”
There are now 1700 Justices of the Peace
in S. A. out of 87,000 males over the age of 20 years,
or an average of one J.P. to every 50 adult males.
Lands-Minister O’Loughlin is the only
member of the S.A. Cabinet who does not seek
election to the Federal Convention. Someone has
to remain behind while the rest of the Govern-
ment is away constitution-making.
It is reckoned that the Maoriland Govern-
ment was backed up 197,000 votes at the late
election, while the Opposition numbered 155,000
supporters. If so, the figures are getting peri-
lously close, and Seddon may have to hustle all he
knows in ’99.
Two Q. Labor-members, Jackson and
M‘l)onnell, crossed over the floor of the House,
and supported an adjournment of the debate on
the second reading of the utterly-idiotic Anti-
Gambling Bill (afterwards shelved). Carried by
23 to 21. Had these two not so voted, the Bill
would have been rejected right there.
Chief-Justice Madden’s appointment as
Acting-Gov. of Victoria, during the five-weeks’
holiday in Maoriland of Gov. Brassey, recalls the
complications which arose in N.S.W. some years
ago, when Gov. Hercules Robinson went to annex
Fiji. He was to be absent six weeks, and Chief-
Justice Martin demanded to be sworn in as
Acting-Gov. Parkes refused, saying that the
province could govern itself for that short time.
Martin and wife kicked up a row, and Robinson’s
wife vowed she would leave Govt. House if
any of the Martin “ faction ” came there.
Result: Hercules K. “explained” matters to the
Home authorities ; not in a very generous manner,
giving the pedigree of the Martins and other
habitues of Govt. House, and the row ended in old
Alfred Stephen being appointed Lieut.-Governor
for life
Even Brisbane
Courier is satisfied
that Nelson is going
to clear out of politics
at the first decent op-
portunity. First he
meant to quit after the
elections ; then he was
persuaded to see the
Q.N. Bank stench dis-
infected ; and still his
party, frightened of
riots and dissension in
the Cabinet, is loth to
let him go. Nelson had
a fair reputation when
he started politics, but
he has damaged it con-
siderably by thick-and-
thin party prostitution.
Even now, however,
he’s held about the
cleanest man on his
side. It’s a great poli-
tical help to be the son
of a parson especially
a Presbyterian parson.
One quaint feature
about the Queensland
increase of newspaper
postage is that Queens-
land itself derives no
profit whatever from
the additional rate le-
vied. No outside news-
papers are to be ad-
mitted into Queens-
land after Ist January
unless they bear the
increased postage-rates
and thereby contribute
so much more to the
postal revenue of the
provinces from which
they came. Queensland,
however, doesn’t insist
on any increased post-
age on its newspapers
sent to other provinces,
therefore it gains no-
thing by its own move,
except the satisfaction
of trying to shut out
some publications
which have spoken dis-
respectfully of its fa-
mous bank. And, as
its own organs are now
speaking about as
badly concerning its famous bank as outside ones
could do, this satisfaction amounts to very little.
Even the S.M. Herald now prints M.P.
after Leg. Assembly names and M.L.C. after
those of members of the Council. John Haynes
was the first really persistent M.P. in New South
Wales.
A Melbourne paper, which professes to be
in the confidence of the National Ass, says that
money is already being raised to defray the exes, of
certain special Ass candidates at next gen. election ;
also, that a much stronger man than Barrister
Coldham has been got ready to stand against
Premier Turner for St. Kilda. Coldham was over
200 votes behind Turner at last contest, and the
Premier has gained much additional popularity
since then.
Four of the brightest and most eminently
disappointing young politicians in Australia are
Drake, of Queensland ; B. R. Wise (there is such a
person, isn’t there?), of New South Wales; and
Deakin and Shiels, of Victoria. All four are
able, eloquent, with good powers of reasoning and
analysis—and all are failures of much the same
pattern. As Omar Pasha said of his prospective
successor Abdul-Kerim, they seem to be too
learned for victory.”
A section of Q. Labor-party has been
“ judiciously influenced ” to support Boodle wraith
and the Q.N. Bank. Exactly how the influence
was applied is a matter of conjecture. But an
astonishing exhibition of Labor in politics was
given the other day, when Leader Glassey and
others attended a peepshow of the St. Helena
political prisoners by special invitation from
Minister Tozer, and were afterwards fed and
wined by their host —the man who told the police
to “ fire effectively” on the unionists of the ’94
strike !
The author of the pregnant booklet,
“Made in Germany,” which has set British
manufacturers by the ears, sums his conclusions
as to how British trade can be regained or re-
tained. Conclusion No. 2
We must federate the Empire. They (the colonies)
must discriminate in their tariffs, and admit English pro-
ducts on more favorable terms than those of other
nations.
Astonishing how long it takes to kill the British
notion that “colonies ” are cows to be milked for
the benefit of Brummagem.
Kanaka Queensland is still illogically tout-
ing for white immigrants. Only the other day
another immigration-lecturer was appointed, in
the person of Rev. G. D. Buchanan, of Wickham
Terr. Presbyterian Church, a canny parson who
is blessed with much eloquence and a well-developed
love of the saxpence. Why a parson should be
sent on such an errand is difficult to understand.
No lecturer who tells the solid truth about the
land of debt and boodle and strange banks will
attract emigrants, and no parson can be reason-
ably expected to tell lies for mere sordid cash.
THE CHILD OF THE BUSH.
(In Martin Place, Sydney.)
LITTLE BILLEE (from the Big Scrub): “ There are some awful big huts
about here, father!"
Treasurer Holder (S.A.) has hopes that the
drought will not cause a loss of more than
.£lO,OOO to the revenue during the financial year
ending next June, and a surplus of about .£4OOO is
expected.
Premier Seddon is already laying his plans
for a commercial union between Maoriland and
the future Australian Commonwealth. Ex-
Treasurer Ward was strongly of opinion that
Maoriland should be a member of the Common-
wealth, and so was ex-Premier Atkinson,, but
Ballance held contrary views and believed in
standing aloof. If Maoriland does come into the
Federation, the lumping of all the debts together
will reduce its liability by £7 per head, and its
interest bill by 6s. per annum for each inhabitant.
If it stands out, and Seddon’s scheme of a com-
mercial league falls through, it will retain its big
debt all to itself and be practically shut out by
the Federal tariff from its best market. Either
way the adhesion of M.L. is tolerably certain
sooner or later.
Some more Brisbane police-court sen-
tences, this time by magistrate Murr j,o or 14

THE MEDIUM POTATO.
MURPHY : “ The little taties be very small this year, Flynn.”
FLYNN : “I doan’t know ; some of 'em does be a very fair size."
When Yic. Parliament adjourned the
~ther day Melb. Paunch threw its usual vague
valedictory brick at the Ministry on the ground
that it ought to have “busied itself in trying to
mend the country’s prospects from the point of view
of the breadwinner.” Possibly the well-meaning
Turner would have done so if he had known how,
and probably Paunch would tell him how if it
knew itself—which apparently it doesn’t. Tory
journalism in Australia is very vague, and seldom
gets beyond the statement that “ measures should
be taken to restore confidence and promote the
development of the resources of this great
country,” or something like that.
One unfenced waterhole at Leichhardt
(Sydney) has been responsible for 13 drownings.
It is ori a piece of vacant land, the owner of which
is lost or dead—anyhow he has been unknown for
many years. No taxes are paid on this land. In
the present ridiculous state of the municipal law
there is no provision for prompt seizure and sale
to recover the unpaid rates. If the local authori-
ties fill in the hole they must do it at their own
expense, and if the owner ever does turn up he
may sue them for damage to the blessed property.
Wanted in N.S.W. a law similar to that now
existing in M.L, by which all unclaimed land
vests in the Public Trustee and is sold or leased
instead of remaining an eyesore for ever and ever.
Already there is a small outbreak of pro-
vincial jealousy as to which capital shall be the
scene of the Federal convention. Melb. demands
it on the ground that Sydney had the last
convention, and it is generally understood that
the demand will be conceded, on the understanding
that the second convention meeting, which is due
about four months after the first one, takes place
in Sydney. This tacit arrangement would be all
right, only Adelaide, which has had no convention
at all yet, juits in a very reasonable claim, so the
movement towards Unity may end in blood and
hair just when it seemed about to begin. The
presence of the convention is no special honor,
and no great profit beyond a trifling sum for
drinks, but these provinces are dreadfully solid on
the question of their dignity.
About the most unanimous curse ever
heaved at a Govt, department was that slung by
Vic. Assembly at Duffy’s Post Office management.
When the postal estimates came on for discussion
and gave the House a fair chance to let off its
long bottled-up opinion, it rose almost as one man—
Liberals, Conservatives and Labor-members alike —
and categorically denounced Duffy as a hopeless
incapable. It pointed out about 800 ways in
which the department was a bungle and a swindle
and a failure, and Duffy replied by cackling a few
inane jokes, and didn’t even attempt to seriously
answer any of his critics. If the Turner Ministry
hopes to live it will have to throw its P.M.G.
overboard very soon. Duffy is a flippant and
casual individual, who seems to regard his depart-
ment as a joke, or an Ally Sloper, or something
of the kind.
One proviso in the Federal Enabling Act
is that three provinces, “ including New South
Wales,” must be in the movement before anything
can be done. It is this which makes the obvious
lukewarmness of the N.S.W. Government especi-
ally ominous, for it holds the key of the position.
Tasmania, S.A., Victoria and Westralia would
make a respectable Federation to begin with, but
that would require a new Act, and before it could
be passed the movement which is now tepid would
probably be quite cold. The trouble is that
N.S.W., being the one essential province accord-
ing to the Act, is in a position to demand the
location of the Federal capital within her borders
or some other special concessions, failing which
she can burst up the whole business. The Federal
movement was supposed to “start fair” for all
parties, but somehow it didn’t.

In N.S.W. 1895
was the last year of
Protection Protec-
tion, that is, tem-
pered with the know-
ledge that Freetrade
was coming in the
very adjacent future.
According to Part
VII. of the N.S.W.
Statistics, just issued,
the number of hands
employed in the fac-
tories and workshops
increased in that year
by 1082, and this fact,
though not in itself
very definite proof,
tends to show that
things in l- v 95 were
moving slowly in the
right direction. The
thorough-going Pro-
tectionist opines that
this increase was the
natural improvement
caused by Protection,
and would have been
much greater only for
the baleful shadow of
approaching Free-
trade. The other man
is of opinion that it
was the natural im-
provement caused by
the near approach of
Freetrade, which in-
stilled hope and joy
into the manufac-
turer’s bosom, and
enabled him to bear
up against the hi >rn >rs
of Protection in the
immediate present.
And until the figures
for 1896 come along
a year hence the two
parties will probably
keep the wrangle go-
ing without anything
definite resulting.
Attorney-General Tommy Byrnes is be-
coming recognised as the most reactionary force in
Q. politics. It was only the other day that he
managed to cancel the Q. enactment against
deathbed legacies to churches and convents under
priestly influence, to the prejudice of widows and
orphans; and the priestly party trusts him
wholly. He is manoeuvring now to subsidise
R. schools at the expense of the State, and
overturn the Q. education system. In return for
his devotion to Mother Church, the clerical in-
fluence is continually strained in favor of the man
advertised as “ humbly prostrate at the feet ” of
Pope Leo.
Q. Police-Commissioner Parry-Okeden has
just done a tour through the North, following
closely on the recent footsteps of Archie Meston.
Meston came back from his inspection of the
autochthones with ghastly tales of blood, murder,
leprosy and other diseases, oppression, rum and
pessimism generally. Tired officialdom had either
to put things right or send someone to assert that
they weren’t wrong. Consequently, Okeden re-
turns, as everybody expected, with the glad tale
of how the Gulf aboriginal is a bright and sunny
nigger who leads an idyllic existence and is fondly
cared for by everybody. And peace supervenes
beautifully once more.
The Maoriland election made a consider-
able slaughter among the members of last Parlia-
ment. Sixty-five of them stood for re-election,
and of these 21 were left out in the cold. Five more
fled from the wrath to come, and didn’t face the
opprobrium and decayed egg of the infuriated
elector. Against this, 10 old politicians who went
under at previous elections and have since been
looking longingly at the honorarium from afar off
have got back again. Ten candidates, four of
them from Dunedin, forfeited their deposit of <£lo
each. No less than 12 candidates raged at the
poll in the windy southern city, and under these
conditions the four men at the bottom of the list
were so very low down that their postion was
hardly worth mentioning.
When the Maoriland provinces were to be
swept into the great maw of the Central Govern-
ment, they got nearly two years’ notice of the com-
ing event. It was provided among other things that
on the merging of the provinces the Central
Govt, took over any cash balances, all debts, and
all outstanding liabilities. J. D. Ormond, super-
intendent of Hawkes Bay (good, careful man),
actually handed over a cash balance. “ Jemmy ’
Macandrew, superintendent of Otago, being a
pawky, long-headed Scot, increased that province’s
liabilities by about .£lOO,OOO, let a large number
of contracts for public works which the Welling-
ton Govt, had to take over and finish and pay for,
and had nary cent in the provincial Treasury.
There should be a fine opportunity to work a
similar oracle when Australian Federation is a
little nearer.

Signs of the times. In Brunswick street,
Fitzroy, Melb., there is a fruit-shop which pro-
vides a concert on Saturday nights for the delecta-
tion of all customers who have bought sixpenn’orth
or upwards of fruit during the week. A little
lower down, in the same street, a bootmaker re-
hoofs the boots of the weary traveller for Is. 6d.
while he waits.
The tick is truly übiquitous. He has been
discovered on cattle, horses, sheep, native com-
panions, turkeys, Europeans, niggers, Chinamen,
Japs., snakes, hens, iguanas, lizards, and even on
the body of the common fly. He came mysteri-
ously and unaccountably, and from many inland
districts he is disappearing just as mysteriously as
he came.

“ SINBAD THE SAILOR,” AT TIER MAJESTY’S THEATRE, SYDNEY.


The Englishman, Relph, is evidently a boss
sprinter of the Zimmy type. Relph, at present,
looks much the same as the ’Murkan did 12 months
ago, and as Martin is at last beginning to look in his
turn. But the new man’s chance of improving between
now and Natives’ Day should be quite as good as Bil-
martin’s, all things considered.
Relph debuted magnificently in a series of
flashing sprints that place him in the first rank of
finishers. The first day he ran into third place in the
New Year Cup with a run in the last 100yds. the like of
which the St. Kilda track had never before seen. Relph
passed no less than nine men, and had the race been a
couple of yards longer it was his. On the Saturday he won
the Mile by inches from Walne, the men hanging head to
head for nearly half a lap. Porta is a very different man
from what he was last season. In the Five Miles he won
with three lengths to spare from Bilmartin, and badly up-
set that adamantine person’s apple-cart in the Brassey
Stakes. Bill had backed himself considerably for this
event but underrated the Italian’s powers and left his
sprint too late, with the result that he came in a foot be-
hind. Walne won the first-class handicap from 10 yards,
showed some fine but unsuccessful sprints, and did a
marvellous amount of fair, unselfish pacing over the
longer distances. Kuhle was another man who did a 10l
of good, honest riding without scoring a first.
* • • *
Porta’s brilliant finishes in the International
Scratch and the Brassey Stakes next day showed up the
excellence of Walne, who beat the Italian twice in heats
during the meeting. Walne’s well-sustained run of nearly
600 yards in the “ Lap” race, urith Porta vainly trying to
pass him all the time, was a remarkably game perform-
ance. The Queensland champion is seemingly the most
determined pacemaker of them all.
» * » *
The judging at the big Ballarat and Bendigo
cycle meetings on Xmas and Boxing days was even more
erratic than that of the Austral meeting. In one race
Martin ran an unmistakable second, yet he was placed
first, the third man being second, and the real winner
third. In another, the cunning push fell in heavily over the
judge’s error. It was arranged among those who had any
sort of a chance that the favorite was to go down while
a middle-mark man was to win. The money was on and
the race was splendidly executed, the crack just being
beaten by half-a-wheel after a magnificent struggle. But
when the numbers went up who should be pronounced
the winner but the disgusted favorite himself. There was
no getting away from the judge’s decision, and the youths
of much brain had to return to Melb. empty in pocket
and with a heap of kudos which their champion wasn’t
taking.
• * • *
Alex. Jewell, a Maryborough cyclist who has
done nothing on metropolitan tracks so far, made a
sensational record during the holidays. At Ballarat,
three weeks ago, he won every race on the card, heats
and finals, and carried off the pacing-prize in the long-
distance event as well. On Boxing Day he continued his
good work by winning the big handicap in the same
locality and two minor events. The rider’s brother, Don
Jewell, is a well-known Vic. cyclist, and earned some
eminence on one occasion when Porta respectfully saluted
him and Don Walker under the impression that they were
members of the nobility.
♦ * • *
It is gravely announced that the A.N.A. cele-
bration committee has received entries for its big £3so'
cycling contest from Lerna, the Swiss champion ; John
Green, one of the English top-notchers, and Starbuck, the
Yankee long-distanoer, all of whom are to arrive in Aus-
tralia by 10th inst. By the way, what has happened to
Huret, Jaquelin, Linton, Michael and Johnston, all of
whom signified their intention (in the Melb. press) of com-
peting in the Austral, just over ?
* * * *
During the Bendigo cycling carnival, the visitors
were shown over the goldfields, and, after being lowered
to the bottom of Lansell’s big shaft, were handed a number
of specimens for examination. A hunk of quartz richly
studded with gold, and containing close upon 20z., struck
Porta’s attention, and, holding it before the manager’s;
face, he inquired, “ You give me him?” “No fear,” re-
plied the stalwart Cousin Jack. “Me take him Italy
you give me joost leetle one, then ? ” A laborious expla-
nation was entered into, and at last Porta was hustled
into the cage disconsolate. “ Eef I get him,” he confided
to Bilmartin, later in the day, “ I keep him always ; show
the loaf of ze peepel Australia, and how Porta loaf zem.”
Bill smiled. “ I’d boil it down,” he observed. Bill owes,
a lot of his prosperity to the fact that he methodically
“ boils it down.”
* * * *
After the Bendigo (Vic.) cycling sports, on Box-
ing Day, a party of visitors were having threepenn’orth at
the huge telescope which an itinerant astronomer was ex-
hibiting in Pall Mall. Bilmartin was one of the crowd,
and, as he pressed his eye to the aperture, the ’scope hap-
pened to be turned on the ringed planet Saturn. Bill
heaved a mighty breath and stood transfixed. At last he
turned. “ Guess that’s about the neatest bicycle track in
the universe,” he remarked. And thus was Saturn
summed up by Bilmartin.
♦ * * *
The Red Bird and Dunlop people, recently so
/ike brothers that they joined forces to pace Bilmartin in
his match with Parsons, are now at daggers drawn, and
the men of Tyre are combing Australia for a man to
permanently down the iron-jawed one. Lewis, when he
recovers from his accident, will have the Dunlop pacers;
at his disposal in his match with the Yank.
* * » *
Porta wildly challenges any Italian in Aus-
tralia to a match over whatever distance he likes to name 1
for £5O a side, and especially directs his offer to Boidi.
“ Boidi, he skite, ‘ Porta, pooh, me lose Porta.’ Now
Porta’s pocket skite,” excitedly remarked the challenger
as he gave out his intention to a Melbourne reporter.
The challenge is said to be due to the superior
Boidi’s refusing to pal-up with his CQmpatriots- here.
Touched on the subject he remarked : “ Me, Boidi, me
doctor my country. Maccaroni, pah ! he oyster man-
Sardino, pooh ! he sell bananas. Me, Boidi, doctor.”
* * * *
Cyclists are really the most modest of all ath-
letes. After the preliminary heats of the last Austral
were run, the competitor who had shown possibly the
best form of the lot strolled into a Bourke-street cycle-
agency and coolly offered to ride one of the firm’s
machines in the final heat, provided he were laid the
jigger and £lOO to nothing about the result. He was
politely told to take a walk.
« » » *
What have bike-reporters in general to say to
the following allegation by a Melb. oracle ? “ Pacing is’;
rarely introduced in mile events. It is in this way. The
men don’t want it fast at the start, and they need not
follow the pace-makers if they like. The main thing is to
be in the rear when the bell rings for the last lap. The
man in this position wins five times out of six.” If this,
be logical reasoning what is the value of a mile-“ record,”
anyway ? Two men are matched to race a mile with;
triplets to lead them individually. One man sets out.
with the stern determination to go his fastest for the;
whole distance and beat the record. He and his triplet
travel on their joint “ own,” in opposition to the other
fellow and his triplet. But no matter how fast the pace
of the record-breaker, and notwithstanding that he is
getting the last ounce of strength out of himself, he must
suffer defeat if he retains his lead until the bell rings for
the final lap. The other fellow is bound to excel the ex-
celler, for the simple reason that he has more ground to
cover within the last few seconds. Of which The Bulle-
tin will merely say, in the meantime, that bike-racing
leads to extraordinary conclusions.
* * • *
The greater part of the racing at St. Kilda
ground last Friday and Saturday was a dreary procession
of pettifogging stiff-’uns, going slow with a view to future
handicaps. Only the first-class men cared to make a
decent display, and two or three of the highflyers care-
lessly omitted to give the authorities any excuse for not
wiping them out.
* • * ...
The bike craze has reached Smyrna in Asiatic
Turkey, and a four-lap track banked to allow of 30 miles
an hour records has been constructed for the use of
“ scorchers ” of the Prophet.
* * * *
At a recent N.S.W. cycle meeting the writer
counted over 40 of the hawk-nosed gentry gathered in
the pavilion openly crying the odds. And a certain
wheelman with a pelican beak was the same afternoon
called before the stewards for “ eccentric” riding. Great
Mosesh, how long before wheeling is killed like the other
Jew-ridden sports?
» * » »
The all-engulphing trade which swallows every
cyclist of the first rank has pounced upon Walne, who
has decided to relinquish bridge-building and shearing in
Queensland for a billet in a Melb. cycle agency. Walne is
a rapidly-improving man, and promises to be on the
scratch-mark before long.
* * * *
Damien, who scored his first notable win at
Randwick the other Monday, was the recognised “likely
outsider” for last Melb. Cup. A good many Victorian
followers of the horse seem to have missed backing him
when the right time arrived.
* * * *
Tom, who ran second in the Highweight Handi-
cap and third in the Summer Cup at Randwick meeting,
used to be hacked about Maitland sale-yards, and won a
number of casual saddles and bridles at small bush meet-
ings before his abilities were known. He has won 15 races
in the last 12 months, and has been unplaced only once.
* * * *
In the four days’ racing at Randwick the im-
ported sire Splendor (Speculum—Balhilde) topped the list
of winning sires with three wins to his credit, whilst his
progeny were frequent place-getters. Of the jockeys D.
Cook and H. Keane dead-heated for first place with three
wins apiece.
* » * *
Two hundred and fifty pounds seems a big price
to claim a two-year-old for out of a selling race, but the
S.A. trainer, T. Kiely, knew what he was about when he
secured Tapioca at Randwick for the aforementioned
sum. Many of the S.A. two and three-year-old events
will doubtless be picked up by this very speedy son of
Goldsborough, who will yet be found capable of holding
his own with the best of his year.
» * * »
Notwithstanding the eleventh-hour scratching
■of Cartridge in Villiers Stakes, the public piled their
shekels on him for the Carrington Stakes, only to be once
more “badly left,” as Carbine’s son was again scratched
for no reason assigned at the last moment. Merrily,
therefore, did the aggrieved punter cheer when the much-
fancied Response was badly beaten in Tatt.’s Cup. Isn’t
it about time the authorities made inquiries into these
late scratchings—for which, as a rule, no reason, honest
or otherwise, is given—with a view to the alteration of the
rule ? The public deserve protection in matters of this
kind.
» * * «
The favorable wind materially helped Precaution
when she beat all previous Australian records by cutting
out Carrington Stakes in lmin. 13Asee. Trained quietly
at Rosehill, but few people knew of the wonderful sprint-
ing capabilities of this diminutive filly, who had previously
run a few times very indifferently at suburban courses,
consequently her party managed to win a fair stake at
long odds. So it was alfor her a record performance.
It will be difficult to hand.va • icr out of the Newmarket,
for, though but a galloway, slu looks a weight-carrier.
* * * *
That arch-rogue, Newman, carrying but Bst. 41b.
in the very poor company that contested Tatt.’s Cup,
further discounted his claims to be classed a stayer by
finishing nowhere. Everyone (barring perhaps his san-
guine owner) is now thoroughly disgusted with him, so
he will probably bob up serenely for a big handicap—un-
backed, but well-cursed by his previous unfortunate
backers—and win in a canter. The rogue horse has
ireserves of innate cussedness which no one has ever yet
quite fathomed.
* * * *
Ex-N.S. Waler, Le Var (Caulfield Cup third),
<easily won Westralian Derby at Perth on New Year’s Day,
while his former stable mate, Paul Pry, annexed the
Perth Stakes. In the latter race the Perth Cup favorite,
Inverary, was seriously injured The Cup, probably
thanks to the handieapper’s skill, was kept “ at home,”
none of the t’othersiders finishing in the first three. Still
the chueked-in Destiny, owned by the Premier’s brother,
only managed to secure third place, much to the disgust
of the clever division. Altogether the meeting was a
great suocess, and the t’othersiders scored well in the
minor events.
* * « •
Melb. Age made an amusing botch of a cable-
message the other week. The names Norman (Sir Henry),
Edward Grey (Sir), and Sir (David M.) Barbour came
along the wire, apropos to a royal commission of enquiry
into the West Indian sugar industry. Somehow the Age
mixed up this item with a sporting event, and published
the three names as placed horses in the Staffordshire
Hurdle Race —Norman, 1; Edward Grey, 2; Sir Barbour. 3.
Worse still, the ingenious journal found a pedigree for
the winner, and the ex-Governor of Bananaland was
represented as the joint production of Lucifer 11. and
Norah.
* * * *
“ It’s very bard,” said an ex-jockey the other
day, as he lifted his nose out of the pewter-pot, “to keep
in with both sides. I rode to the boss’s orders and lost
the race when the stewards had the mare backed, so they
rubbed me out. My boss raced for the books. They pay;
the stewards don’t. If I’d won I’d have got the sack, and
as like as not never have got another job, for no one’ll
trust a man that doesn’t ride to orders. As I lost I got
the bloomin’ sack, and quite likely I’ll never get back
again. I was dead sure of the blanky sack either way.
The sack’s the only thing you are sure about the way
things go now.”

Milling prospectors’ outfits are advertised
in Sydney. They include a boiler. The best use
the average prospector, with an unknown mate,
can make of the boiler is to get inside and stay
there and keep the hole at which he entered
■covered with his gun.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; !),
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. #

Public Notices.
Land and Income Tax Office,
Custom House, Sydney,
29th December, 1890.
LAND AND INCOME TAX ASSESSMENT ACT
OF 1896.
"V’OTIOE is hereby given, under section 30 of the above
Act, that Returns of Income for the purposes of the
assessment of Income Tax are required to be furnished to
the Commissioners of Taxation, at their Office, on or
before the FIFTH day of FEBRUARY, 1897 ; and all per-
sons who are in receipt of income, and all persons liable
under the provisions of the Act in any representative
capacity to furnish Returns for the purposes aforesaid,
are hereby informed that the prescribed Forms of
Returns, except those for Companies, are now available,
and may be obtained on application at any Post Office,
Police Office, Custom House, Railway Station, and the
Office of any Clerk of Petty Sessions in New South Wales,
Stamp Duties Office, Phillip-street, Sydney, or at the
Taxation Office, Custom House, Sydney.
, Forms for Companies can be obtained at the Stamp
Duties Office, Phillip-street, Sydney, or at the Taxation
Office, Custom House, Sydney.
All owners of taxable land subject to mortgage are
required to furnish Returns, on or before the above date,
showing particulars of money owing by the owner, and
secured wholly or partly on mortgage of such land ; also,
particulars of every security given to or held by the
mortgagee of such land, in consideration of the moneys
advanced. Forms of the prescribed Return may be
obtained on application at an3' of the abovementioned
Offices.
All persons carrying on any business, trade, or pro-
fession on their own account are required to furnish
Income Returns irrespective of the amount of their

The scaffold of Melbourne gaol is so
arranged that, after the drop has fallen, a patent
catch retains the trap-door, to prevent it swinging
back and striking the suspended hangee on the
head. And yet there are certain narrow-minded
people who contend that present treatment of the
criminal is brutal and devoid of all instincts of
humanity.

What strikes the t’othersider first in the
agricultural districts of W. A. is the mild hope-
lessness of the old settled inhabitant. The
second thing that strikes him is the far-off, upward
expression in the old settler’s eye, his slowness of
speech, the listless hang of his limbs, his general
looniness, and his uncomplaining patience. In his
own way, the confirmed Westralian is a good
employer. He doesn’t drive his employe hard in
the desire to make money, for he never expects to
make any. If a thing isn’t done to-day it may as
well be done to-morrow, or next day, or next year,
or some other time. If it is never done at all it
probably doesn’t matter much. There is so much
space, and so much time, and so much sand and
Scrub, and such vast areas of everything that the
vastness of them has settled down on the soul of
the old Westralian and squashed it out flat. In
the old days places were so far apart that no one
ever seriously expected to get anywhere, and they
were so much alike that any other place would do
as well. There was so much wilderness in propor-
tion to population that no one ever seriously
hoped to do anything with it. The ancient
Westralian in places far away from the boom
looks like a man who has been sent out alone into
the universe to clear away chaos with a shovel
and has-been floored by the vastness of the con-
tract.
“ The Patents, Trade Marks and Copyright
Laws form one of the most complicated branches
of legal science.” This is true, and it is practi-
cally impossible for any one unacquainted with
their technicalities to draw specifications and
claims, &c., that will stand the test of a law-suit.
Hitherto expert Patent agents have made charges
simply prohibitive to most inventors, but Mr.
Arthur Griffith, M.L.A., of 39 Castlereagh-
street (next Hotel Australia), Sydney, and Bolton-
street, Newcastle, having established reciprocal
arrangement with leading Patent agents in all the
principal countries in the world is now prepared
to do the work accurately and effectively at rates
that will place a patent within reach of the poorest
inventor. For the sum of £2O (exclusive of cost of
drawings) he will obtain a certificate of Provisional
Protection (in the first instance) for any invention
in the seven Australasian colonies and Great
Britain, and full Patents all over the world at
similarly reasonable rates. Mr. Griffith also
makes a specialty of designing and registering
Trade Marks and Copyrights.*
During a Clarence River flood, a year or
two back, a bull swam some distance and alighted
on the verandah of a house, where he stayed until
the water receded. When dry enough he pro-
menaded the verandah with a view of ascertaining
his whereabouts, and was astonished to find, on
glancing through a glass door as he was passing,
another bull. He challenged and charged, but
when he had smashed the door his antagonist had
disappeared. A hasty glance round the room (in
which, by the way, hung a splendid mirror) re-
vealed the late assailed, and again the attack was
made. This time bull No. 1 completely van-
quished bull No. 2, and left the scene triumphant
with the mirror-frame round his neck and much
lace curtains and antimacassars on his horns.

AN INTERESTING NOTICE OF A MOST
SUCCESSFUL CAREER.
MARVELLOUS TESTIMONIALS,
j FLATTERING- PRESS OPINIONS.
- - - MIRACULO ,'S CURES.
A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfaline Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
wduld be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian. Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W. A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “ Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

The Eye-sight measure sent per post by
the inventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough,
the well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney,
is a wonderful little contrivance which will enable
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection
such as long sight, short sight, old sight. Full
particulars from Messrs. Greenfield and Barra-
clough. #
The imports from Australia to New
Guinea amount to ,£28,360 per annum, and the
Papuan exports total up to ,£16,200. Also, last
year New Guinea sent -£l5O to England as a con-
tribution to the London Missionary Society. . . ,
We shall yet see a Papuan apostle clad in orthodox
black preaching the Word to the heathen of Bel-
gravia and the savages of Seven Dials.
Mounted shearer, about ten miles out from
Nyngan, meets a blackfellow walking. “How
many miles is it to Nyngan, Jackey ? ” Jackey :
“Nomile at all. Good road all the way.”
London Daily Chronicle undertook to
pay Dr. Nansen, should he reach within 10 miles
of the Pole, -£lOOO for a telegram announcing the
fact, and .£4OOO for a 15,000-word article enlarging
on it. He failed to get within the radius, so the
Chronicle had (as per arrangement) only to pay
,£5OO for the telegram and £2OOO for the article.
Edward Dyson’s book of verses is now
published. It includes all his best Bulletin
work, and is handsomely bound in cloth. The
title is “ Rhymes from the Mines and Other
Lines,” and a big sale is confidently anticipated.
More than any other, Dyson is the miners’ poet,
and his verse swings with the pick, has the clash
of hammer on drill. Post-free from Bulletin
office, ss. sd.

A MIRACLE IN SYDNEY.
LOCOMOTOR ATAXTA CURED.
BOILER-MAKER JARVIS WAS TOLD
BY DOCTORS HE MUST DIE.
ON THE VERGE OF THE GRAVE.
A FINAL EXPERIMENT AND ITS
ASTOUNDING RESULTS.
THE DOCTORS BEWILDERED.
INTERESTING INTERVIEWS.
FULL PARTICULARS OF A REMARK-
ABLE RECOVERY.
A SENSATION AT ULTIMO.
One of the best-known residents of Ultimo,
Sydney, is Mr. Thomas Jarvis, who resides at 45
Crown-lane. A multitude of his acquaintances
were grieved to notice how his fine physique was
slowly but surely being undermined by an in-
sidious and mysterious malady. The disease
baffled all who attempted to diagnose it, mean-
while Jarvis suffered agonies mentally and physi-
cally. His trouble developed at last so rapidly
that he was
FORCED TO HIS BED,
and his torture becoming unendurable, large
quantities of opium and morphia were adminis-
tered to alleviate his sufferings. He applied to a
well-known doctor, but that gentleman, after a
careful scrutiny pronounced his ease hopelessly in-
curable, and gave him an order addressed to the
Director of Charitable Institutions. Poor Jarvis
went home to bed, and another doctor was called
in and agreed that
JARVIS WAS INCURABLE,
and all that could be done for him was in the
direction of deadening the pains which racked
him. While Jarvis was patiently waiting for the
kindly hand of death to end his sufferings, his
attention was directed to an advertisement in the
press, and, thinking that a case therein stated
resembled his own, he determined, as a last resort,
to try Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.
The first box had a magical effect upon him the
very first week. His appetite returned, and he
once more experienced the
LUXURY OF A SLEEP.
The second week, with the second box of pills,
confirmed the symptoms of recovery, and he per-
severed with the third and fourth boxes with
equally surprising results. He is now taking his
fifth box, and in five short weeks
. AN ABSOLUTE CURE
has been revealed. Every day his strength in-
creases, and, although not yet robust, he is a new
man, full of vitality, confidence, and enthusiasm.
It is no wonder, then, that his case is causing
quite a sensation at Ultimo, and the news is
spreading throughout the city and suburbs.
Under these extraordinary circumstances it was
decided to send a representative of the Austra-
lian Workman to make inquiries so that the
cure may be fully authenticated and the public
may obtain all particulars. So remarkable a cure
deserves the widest publicity in the interests of
SUFFERING HUMANITY
whose maladies baffle all the science, learning, and
experience of the medical profession.
THOMAS JARVIS INTERVIEWED.
On Thursday afternoon, our representative made
his way to the humble home of Mr. Thomas
Jarvis, 45 Crown-lane, Ultimo. He was discovered
nursing a two-year-old baby, and his face declared
him to be one of the happiest men in Sydney. He
was up and about, and able to answer the knock
at the door. Although pale, and bearing still the
marks of a critical illness, and a period of pain, it
was very clear that he was an invalid on the rapid
road to convalescence.
In reply to the preliminary questions, he said :
“ You want to know all about my case ? Well, I
am glad of an opportunity to tell you. The facts
may be of value to those who are stricken as I
was.”
“ How long were you suffering ? ”
“ Well, twelve months ago, I got so bad that I
had to
LEAVE OFF WORK,
and I have done nothing since then. About five
years ago, next Christmas, I met with an acci-
dent ; I was badly scalded while taking a bath in
Pitt-street, Sydney. Since the accident, I have
been in failing health, and, after four years of
gradual development of the malady, I was forced
to give up my billet at the Glebe Island.”
“ What were the symptoms ? ”
“ Simply indescribable. The pains were some-
thing terrible. They were just about as much as
I could bear. They were for all the world like as
if a
SAW WERE CUTTING MY BONES
in two in every part of me. I was incessantly shiv
ing and shaking, and could neither eat nor slei
On the 23rd March last I saw a well-kno
doctor, and he told me that my case was utte
hopeless. However, he gave me an order to t
Director of Charitable Institutions.”
“ Did you make use of the order ? ”
“No ; I came home and went to bed, and ha
been pretty well in bed ever since. Five wet
ago I could not walk across the floor ; in fact
could not get out of bed. I felt then that it wt
ALL OVER WITH ME,
and only a question of days when I would give
the ghost. My doctor was attending me, and c
all he could, by opium and morphia pills, to et
the agony I was in. I was under him for five
815 L w ® e^s ’ came t° the conclusion that I w
suffering from Locomotor ataxia, or Progressi
locomotor ataxy, a disease of the spinal coi
characterised by peculiar disturbances of gait a
difficulty m co-ordinating voluntary movemen
He told me that I might last for ten years, a
that I might never get the use of my legs. (Ji
then the patient kicked his legs about with ener
to show that medical opinion is not infallibl
Well, just as a drowning man will grasp at
straw I grasped at an idea conveyed by t
advertisement describing the cure effected in t
case of another boiler-maker in England, whicl
read in the Australian Workman, by Dr W
hams Pink Pills for Pale People. I read in t
advertisement of a case which seemed to fit r
own. I determined to give the Pink Pills a tri
anyway, and 11
SENT FOR A BOX.
The effect was as marvellous as it was magic
a £ le and B l eep as fchou ffb w
the matter with me. It was the first appetite a
rest I had for many a day. It was
immediately left the bed and was abTe^mo
about. I have taken four boxes of Pink Pills
during the past four weeks, and am now taking
the fifth box.
I CAN WALK ABOUT
with the aid of a stick, and am gradually, but
surely, recovering my former vigor and vitality.
You see those crutches there ? They are perfectly
new, and I have never used them. They, were
made for me before I got the Pink Pills, but I was
too weak to use them, and now I don’t want them.
All I want is this stick, and I hope soon to do
without it. You see I am rapidly regaining my
strength.” ~
“ He is 100 per cent, better,” said Mrs. Jarvis,
who had come in and taken the baby on her knees.
“The Pink Pills have
SAVED HIS LIFE,
and made another man of him. ”
A friend who called in at this point, said that
Jarvis looked 10 years younger that day than he
did a fortnight previously.
“I can now,” continued Jarvis, “have a cold
bath, and all the feeling has come back in my legs.
If I keep on going the way I am, I hope to be able
to work in two months’ time. I feel that I shall
then be well enough to resume work as a boiler-
maker, and you know I have to be in the very
nink of health and strength to work at that trade.
I am i
BETTER TO-DAY
than I have been at any time during the past five
years. I ascribe all the change and benefit to these
Pink Pills. They have saved my life. I am in
no pain now, whatever, and feel like a new man.
On the mantelpiece there is a box of
OPIUM AND MORPHIA
pills which the doctor gave me to deaden the ex-
cruciating pain. But they can stay where they
are, for I no longer need them. Everybody who
knows me has been amazed at the change in me.
They can hardly believe their eyes. My being
about again has caused quite a sensation out here. ”
Having wished Mr. Jarvis continued speed
towards recovery, our representative withdrew.
WHAT MR. DONOHUE SAYS.
In order to obtain further confirmation of the
cure wrought by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People, our representative interviewed Mr. James
Donohue, a step-brother of the patient. Mr.
Donohue was discovered bard at work in the boiler
of a locomotive at theEveleigh Railway workshops.
Eew men are better known in trade-union circles
than “Jim” Donohue. He is a member of the
Iron Trades’ Council and is the representative of
the Boilermakers’ Society. He is the secretary of
the representative committee of the Eveleigh
Railway workshops. He has always taken the
deepest interest in trades matters, and was secre-
tary for several years of the Boilermakers’ Society
of N.S.W. He has held responsible positions in
Victoria, having been the representative of the
Ballarat Boilermakers’ Society at the Trades Union
Congress held in Melbourne in April, 1884. He
was also representative of the Melbourne Boiler-
makers’ Society on several occasions in matters
between the Society and employers. He occupied
most intelligently similar positions in Newcastle
and Sydney. Twenty years ago he was a promi-
nent member of the Boilermakers’ Society of
California, and was Chairman of that Society at
San Francisco. The testimony of such a well-
known and representative trade unionist will speak
for itself.
“Yes,” he said, “I am a step-brother of
Thomas Jarvis, who is now 35. He is a boiler-
maker, and was employed at various workshops,
and was for some time with the Colonial Sugar
Co. at Fiji. He met with an accident five years
ago at the swimming-baths in Pitt-street, Sydney.
He was severely scalded about the legs through
falling over an exposed steam-pipe. He was bad
sor months, but seemed to get all right, but this
complaint, which is a conundrum —I fancy it is a
sorm of paralysis—developed. After the accident
he worked for a considerable time at Glebe Island
abattoirs, and gradually grew worse, ultimately
losing the use of his legs. That occurred two
months ago. The doctors who attended him con-
sidered the
CASE HOPELESS,
and reckoned all they could do for him would be
to relieve the torture he was enduring. He was
thus treated with no idea of effecting any cure.
More as an experiment than otherwise Dr.
Williams’Pink Pills for Pale People were tried.
He took a box a week and the effect has been mar-
vellous. He became better rapidly, and is now
using the fifth box.
I NEVER SAW SUCH A CHANGE
in a man. I didn’t believe in patent medicines as
a rule, but have so altered my opinion that I an
recommending Pink Pills to all sufferers of my
acquaintance. I may mention that my daughter,
Mrs. Thomas O’Toole, of Darling-street, Balmain,
who has been suffering from weakness, is
using these Pills and deriving great benefit. She
was induced to take them owing to their wonder-
ful efficacy in Tom s case. Tom Jarvis expects to
be able to
GO TO WORK
in about two months’ time. He does not want
have much the matter with him physically
follow the occupation of a boilermaker. Ve
naturally, Tom’s cure has caused quite a sensati
among the boilermakers, and they can hare
cremt the news. I recommended the Pink Pi
to Mrs. Cunningham, of 135 Henderson-roa
Alexandria. She began taking them two da
ago. She has heen suffering for 20 years fre
chronic nervousness and affections of the brai
Since womanhood, she has never been out of t
hands of the doctors. Should Pink Pills do
much good for her as they did for Tom Jarv
then there will be another sensation at Alexa
ana.

tioned.
DRAPERY GOODS
to the value of £2 and upwards that can go by Parcels
Post, with the exception of certain heavy or bulky low-
priced lines, are sent by us to any Parcels Post Office in
the Colony POSTAGE FREE. Goods are thus delivered
to all parts of the Colony at Brisbane Closing-out Prices—
a boon which country residents will fully appreciate.
During CLOSING-OUT SALE it is often useless to order
from Samples; when once lines are sold they cannot be
replaced. In sending samples, therefore, first, second,
and third choices should be made in each instance ; and,
in the event of all being sold, authority should be given
to us to substitute the nearest. Our best judgment is
always used in substituting, and goods the nearest, both
as regards price and pattern sent.

“Papa’s Hair.”
A gentleman has sent us the following : —“ A few
weeks ago I bought a bottle of your ‘ Papa’s Hair,’
and it has acted with the most satisfactory results.
Previous to doing so, my hair had a mousey color
streaked with grey. The dear children would try
to count papa’s grey hairs, hut, because of the
number, could not succeed. Being only 44 years
of age, I determined to improve my hair, and tried
your ‘ Papa’s Hair,’ and am pleased to write that,
though at first there seemed little difference, gradu-
ally the hair regained its original color (there was
no dyeing whatever) and it is now strong and glossy.
I asked my little daughter this morning to count
my grey hairs, but when looking for them she cried
out —‘ Why, papa, there are no white hairs; mamma
must have pulled them all out.’ ”
frW* This Preparation is not a Dye, but acts gradu-
ally on the Roots of the Hair, and restores Grey
Hair to its natural color and lustre.
, No preparation will make hair grow better than
“Papa’s Hair."
It effectually prevents the formation of Dandriff,
as it is called, the presence of which always denotes
diseased action in the cuticle of the scalp, causing
premature baldness, or a falling off of the hair.
DIRECTIONS.—WeII shake the bottle, and with
a small piece of sponge rub into the roots of the
hair, night and morning, till the hair is restored to
its natural color ; afterwards apply twice a week.
3s. per Bottle, by post Bd. extra.

Dr. Service, of Sydney, proposes a new
method of food-preservation— flooding the contain-
ing receptacle with oxygen at high pressure, in
ie presence of a certain amount of moisture, and
len hermetically sealing it by turning a tap. The
theory is that in such a highly-oxygenated atmo
sphere no germ can live. Dr. Service has utilised
the same idea with much success in the treatment
or wounds.

Bulletin correspondent:—You ask
what would happen to a Maori if he proceshed
in Auckland to commemorate the six or eight
occasions on which he made the J. Bull crowd run
for their lives ? ” Well, they don’t exactly procesh,
but they do annually hold an awful blow-out to
commemorate the Treaty of Waitangi, by which
they made Bull recognise them as a sovereign
people fighting for just rights, and recognise their
ownership of the land, and their right to fish in
all rivers and waters for all time. And this is
what happened at Waipatu, where I saw it. The
Britishers rolled up in great force, and only
laughed when the Maoris remarked—“ Py colly,
some time another day long ago we give you ’ell; ”
and then proceeded, to feast (on the cheap) and to
make the Maori drunk (on his own liquor) and
hug his pretty wahines (on the cheap), and then
get drunk on the liquor left by the Maoris of both
sexes. That’s all—no gore.—J.

In the opinion of a Bulletin corres-
pondent, Koo-wee-rup (Yic.) contains more con-
centrated essence of - hopelessness than any other
alleged village settlement in Australasia—not
even excluding the experiment in misery at
Southport, Tasmania. It appears he once
endeavored to settle at Koo-wee-rup, and
kept on endeavoring for three months. Dur-
ing that period all his clothes and most of
his cuticle were distributed impartially on the
jagged points of the all-pervading ti-tree stumps ;
his horse, weakened by excessive chewing of ti-
tree bark, got into a crab-hole and wouldn’t Koo-
wee-rup any more; his wife fell into the “big
drain” twice while trying to catch eels ; his child-
ren were chased by big black snakes, and got lost
and sunstruok on the way to the half-time school
four miles up the swamp ; a deer or other wild
animal from the surrounding scrub-forest came in
the early morning and demolished the few
decrepit cabbages and frost-bitten potatoes in the
‘ ‘ garden ” ; the native-cats or ’possums or some-
thing killed the fowls ; a neighbor, or a neighbor’s
pig, came in the night and carried off the only re-
maining “ fifty ” of flour and half a sack of dried
onions; and a vagrant kangaroo-dog stole the
baby out of the gin-case cradle and only dropped it
after a two-mile chase through the ti-tree. Then
the inspector decided that insufficient work had
been done on the holding to entitle the
settler to half-time employment on the Govt, drain.
More ti-tree stumps should be grubbed, he said.
The poor devil of a villager had built fences with
ti-tree stumps, walled and floored his dwelling with
ti-tree stumps, poured them down crab-holes in
tons, had grubbed them out and piled them up in
huge bonfires that scorched his hair and whiskers,
and burnt down his fowlhouse ; heaps of ashes,
6ft. deep, surrounded the premises, and lay like
sand-dunes up against the impenetrable wall of
standing ti-tree at the back ; and all the time the
house and garden kept sinking slowly down until
they got below the level of the canal, and the water
soaked through the floor and put the fire out.
Yet, despite all this, the inspector wasn’t satisfied,
for the ti-tree stumps increased and multiplied.
At every fresh subsidence of the homestead they
sprang up like mushrooms. It was no use, so the
settler shook the swampy “sugee” from his bare
feet, and gave the ti-tree stumps best. Charles
Dudley Warner and other amateur gardeners
anathematise the American weed called “ pusley.”
They should try to eradicate ti-tree stumps, espe-
cially at Koo-wee-rup.
