They Laid Their Snares.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sisters were twins; their absolute
devotion to each other was based upon the fact
that opposites agree. They were Maoriland born
and bred never having sailed beyond its coasts
and they bore soft-sounding native names.
Runa, the eldest, carried romantic sentiment to
a fine point. Her longing was to roam among the
cities of the ancient fables : every taste and inclina-
tion pointed to the civilised and antique.
Airini the younger was agricultural, a daughter
of the soil ; whole-souled in her ambition to own a
model farm and raise “ show-beasts. ” She could
shoot a dozen rabbits out of thirteen, and ride anv-
thing with four legs and every dog loved her
whistle and worked his level best for her with
cattle, sheep, or rats.
Last Christmas there came to Arong-Aromr
station two young men from England with letters
of introduction to the owner, Robert Feilden
1 hey were cousins, one heir to a vast inheritance'
the other proud possessor of a healthy frame and
liver and a capacity for unlimited hard work
The former promptly fell in love with Runa,
the romantic; the latter with Airini, the practical •
and February found them on the verge of a de-
claration, and both confiding in the twins’
brother a lad of sixteen, universally described as
“a limb.”
* * * .
It was an autumn morning such as Maori-
land alone can claim ; an invigorating twinge of
frost in the air, and brilliant sunshine tinting the
bedewed spider-webs which interlaced the wire-
fences. Across the flax swamp and manuka
downs a hundred miles appeared but as a day’s
lounge, to where the purple gullies and golden
spurs of the Hunters’ Hills raised their high-
drawn snow-line against the intense blue of the
sky.
The millionaire, Donald Craigie, paced the
brown creek gravel in front of the homestead, his
arm linked through that of young “Bob”
Feilden. After a while he strolled to the stables,
and the limb ” entered the schoolroom where his
younger sister strummed the jangly old piano in
vague longing for the sympathy music sheds.
“ Look here, Ai —it’s a plant, and you’ve got to
help me,” Bob began.
“Not a bit. I know you—you’re not going to
get me into any more rows ! ”
“ I thought you rather cared for Runa’s in-
terests ?”
Airini faced round.
“What’s about Runa?”
“ Only a chance—a real certainty that she’s
going to see all the pictures—and things at last! ”
Airini gasped.
“ How— ever ? ”
“As if you didn’t know —if she’d only give him
the chance.”
“Well?”
“I’ve arranged it all, but you’ve got to help.”
“I’m not going to side against Ru.”
“No one wants you ; all you’ve got to do is to
stick to me. We’ll take them to the limestones,
then we’ll climb the rock-table, and I’ll help you
up—she’ll have to stop below —and trust him
to”
“It’s a shame.”
“ I only wish someone would plant me for 18
thou, a year. Why on earth she won’t give him a
chance ”
“ Idiot you are ! do you suppose a girl’s going
to say ‘ Ask me’? If a man wanted me he’d have
to rush in and say, ‘ You’ve got to listen,’ and
carry one’s heart by storm. Pouf ! —l’ve no
patience with the modern man who’s got to have
chances made for him.”
Bob pressed a little folded paper into her palm,
and went away;
She read on the crumpled missive : “They laid
their snares about me, they encompassed me as
with a net.”
She laughed a little regretfully as she thrust it
into her pocket.
rut^ —but all’s well that
the Hm , b ” sou g ht Buna and pre-
sented her with a similar note. She read it.
, .don t see the joke,” she grumbled in evi-
late lmtatlon - She was always up in arms of
ril e -W lain by-and-by.” And Bob went off
for a walk with Walter Carey, the impecunious
suitor, who talked with him long and earnestly.
* * « *
At luncheon Bob proposed a ride to the
lmestones, and Airini, without a smile, seconded
tne motion. Runa demurred—it was so hot—and
it would be sure to be cold before they got back.
■Donald Craigie did not look at her, but simply
said he would go—and—she came down in her
namt as the horses were brought to the door.
Walter Carey climbed the hack appointed to
him and turned its head down the drive. Bob
shouted after him.
(( Hulloa, Chicken ! not coming with us ? ”
lam afraid not; I’m expecting letters and
must go to the township. I’ll follow and pick
you up on the way home. ”
Airini s under lip tightened ; her cob bounded
to the sudden dig from the heel which went with
a tierce jab at the bit.
, w l ° an d tw p they rode, Craigie and Airini
ahead, Bob and Runa following.
At the rocks they linked reins to stirrup-irons
and left the horses to browse on the short, thick,
dark-green native grass, and then they wandered
iu .Pong the treacherous limestones, where,
hidden by lawyers and ti-tree, deep caverns with
but a funnel mouth yawned in the seemingly fair
depressions, where the piri-piri and native bracken
grew m the profusion only found in unburnt
corners.
Under a great overhanging weatherbeaten shelf
Donald Craigie halted.
« ”lovely and cool in here,” he said—-
wouldn’t you care for some of these ferns, Miss
Runa?”
"I’m j us k shivering ! ” and she hurried on.
They reached a narrow passage, where the sun
threw off from the white walls a baking radiance.
‘Now, here’s a snug corner ; just'look how this
beach convolvulus is blooming even how,” Donald
cried.
‘I hate it hot! ” and with the words Runa
made a rush to join her brother and sister. But
too late—the table rock was right ahead. Bob
scrambled up, and with his heart in the job
managed to drag Airini to its flat summit.
Runa remained below helpless, speechless,
furious; yet her heart went pit-a pat, not from
anger, but in response to the pleading voice at her
side as they wandered far from the table rock,
and forgot that the world was more than they
two.
Up above, Airini, flushed and beautiful, faced
her brother.
“ It’s mean, and —I wouldn’t be in Runa’s place
for millions.”
“ Not if it happened to be somebody else ? ”
Thwack came a hunting crop across Bob’s
shoulders.
“Pax!’ he grunted, “do you remember the
note I gave you this morning? ”
“Yes —we’re boxed, I know, but as it’s only
you and I goodness ! I believe there are pigs
as low down as this already ! ”
( A smile suited to his pseudonym broadened
“ the limb’s ” features as a scrambling sound rose
from the face of rock opposite to that they had
ascended.
Bob remarked :
“ I’m awfully dry—l’ll just slide down and get
a wet at the river.”
He disappeared, and another head rose above the
flat mossy platform.
It was Walter Carey, furiously red with exertion
and desperate resolve.
She dare not retreat, for fear of what she might
meet below. She could not go forward—she sat
in such a whirl of feeling that speech was im-
possible.
A moment’s silence, then the tide of his emo-
tions reached its limits and overflowed —he spoke
for both, as she at length shyly agreed.
Two hours later.
“Do you think we might venture down now?”
she said.
“They should have got it over—to judge by
. ourselves?”
She did not reply, but in response to her eyes
he whispered :
“ It is only just begun ! ”
Then they turned their eyes to the flat before
them ; in the far distance Craigie and Runa rode
homewards, and nearer' their two steeds stood
waiting. Bob’s horse was absent.
“Why !” Airini began.
“Yes! We needn’t have stopped here at all;
they were far out of hearing—but—you don’t
regret it, do you, darling? If I only had a little
• money like that Pirate Donald ”
“I’d never speak to you if you had ; I like a
man to work his way up—we’ve both got hands.”
His reply as to the quality of hers was lost in
the rattle of dislodged limestone-shale as they
safely reached a footing in the narrow cleft.
M.L.
MABEL HOLMES.
“ There was much fuss,” writes a Bul-
letin correspondent from Westralia, “about the
whistling spider discovered by the Horn expedi-
tion. What do you think of a barking lizard ?
The other day, whilst taking away some stones
from a mullock-heap to build a forge, I unearthed
three gaudily-striped lizards about 6in. long and
with beaver-like tails. On spotting the disturber
of their home, they all three began to bark, and
rushed at me for all the world like puppies of a
tender age. Their tongues, too, were similar to
that of a dog. No Munchausen about this. Fact.
—O.H.T. Black Flag, W.A.
Solicitor Snowden, M.L.C. and ex-Mayor
of Smellbourne, has put all the traducers of his
precious profession.to rout. “ Whenever I hear a
person abusing the law and the lawyers,” says this
knighted authority, “ I know that it is for the pur-
pose of covering some design of his own.” Maeau-
lay, Sydney Smith, Dickens, Thackeray, and
every other contemptible scribbler who has jibed
at legal craft and craftsmen were covering dark
personal designs under their dishonest satires.
Snowden knows it. A person who asserts that
Snowdon has personal reasons for libelling the
abusers of the. Law is a liar, and the rule that
nearly all men, except lawyers, are liars ought to
be added to the statutes.
A well-known Northern parson, who
habitually heaves great chunks of the Age of
Consent at a suffering public, will be best remem-
bered for one act of magnanimity and Christian
forgiveness which brightened up his hard sectarian
career. In the old days this reverend had a dear
friend, so dear that he fixed the friend’s hard-
sounding cognomen on to his baby son as a
middle name. The baby, however, was not a
success ; he was never a healthy infant, and his
young life ended suddenly under a big marble
monument, with all his three names spelled
out on it at full length, which the sorrowing
parent set over his little remains. A few years
later, when the sharp edge of grief had worn
off, a boom came, and the parson gave
way to the temptations of this world
and “ went for the beans.” No one
knows exactly how it came about, but a disastrous
transaction in which the parson and his friend were
both engaged ended the old comradeship. The
two scattered friends speak no more. It was just
then that the above-mentioned act of pious forgive-
ness came in. It took the shape of a hammer and
chisel wherewith the bereaved parent went to the
cemetery, and solemnly chipped out the middle
name off the infant’s tombstone. And now a long
blank on the stone tells the story of the brother-
hood of souls that will never be repaired. This
tale is a touching example of heaping coals of fire
on an enemy’s neck, or something. Anyhow, it
would make an interesting tract if properly worked
up.

Evidently the New Woman is not a familiar
object in Queensland. At all events, when the
Brisbane police recently heard fearsome shrieks
issuing from a house, and on investigation found a
widow dressed in her late husband's clothes and
with a false beard on, sharpening a razor and utter-
ing dismal cries in between times, they simply
arrested her as a lunatic. The widow had lather
all over her countenance, and said that she was
about to shave herself. If the New Woman isn’t
allowed to wear male attire and a beard and whoop
while she is shaving herself, what’s the good of
being a New Woman at all ?
Now that a Melb. court has allowed wit-
nesses who want to remain dark to give evidence
in court without stating their names, how far is
the system likely to go ? Supposing a Judge in
one of the criminal courts-follows suit, and allows
an unknown man to climb anonymously into the
witness-box, and swear that he saw the prisoner
commit the crime, and then go away as he came
and disappear in the crowd ! Or suppose the wit-
ness is allowed to conceal his evidence as well as
his identity—one thing is about as logical as the
other ; and he sidles into the court with his face
concealed in his hat like one who prays surrepti-
tiously in church, and whispers for ten minutes in
the Judge’s ear, and then the court lifts up its
voice and says that the case is fully proved, and
the sentence is six months ! Or suppose he
doesn’t come in at all, but merely sends
in his evidence anonymously on a post-
card ! And, again, suppose the prisoner is
anonymous as well as the witness, and is simply
tried as No. 18,732. Or why not have the whole
business conducted on the same basis, and let the
name of everybody be suppressed, and let the
evidence on both sides and the speeches for the
prosecution and defence be dropped through a hole
into the jury-box, and the verdict be dropped
through another hole to the Bench ; and if the
accused is found guilty let him be remanded to his
cell, and the sentence be written out and thrown
in through the window ? The law is supposed to
treat all parties alike—at least, The Bulletin has
a vague idea that it once heard something to that
effect.
Dear Bulletin, — There is a material Hell,
and the name of it is Broken Hill. Ordinary
summer temperature about 110° in the shade, with
the hot air jammed immovably against the earth
by the clouds of smoke ever hanging over the
town. Each adult consumes, on an average, 40
pints of liquor per diem. Often the dust is so
thick that lamps have to be lit at 3 o’clock in the
afternoon, heavy clouds literally darkening the
earth. Add lead fumes, bad water, flies, the
deadly monotony of the scenery, and the compre-
hensive ugliness and grime of all things, and they
make a beautiful total.---D.S.

Hypnotised .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
" Take my advice, and get someone to help you.
It’s not for his sake, so much ; he’s safe enough
for the present. It’s you I’m afraid for, Miss
Annie ; you’re too young for this sort of thing—
much too young and inexperienced.”
Two resolute dark eyes looked up from a thin-
lipped, white little face. “I have great influence
over him, doctor, and you know I’m not wanting
in nerve or presence of mind.”
“ Aye, that’s true enough, as I can vouch for.
Haven’t I known you from a child ! ”
“And I must stay with him, doctor, until he
gets better, or until — ”
A rosy flush softened her keen features. The
doctor smiled, and patted her hand reassuringly.
Nothing had pleased him more than his little
friend’s engagement to that fine young fellow,
Inglewood. Soon she would marry him, and so
get quit of that scientific brother of hers whose
precarious mental health rendered him so unsuit-
able a companion. As for him, he would either
degenerate into unmistakeable lunacy, or, possibly,
recover and do good work in the scientific world.
Dr. Brown did not much care which ; he had little
sympathy with fools whom friends and doctors
warned in vain.
He took his hat from the peg.
“ Oh ! I know you, Miss Annie. You wouldn’t
scream and get hysterical like the rest of your sex.
All the same, you’re a woman, and you’d show it
one way or another. Take my advice, and get a
friend to stay with you. Why, my girl, your
nerves might be gone already, and you’d not know
it, till occasion came to try them.”
“Well, I’ll think of it, doctor.” But she
smiled a little as she closed the door behind him.
She was one of the few women to whom physical
fear is almost unknown.
“Annie, I want you,” came an irritable voice
from the study. The pale-faced student with
features so like her own, but weaker, looked up
dreamily as she entered. “ Sit down a moment,
I’m not quite ready yet. ”
She took a seat beside him and sewed. He was
at his desk once more busily writing, words drop-
ping from his mouth occasionally, as every now
and then he rose to consult one or other of the
books strewn about on the shelves. Once Annie
glanced at what he had written ; she had received
some education, and she saw at once that it was
nonsense.
At last he spoke to his sister. “ I’m getting on
splendidly, Annie, splendidly.”
“I’m very glad, dear.” She had heard the
remark many times before.
“It’s an experiment, Annie —an experiment.
There’s only one thing needed, and I must cut
your throat for that. Now, please, don’t make
any fuss about it.”
He had opened a drawer and taken a clasp-
knife from its recess.
Woman’s brain remembered what woman’s
brain once invented. Annie folded up her sewing.
“ Certainly, Will, but ’’-—touching her pink
blouse —“ let me change this first. We are not
very rich, you know" and I could not afford to
have it spoilt.”
“ All right; but don’t keep me waiting.”
She rose, somewhat hurriedly. He looked at
her with suspicious eyes. She stopped, and re-
arranged the books on his desk; then walked
slowly from the room.
As she had feared, the hall-door was shut. To
open it would risk his instant pursuit of her. Her
best plan was to ascend to her room as quickly as
possible, and there make signals from the window.
If that failed, she must climb down by aid of the
creepers and the big pear-tree whose branches
shadowed her room. The distance to the ground
was not great. The only danger was that he might
hear her from below. It would be better if this
had not to be faced.
Thus she made her arrangements, in one rapid
moment of thought, standing at the foot of the
stairs. She was not consciously frightened ;in
fact, she was amazed at her own fearlessness. And
this after the doctor’s warning that very day !
Amidst grief for her brother, she could not help a
strange thrill of exultation that the danger had
come and found her ready to meet and overthrow
it.
She reached the bend in the staircase. Above
her was the skylight. It was the glass protecting
an exquisite picture, a dazzling bit of blue sky,
nothing more. She raised her eyes to it for a
moment. At the same instant, a sudden pain
darted through her head ; she felt confused, and
stumbled on the stairs. Another minute, and it
was over. She was her calm, collected self once
more.
At last, she was safe in her room, with its small
ornaments and cheap prettinesses. There was a
photograph of her friend on the undraped mantel-
piece. She smiled as she saw it, and hoped he
would be able to call that evening.
Suddenly she remembered what she had come
up for. To change her blouse, to be sure. She
did so, selecting one of print from the wardrobe.
The window was slightly open at the foot. She
pushed it up further, and looked out. A long
tendril of the creeper panted on the window-sill;
and through the pear-tree’s open branches she
could see the road, quite within call. Three men,
stalwart young fellows, glanced up as they passed
along. She watched them out of sight.
“ Coming into the garden ?” cried a shrill voice
from below.
It was her neighbor’s daughter looking up with
flushed cheeks from the plot she was weeding,
“ Not just yet, Minnie; my brother wants me
very particularly to help in an experiment.”
Yet she lingered, half wishing that Minnie
would ask her what the experiment was. She had
a vague impression that it would be better if
someone knew that Will wanted to cut her throat.
She walked downstairs rather reluctantly, till
roused by her brother's voice :
“ Ready, Annie?”
“ Yes, Will.”
He closed the study-door again. She was now
at the foot of the stairs.
Suddenly there was a loud knocking at the back-
door, and, rather glad of a respite, she hastened
to see what was the matter. It was the Chinaman
with vegetables.
“Not to-day, John. I can’t wait.” John
grinned and prepared to resume his burden.
“You see, my brother wants me for an experi-
ment. ”
She looked at him wistfully. How she hoped
he would ask her about the experiment. The
more she thought of it, the more convinced she
felt that there was something disagreeable, nay,
even wrong, about what Will wanted to do.
There were tears in her eyes as she touched the
study door. She had almost turned the handle
when there was another interruption—at the front
door this time.
She hastened to open it, and the sturdy figure
of her young was before her.
He was an unobservant fellow, and greeted her
with a smile. .
“ Got out of office early ; luck, isn t it. lut on
your hat, Annie, and come and have a stroll
before tea. ”
“Oh ! I can’t, Mark. Will wants me very par-
ticularly to help in an experiment.”
“Oh ! all right. I’ll wait in the parlor till you re
ready. ”
He sauntered towards the other room.
“ It’s for an experiment, Mark.”
She stood with her hand on the study door.
“Oh ! very well; I’ll wait here for you.
He spoke from the depths of an arm-chair, and
from behind a newspaper.
She heaved a little sigh, and entered.
He was in time to save her, but barely.
Maoriland.
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
Caloonah.
A TOWNSHIP IN NORTH AUSTRALIA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ten white men at Caloonah; heat through the
long summer ranging from 95' to 112' ; offices and
buildings built of iron, raising blisters on unwary
fingers that touched it by daytime. Wind that
carried before it endless clouds of red dust.
Caloonah, 100 miles from civilisation, and owning
a store, a post-office, a public-house, and Govern-
ment offices. Outside it, endless plains to south
and west, a bordering of mulga forest to north
and east, and the quivering hot horizon. Inside
it ten white men doing their work listlessly,
taking their rest listlessly, and developing, as the
slow months dragged by, the vacant, dull expres-
sion which speaks of dead hopes, lost ambitions,
and inertness of mind and body.
A big kerosene lamp flickered overhead in the
long room of the Queen’s Arms. Hundreds of
insects lay dying or dead on its shade or bowl.
The hot air was heavy with the fumes of tobacco
and liquor. Jones lay on his back on the floor,
his eyes closed. Johnson, Farnham, and Clowes
played euchre. Meyer sang a few bars of “I
fear no foe.” His voice was thick and hoarse, and
blended with the shrill of the mosquitoes, the
heavy boom of sleepy blow-flies, and the rush of
the wind that tore and banged at a loose sheet of
iron on the roof.
“ that row ! ” Jones was querulous, irrit-
able ; with the greatest effort he lay still, his
hands tightly clenched under his head.
“ But I love an’ fear the glaaaamer thro’ thy
drooooping laaashes seen,” mumbled Meyer.
Redwood leant against the wall, staring down
the low room with its long deal table, wooden
benches, earth floor, and ten dejected, perspiring,
hopeless occupants. “ Can you fellows believe
you ever drank iced champagne, wore decent
clothes, and a rose in your buttonhole, and kissed
a pretty girl? ”
The iron banged louder than ever, and Jones
set his teeth, and pressed his head harder on his
clenched hands.
“ Now, hell ’d be choicer than Caloonah. So
many new arrivals, such gossip, scandal, yarns,
and girls, grand girls.”
Meyer nodded. “Through thy drooooping
laaashes seen,” he repeated, in hoarse bass.
There was a queer, tense feeling in the air, the
overpowering heat, the perpetual wind, the end-
less weariness of life. The iron grew looser, and
banged with steady persistence. Jones rose slowly
to his feet, and staggered slightly as he took a
pea-rifle from a nail. A big blow-fly fluttered
into the lamp, sizzled for a moment, and then
went pop. Jones aimed with shaking hand at a
scrap of paper on the wall, and missed, and then
at a small cigarette-photograph on an upright, and
missed again. , , .. _ ,
“ Stop that ! ” cried Farnham, sharply. Isn t
it hot enough, without adding that noise
and smell to our miseries ? ” ,
“ Besides, you can’t hit anything,” grunted
Neville. “ Why, you’d miss Redwood there.”
Jones laughed, and his eyes were like a drunken
man’s, though he had had but one glass of beer the
whole evening. He swung round on his heel.
“ Oh, I can hit Redwood,” he said, thickly. Bang
went the little gun again, and Redwood fell for-
ward. The aim had been sure enough, this time.
“ I knew I could hit him,” he said.
He got three years, with hard labor, and the
sentence was considered light. And the Govern-
ment, possessing neither heart nor soul, nor
common sense, provided Caloonah with other men
in place of Jones and Redwood, and considered
itself aggrieved in what had occurred. With one
of the new men has gone his wife. Briggs and
Burnham are better-looking than the benedict.
Neville is unscrupulous ; but that story is not)
finished yet, and after all, do even the big sins
count at Caloonah ?
M.D.
Courteous communication to The Bulletin
from Adelaide: —
THE ORIGIN OF THE SCOTCHMAN.
Old Nicholas working at the mould,
Turning out Chinamen, Japs and Jews,
Made a botch;
So, adding a stronger smell for gold,
And an extra pocket for same in its trews,
He called it Scotch.
Wake.
In a Melb. hospital they have been care-
fully nursing up the survivor of the recent
tragedy with a view to hanging him, if the law
can so arrange it, as soon as he is well enough to
stand being killed. The said tragedy is one of
many dozen cases in which the double-barrelled
suicide failed to come off. It seldom does come
off, in fact. The average man has so little know-
ledge of anatomy that if he does manage to kill
himself it is generally either by accident or by a
laborious process of sawing himself in many
pieces. Therefore when a man and woman agree
to die together it usually happens that one sur-
vives, or else both—unless in such cases as poor
Dacre’s, whose medioal training enabled him to
do the tragedy quickly and successfully. The
only reasonably sure way to arrange a double
suicide is for the parties to go out in a boat till
they are in deep water, and then tie a big chain
securely round them both, and tie the anchor to
the chain, and go overboard with all this pomp
and ironmongery to sink them and keep them
down. All other kinds of double suicide are un-
reliable.

Mr. Herbert has been an officer in the U. S. naval service for the past 16 years.
His duties have taken him all over the world, and he has been on a constant go for
several years, tnrough the Dardanelles, around the famous rock of Gibraltar, in the
Mediterranean, through the Suez Canal, about the South Sea Islands, in Hawaii,
round Cape Horn, to South America, Africa—everywhere, almost, under the sun he
has cruised as a navy officer. On his return from one of his voyages the strain told
on him. His blood was in a very bad condition, and his stomach was weakened
and, as he says, he was “ knocked out.” Mr. Herbert says:
“Although I look very hearty now, four years ago my health and physical con-
dition were anything but good. Then it was that I found the constant knocking
about had at last told on me. My blood was very poor and my stomach as bad I
had been cruising about in the service for a long time without anv rest and without
ceasing from my duties. Frequent changes in water and climate,*'such as I exueri-
enced, will weaken and debilitate tlie strongest of us, and I was no exception In
fact, I was completely run down, and when I came home my family insisted on ’call
mg in the doctor. He prescribed for me and did all he could, but nothing came of
it, and I grew worse. As my blood seemed chiefly the cause of the trouble I thought
I would give Ayer’s Sarsaparilla a trial, seeing it advertised to cure such cases I
took a bottle in an aimless sort of a fashion, but when I found it helped mv stomach
and was commencing to clear my blood, too, I thought there might be some real
merit in it, and accordingly began on a second bottle. Well, to make a long storv
short, the third completely cured me and restored me to my old-time health Mv
blood was better than ever, and my stomach did not bother me in the least A
Sarsaparilla cured me Now I take it whenever I feel that my system needs £and
Sarsanarma 8 Tt r h nyS T equippe<l for » cruis * anywhere without a bottle of Ayer’s
Sarsaparilla. It has, I assure you, my hearty indorsement and recommendation.”

The Editor of the “CHRISTIAN MILLION”
under the heading of General Notes, on August 20th, 1896, wrote :
“ good article will stand on its own merits, and we may rely
upon it that nothing will continue long which does not, in
a more or less degree, harmonise with the statements
which are published concerning it.”
MR. HALL CAINE.
Author of “The Deemster,” “The Manxman,” &c.,
when speaking on “Criticism,” recently said :—
“ "W hen a thing that is advertised greatly is good, it goes and
goes permanently ; when it is bad, it only goes for a while :
the public finds it out.”
The Proprietor of BEECEAM’S PILLS -
has said over and over again :
It is a fallacy to imagine that anything will sell just because
it is advertised. How many nostrums have been started
with glare and snuffed out in gloom ? The fact is, a man
is not easily gulled a second time; and every dissatisfied
purchaser does ten times more harm than one satisfied
does good. Assuredly the sale of more than 6,000,000
boxes of BEECHAM’S PILLS per annum, after a public
trial of half-a-century, is conclusive testimony of their
popularity, superiority, and proverbial worth.”
